فما دون في حق من هو عدل (وما) روي عن ابن عباس) وإن رواه الطحاوي فلم يصح) وفيه انقطاع باطن كيف وهو) أي أمير المؤمنين عمر (كان) يقدمه على الأكابر ويسأله ويستحسن قوله) فكيف يكون له هيبة منه في عرض رأيه؟ روى البخاري عن ابن عباس قال : كان عمر رضي الله عنه يدخلني مع أشياخ بدر وكأن بعضهم وجد في نفسه فقال : لم تدخل هذا معنا ولنا أبناء مثله ؟ فقال عمر: إنه من قد علمتم فدعاه ذات فأدخله معهم، فما رأيت أنه دعاني يومئذ إلا ليريهم قال : ما تقولون في قوله الله ﴿إِذَا جَاءَ نَصْرُ اللهِ وَالْفَتْحُ﴾ [النصر: 1] فقال بعضهم : أمرنا أن نحمد الله ونستغفره إذا جاء نصرنا وفتح علينا وسكت بعضهم فلم يقل شيئاً فقال لي : أكذلك تقول يا ابن عباس؟ فقلت : لا ، قال : فما تقول؟ قلت: هو أجل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه وسلم أعلمه الله فقال : ﴿إِذَا جَاءَ نَصْرُ اللَّهِ وَالْفَتْحُ﴾ وذلك علامة أجلك، فسبح بحمد ربك واستغفره فإنه كان توابا فقال عمر : ما أعلم منها إلا ما تقول (وكان) أمير المؤمنين ألين للحق وأشد انقياداً له :(قال لا خير فيكم إن لم تقولوا ولا خير في إن لم أسمع) ذكره في «التقويم»، كذا في التيسير» وإذا كان قوله هذا فكيف يهابه ابن عباس في عرض رأيه وقصته مع المرأة في نهيه عن مغالاة المهر شهيرة) في «التيسير»، روى أبو يعلى وغيره عن مسروق قال : ركب عمر بن الخطاب على منبر رسول الله صلى الله عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه وسلم ثم قال : أيها الناس ما إكثاركم في مهور النساء، وقد كانت الصدقات فيما بين رسول الله صلى الله عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه وسلم وبين أصحابه أربعمائة درهم ذلك، فما دون ذلك، ولو كان الإكثار في ذلك تقوى عند الله أو مكرمة لم تسبقوهم إليها، ثم نزل، فاعترضته امرأة من قريش فقالت له يا أمير المؤمنين نهيت الناس أن يزيدوا في صداقهن على أربعمائة درهم؟ قال نعم قالت أما سمعت الله يقول ﴿وَاتَيْتُمْ إِحْدَتْهُنَّ قِنطَارًا فَلَا تَأْخُذُوا مِنْهُ شَيْئًا﴾ [النساء: 20] فقال عمر : اللهم كل أحد أفقه من عمر . ثم رجع فركب المنبر ثم قال : أيها الناس إني كنت نهيتكم أن تزيدوا النساء في صداقهن على أربعمائة درهم، فمن شاء أن يعطي ما أحب. فإذا كان له مع هذه المرأة ما علمت فأي مهابة كانت لابن عباس في عرض رأيه، ثم إن أمير المؤمنين عمر استشار الصحابة، فأشار العباس بالعول، ثم اتفق الصحابة، ولم يكن هناك لأمير المؤمنين رأى قبل تقرره عند الصحابة فأي مهابة من أمير كانت لابن عباس، ثم إن الدليل الذي ينقلون عنه في إبطال العول غير معقول، فإن قائلي العول لا يقولون بنصفين وثلث حتى يرد عليهم ما أورد بل هم أيضاً يقولون إن الله لم يجعل السهام كذلك، فينقص سهم كل حتى لا يلزم نصفان وثلث فالذي رد به هو بعينه حجة لهم. وهذا النحو من الرد بعيد عن ابن عباس كل البعد ثم الذي نسبوه إليه في مثل هذه الصورة أن يسقط سهام البنات والأخوات لأنهن قد يكن عصبة ويخرجن عن السهام المقدرة، فهن ضعيفات في استحقاق السهم فيسقط سهمهنّ، وهذا أيضاً لا يظهر له وجه، فإن سهامهن أيضاً ثبتت بالنص ولو في حال كسهام غيرهن فإسقاط واحد لإتمام آخرين مما لا وجه له شرعاً وعقلاً، فالحق أن ابن عباس بريء عن مثل هذا القول فافهم والله أعلم بأحوال خواص المؤمنين

الأصل الثالث الإجماع وقد يقال كما في التحرير) لا نسلم أن التعظيم بالسكوت عن الحق فسق بل (الفسق إنما هو السكوت عن منكر، وقول المجتهد ليس كذلك بل هو واجب العمل (أقول) في دفعه (الكلام) ههنا (قبل استقرار المذاهب، بل عند البحث والمناظرة فيها لتحقيق الحق (فالمقام مقام الاستفتاء، وعلى المفتي يجب إظهار (قوله وما هو الحق عنده فالسكوت عن إظهار الحق ترك الواجب وهو حرام، وكذا التعظيم (فافهم) وقد يقال : إظهار ما هو الحق عنده إنما يجب عند السؤال، ولا سؤال ههنا فلعله إنما سكت لأن الباحثين مجتهدون فيعملون برأيهم، وهذا لا حرمة فيه فتأمل فيه، فإن حالة البحث هل هي سؤال معنى فيجب الإظهار، وهو الظاهر (الظنيون) القائلون بظنية هذا الإجماع (سكوتهم ظاهر في موافقتهم القائلين وإن كان يحتمل أن يكون لأمر آخر لكنه بعيد غاية البعد لما علم من عادتهم لكونهم لا يخافون لومة لائم في إظهار الحق (ترك السكوت في مثله بالاستقراء) في أحوالهم الشريفة (كقول معاذ لعمر) أمير المؤمنين لما بجلد امرأة زنت (ما جعل الله على ما في بطنها سبيلا) والجلد يحتمل السراية إليه وقول عبيدة لعلي أمير المؤمنين رأيك في الجماعة أحب إلى غير ذلك من الوقائع، كقول ابن مسعود لأبي موسى الأشعري حين حكم بفرقة زوجة الأعرابي بشربه لبنها : إن مدة الرضاع سنتان بالنص، اعلم أن هذا الدليل لو تم لدل على كون الإجماع قطعياً فإن الساكتين إن كان سكوتهم رضا فقد تم الإجماع بموافقتهم، وإن كانوا كتموا الحق وسكتوا ففسقوا، فقد خرجوا عن أهلية الإجماع فتم الإجماع بالقائلين فقط، فتحقق الإجماع عند سكوت البعض وفتوى البعض قطعي فافهم (الجبائي) قال (قبل الانقراض (الاحتمالات) المذكورة (قائمة) فلا يكون حجة (وبعده تضمحل بالكلية فيكون إجماعاً قاطعاً وربما يمنع) الاضمحلال (بل يضعف) بعده فإن احتمال الخوف باق ولو من المقلدين قال ابن أبي هريرة العادة أن لا ينكر الحكم ولو كان مخالفاً لرأيه فلا يكون السكوت عند القضاء دليل الرضا (بخلاف الفتوى) فإن العادة فيها الإنكار إن كان مخالفاً وذلك لأن الحاكم يهاب ويوقر) فلا ينكر عليه (ويجاب بأن ذلك أي عدم إنكار الحكم بعد الاستقرار أي بعد استقرار المذاهب وتعين مذهب الحاكم (والكلام) ههنا قبله والفتيا والحكم حينئذ سواء في الإنكار عليه عند المخالفة، ألم تر كيف رد معاذاً (أقول : الحكم في المجتهد فيه لا ينقض فلا ينكر عليه لعدم الفائدة في الإنكار (فتدبر) وتأمل فيه .
So below is about one who is just. And what is narrated from Ibn Abbas, even if al-Tahawi narrated it, it is not authentic, and there is hidden discontinuity. How could it be? He, meaning the Commander of the Faithful Umar, used to give him precedence over the elders, ask him, and consider his opinion good. So how could he have awe of him in presenting his opinion? Al-Bukhari narrated from Ibn Abbas: Umar used to include me with the elders of Badr, and some of them felt resentful and said: Why do you include this boy with us when we have sons like him? Umar said: He is one you know. Then one day he invited me and included me with them. I saw that he only invited me that day to show them. He said: What do you say about Allah's word: 'When the victory of Allah and the conquest comes'? Some said: We are commanded to praise Allah and seek His forgiveness when His victory and conquest come to us. Some remained silent. He said to me: Do you say that, Ibn Abbas? I said: No. He said: What do you say? I said: It is the sign of the Messenger of Allah's death, Allah informed him of it. He said: 'When the victory of Allah and the conquest comes' is the sign of your death, so glorify your Lord and seek His forgiveness. Umar said: I know nothing but what you say. And the Commander of the Faithful was softer to the truth and more submissive to it. He said: There is no good in you if you do not speak, and no good in me if I do not listen. This is mentioned in al-Taqwim, likewise in al-Taysir. If this is his statement, how could Ibn Abbas fear him in presenting his opinion? And his story with the woman about his prohibition of excessive dowry is famous. In al-Taysir, Abu Ya'la and others narrated from Masruq: Umar ascended the pulpit of the Messenger of Allah and said: O people, why do you increase the dowries of women? The bridal gifts between the Messenger of Allah and his Companions were four hundred dirhams or less. If increasing that were piety or honor before Allah, you would not have preceded them to it. Then he descended. A woman of Quraysh intercepted him and asked: Did you forbid people to increase dowries beyond four hundred dirhams? He said: Yes. She said: Did you not hear Allah say: 'And you have given one of them a great amount, so do not take anything from it'? Umar said: O Allah, everyone is more knowledgeable than Umar. Then he returned and ascended the pulpit and said: I had forbidden you from increasing women's dowries beyond four hundred dirhams; whoever wishes may give whatever he likes. So if this was his state with this woman, what awe did Ibn Abbas have in presenting his opinion? Then the Commander of the Faithful consulted the Companions, and al-Abbas suggested al-awl, and the Companions agreed. There was no opinion of the Commander before it was established with the Companions. So what awe from the Commander did Ibn Abbas have? Then the proof they narrate from him in rejecting al-awl is irrational. The proponents of al-awl do not say half and a third to contradict what he cited; rather they also say that Allah did not assign the shares thus, so each share decreases so that half and a third do not occur. So what he used to refute is exactly their proof. This type of refutation is far from Ibn Abbas. Then what they attributed to him in such a case is that he drops the shares of daughters and sisters because they may be agnates and leave the prescribed shares, so they are weak in entitlement to the share, so their share is dropped. This also has no clear face. Their shares are also established by text, even if in the same state as others' shares. So dropping one to complete others has no legal or rational basis. The truth is that Ibn Abbas is innocent of such a statement. So understand, and Allah knows best about the conditions of the special believers.

The third principle: Ijma. And it may be said, as in al-Tahrir: We do not accept that honoring by silence about the truth is sin; rather sin is only silence about evil, and the statement of the mujtahid is not like that; it is obligatory to act upon it. I say in defense: The discussion here is before the settling of the schools, but during research and debate to ascertain the truth. So the situation is that of istifta'. The mufti must state his opinion and what he considers true. So silence about stating the truth is abandoning an obligation, which is forbidden. Likewise, honoring (by silence) is forbidden. Understand. And it may be said: Stating what he considers true is only obligatory when asked, and there is no question here. Perhaps he remained silent because the researchers are mujtahids and act on their opinions, and there is no prohibition in that. So reflect. Does the state of research count as a question in meaning, thus requiring disclosure? That is apparent. The Zanians, who hold that this ijma is probable, their silence is apparent in their agreement with those who speak, even if it may be for another reason. But it is extremely unlikely given their known habit of not fearing any blame in stating the truth. Their leaving silence in such matters is established by induction from their noble states, like the saying of Mu'adh to Umar when he was about to flog a woman who committed zina: Allah has not given a way for what is in her womb (i.e., the fetus), and flogging may harm it. And the saying of Ubayda to Ali: I prefer your opinion with the congregation. And other incidents, like the saying of Ibn Mas'ud to Abu Musa al-Ash'ari when he ruled to separate the Bedouin's wife due to her drinking his milk: The period of breastfeeding is two years by text. Know that if this proof stood, it would indicate that ijma is definitive. Because if the silent ones' silence is consent, then ijma is established by their agreement; if they concealed the truth and were sinful, they lose the qualification for ijma, so ijma is established by those who spoke alone. Thus ijma is realized with the silence of some and the fatwa of others, which is definitive. Understand. Al-Jubba'i said: Before the extinction (of the generation), the mentioned probabilities exist, so it is not binding. After extinction, they disappear entirely, so it becomes decisive ijma. But perhaps one may deny the disappearance, rather it weakens after (extinction) because the possibility of fear remains, even from the followers (muqallids). Ibn Abi Hurayra said: The usual practice is not to object to a judgment even if it contradicts one's own view, so silence during judgment is not evidence of consent, unlike fatwa, where objection is usual if it contradicts one's view. This is because the judge is feared and respected, so he is not objected to. And it is answered that this absence of objection to the judgment is after settling (of schools) i.e., after the schools are established and the judge's school is determined, while the discussion here is before that. Fatwa and judgment are then similar in objection when they contradict. Have you not seen how Mu'adh objected? I say: Judgment in matters of ijtihad is not overturned, so it is not objected to because there is no benefit in objection. So reflect and contemplate.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of objections against using silence as evidence for ijma. The author begins by proving that Ibn Abbas was not intimidated by Umar, thus silence of later scholars cannot be attributed to fear. He cites multiple incidents from Umar's caliphate where Ibn Abbas contradicted him openly. Then he discusses the principle of silence (sukut) as proof of consent. The 'Zanniyyun' (those who consider ijma only probable) argue that silence might have other motives. The author rebuts by citing the habit of the Companions to speak the truth fearlessly. He then analyzes al-Jubba'i's condition of the generation's extinction, arguing that even after extinction the possibility of fear remains. The discussion concludes that silence in a context of research and debate, before schools of law are settled, is proof of consent and establishes definitive ijma.

Key Terms
سكوت (sukut): Silence of a qualified mujtahid in response to a legal opinion presented for debate, interpreted as consent unless proven otherwise.
إجماع (ijma): Consensus of all mujtahids of an era on a legal ruling, which is a primary source of law and can be definitive.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the dowry incident support the Hanafi view that silence is not due to fear?
A) The woman was silenced by Umar
B) Umar punished the woman for contradicting him
C) Umar accepted the correction and retracted his ruling
D) The incident shows that only men could speak

Key Takeaway
The boldness of the Companions in contradicting authority proves that their silence in other contexts must be interpreted as consent, not fear.

Al-Ijma — Consensus
advanced
12 min
The Hanafi Doctrine: Silence as Definitive Proof of Ijma
Theoretical justification of silence as proof of ijma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that silence of mujtahids after debate establishes definitive (qat'i) ijma, and evaluate the opposing arguments from the Zanniyyun, al-Jubba'i, and Ibn Abi Hurayra.

Core Idea
Silence of mujtahids in debate is equivalent to express consent, making ijma qat'i.

After establishing that fear is not a valid explanation for silence, the passage moves to the core theoretical debate: does silence of a mujtahid count as consent for the purpose of ijma? The Hanafi school holds that it does, and that such ijma is definitive (qat'i). This lesson dissects the arguments of those who disagree—the Zanniyyun (who consider ijma only probable), al-Jubba'i (who requires extinction of the generation), and Ibn Abi Hurayra (who distinguishes between fatwa and judgment)—and presents the Hanafi responses.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Clearly state that the Hanafi school holds that silence of a mujtahid in the context of research and debate, before the settlement of schools, is evidence of consent and establishes definitive ijma.
The context is crucial: 'قبل استقرار المذاهب' (before the settlement of schools).
2
Present the Zanniyyun's objection
The Zanniyyun (those who say ijma is only zanni) argue that silence may be due to other reasons, so it does not create certainty.
They admit that silence 'is apparent' in agreement, but since other possibilities exist, it is not qat'i.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Zanniyyun
The author uses induction (istiqra') from the conduct of the Companions, who never silenced the truth, to argue that other possibilities are extremely unlikely.
Examples: Mu'adh objecting to Umar, Ubayda objecting to Ali, Ibn Mas'ud objecting to Abu Musa.
4
Examine al-Jubba'i's condition of extinction
Al-Jubba'i says that before the extinction of the generation, probabilities remain (so no ijma); after extinction, those probabilities vanish, making ijma decisive. The author counters that probabilities may still remain even after extinction.
The Hanafi position is stronger: silence before extinction can be definitive if the context is proper.
5
Analyze Ibn Abi Hurayra's distinction between fatwa and judgment
Ibn Abi Hurayra argues that silence in the face of a judge's ruling is not evidence of consent (due to respect), but silence in the face of a fatwa is. The author responds that before schools settled, both fatwa and judgment were similar; after settlement, judgment may not be contradicted because it is not overturned.
The key is that the discussion is about the period before school settlement, where both fatwa and judgment were open to debate.

أربعمائة درهم، فمن شاء أن يعطي ما أحب. فإذا كان له مع هذه المرأة ما علمت فأي مهابة كانت لابن عباس في عرض رأيه، ثم إن أمير المؤمنين عمر استشار الصحابة، فأشار العباس بالعول، ثم اتفق الصحابة، ولم يكن هناك لأمير المؤمنين رأى قبل تقرره عند الصحابة فأي مهابة من أمير كانت لابن عباس، ثم إن الدليل الذي ينقلون عنه في إبطال العول غير معقول، فإن قائلي العول لا يقولون بنصفين وثلث حتى يرد عليهم ما أورد بل هم أيضاً يقولون إن الله لم يجعل السهام كذلك، فينقص سهم كل حتى لا يلزم نصفان وثلث فالذي رد به هو بعينه حجة لهم. وهذا النحو من الرد بعيد عن ابن عباس كل البعد ثم الذي نسبوه إليه في مثل هذه الصورة أن يسقط سهام البنات والأخوات لأنهن قد يكن عصبة ويخرجن عن السهام المقدرة، فهن ضعيفات في استحقاق السهم فيسقط سهمهنّ، وهذا أيضاً لا يظهر له وجه، فإن سهامهن أيضاً ثبتت بالنص ولو في حال كسهام غيرهن فإسقاط واحد لإتمام آخرين مما لا وجه له شرعاً وعقلاً، فالحق أن ابن عباس بريء عن مثل هذا القول فافهم والله أعلم بأحوال خواص المؤمنين

الأصل الثالث الإجماع وقد يقال كما في التحرير) لا نسلم أن التعظيم بالسكوت عن الحق فسق بل (الفسق إنما هو السكوت عن منكر، وقول المجتهد ليس كذلك بل هو واجب العمل (أقول) في دفعه (الكلام) ههنا (قبل استقرار المذاهب، بل عند البحث والمناظرة فيها لتحقيق الحق (فالمقام مقام الاستفتاء، وعلى المفتي يجب إظهار (قوله وما هو الحق عنده فالسكوت عن إظهار الحق ترك الواجب وهو حرام، وكذا التعظيم (فافهم) وقد يقال : إظهار ما هو الحق عنده إنما يجب عند السؤال، ولا سؤال ههنا فلعله إنما سكت لأن الباحثين مجتهدون فيعملون برأيهم، وهذا لا حرمة فيه فتأمل فيه، فإن حالة البحث هل هي سؤال معنى فيجب الإظهار، وهو الظاهر (الظنيون) القائلون بظنية هذا الإجماع (سكوتهم ظاهر في موافقتهم القائلين وإن كان يحتمل أن يكون لأمر آخر لكنه بعيد غاية البعد لما علم من عادتهم لكونهم لا يخافون لومة لائم في إظهار الحق (ترك السكوت في مثله بالاستقراء) في أحوالهم الشريفة (كقول معاذ لعمر) أمير المؤمنين لما بجلد امرأة زنت (ما جعل الله على ما في بطنها سبيلا) والجلد يحتمل السراية إليه وقول عبيدة لعلي أمير المؤمنين رأيك في الجماعة أحب إلى غير ذلك من الوقائع، كقول ابن مسعود لأبي موسى الأشعري حين حكم بفرقة زوجة الأعرابي بشربه لبنها : إن مدة الرضاع سنتان بالنص، اعلم أن هذا الدليل لو تم لدل على كون الإجماع قطعياً فإن الساكتين إن كان سكوتهم رضا فقد تم الإجماع بموافقتهم، وإن كانوا كتموا الحق وسكتوا ففسقوا، فقد خرجوا عن أهلية الإجماع فتم الإجماع بالقائلين فقط، فتحقق الإجماع عند سكوت البعض وفتوى البعض قطعي فافهم (الجبائي) قال (قبل الانقراض (الاحتمالات) المذكورة (قائمة) فلا يكون حجة (وبعده تضمحل بالكلية فيكون إجماعاً قاطعاً وربما يمنع) الاضمحلال (بل يضعف) بعده فإن احتمال الخوف باق ولو من المقلدين قال ابن أبي هريرة العادة أن لا ينكر الحكم ولو كان مخالفاً لرأيه فلا يكون السكوت عند القضاء دليل الرضا (بخلاف الفتوى) فإن العادة فيها الإنكار إن كان مخالفاً وذلك لأن الحاكم يهاب ويوقر) فلا ينكر عليه (ويجاب بأن ذلك أي عدم إنكار الحكم بعد الاستقرار أي بعد استقرار المذاهب وتعين مذهب الحاكم (والكلام) ههنا قبله والفتيا والحكم حينئذ سواء في الإنكار عليه عند المخالفة، ألم تر كيف رد معاذاً (أقول : الحكم في المجتهد فيه لا ينقض فلا ينكر عليه لعدم الفائدة في الإنكار (فتدبر) وتأمل فيه .
So below is about one who is just. And what is narrated from Ibn Abbas, even if al-Tahawi narrated it, it is not authentic, and there is hidden discontinuity. How could it be? He, meaning the Commander of the Faithful Umar, used to give him precedence over the elders, ask him, and consider his opinion good. So how could he have awe of him in presenting his opinion? Al-Bukhari narrated from Ibn Abbas: Umar used to include me with the elders of Badr, and some of them felt resentful and said: Why do you include this boy with us when we have sons like him? Umar said: He is one you know. Then one day he invited me and included me with them. I saw that he only invited me that day to show them. He said: What do you say about Allah's word: 'When the victory of Allah and the conquest comes'? Some said: We are commanded to praise Allah and seek His forgiveness when His victory and conquest come to us. Some remained silent. He said to me: Do you say that, Ibn Abbas? I said: No. He said: What do you say? I said: It is the sign of the Messenger of Allah's death, Allah informed him of it. He said: 'When the victory of Allah and the conquest comes' is the sign of your death, so glorify your Lord and seek His forgiveness. Umar said: I know nothing but what you say. And the Commander of the Faithful was softer to the truth and more submissive to it. He said: There is no good in you if you do not speak, and no good in me if I do not listen. This is mentioned in al-Taqwim, likewise in al-Taysir. If this is his statement, how could Ibn Abbas fear him in presenting his opinion? And his story with the woman about his prohibition of excessive dowry is famous. In al-Taysir, Abu Ya'la and others narrated from Masruq: Umar ascended the pulpit of the Messenger of Allah and said: O people, why do you increase the dowries of women? The bridal gifts between the Messenger of Allah and his Companions were four hundred dirhams or less. If increasing that were piety or honor before Allah, you would not have preceded them to it. Then he descended. A woman of Quraysh intercepted him and asked: Did you forbid people to increase dowries beyond four hundred dirhams? He said: Yes. She said: Did you not hear Allah say: 'And you have given one of them a great amount, so do not take anything from it'? Umar said: O Allah, everyone is more knowledgeable than Umar. Then he returned and ascended the pulpit and said: I had forbidden you from increasing women's dowries beyond four hundred dirhams; whoever wishes may give whatever he likes. So if this was his state with this woman, what awe did Ibn Abbas have in presenting his opinion? Then the Commander of the Faithful consulted the Companions, and al-Abbas suggested al-awl, and the Companions agreed. There was no opinion of the Commander before it was established with the Companions. So what awe from the Commander did Ibn Abbas have? Then the proof they narrate from him in rejecting al-awl is irrational. The proponents of al-awl do not say half and a third to contradict what he cited; rather they also say that Allah did not assign the shares thus, so each share decreases so that half and a third do not occur. So what he used to refute is exactly their proof. This type of refutation is far from Ibn Abbas. Then what they attributed to him in such a case is that he drops the shares of daughters and sisters because they may be agnates and leave the prescribed shares, so they are weak in entitlement to the share, so their share is dropped. This also has no clear face. Their shares are also established by text, even if in the same state as others' shares. So dropping one to complete others has no legal or rational basis. The truth is that Ibn Abbas is innocent of such a statement. So understand, and Allah knows best about the conditions of the special believers.

The third principle: Ijma. And it may be said, as in al-Tahrir: We do not accept that honoring by silence about the truth is sin; rather sin is only silence about evil, and the statement of the mujtahid is not like that; it is obligatory to act upon it. I say in defense: The discussion here is before the settling of the schools, but during research and debate to ascertain the truth. So the situation is that of istifta'. The mufti must state his opinion and what he considers true. So silence about stating the truth is abandoning an obligation, which is forbidden. Likewise, honoring (by silence) is forbidden. Understand. And it may be said: Stating what he considers true is only obligatory when asked, and there is no question here. Perhaps he remained silent because the researchers are mujtahids and act on their opinions, and there is no prohibition in that. So reflect. Does the state of research count as a question in meaning, thus requiring disclosure? That is apparent. The Zanians, who hold that this ijma is probable, their silence is apparent in their agreement with those who speak, even if it may be for another reason. But it is extremely unlikely given their known habit of not fearing any blame in stating the truth. Their leaving silence in such matters is established by induction from their noble states, like the saying of Mu'adh to Umar when he was about to flog a woman who committed zina: Allah has not given a way for what is in her womb (i.e., the fetus), and flogging may harm it. And the saying of Ubayda to Ali: I prefer your opinion with the congregation. And other incidents, like the saying of Ibn Mas'ud to Abu Musa al-Ash'ari when he ruled to separate the Bedouin's wife due to her drinking his milk: The period of breastfeeding is two years by text. Know that if this proof stood, it would indicate that ijma is definitive. Because if the silent ones' silence is consent, then ijma is established by their agreement; if they concealed the truth and were sinful, they lose the qualification for ijma, so ijma is established by those who spoke alone. Thus ijma is realized with the silence of some and the fatwa of others, which is definitive. Understand. Al-Jubba'i said: Before the extinction (of the generation), the mentioned probabilities exist, so it is not binding. After extinction, they disappear entirely, so it becomes decisive ijma. But perhaps one may deny the disappearance, rather it weakens after (extinction) because the possibility of fear remains, even from the followers (muqallids). Ibn Abi Hurayra said: The usual practice is not to object to a judgment even if it contradicts one's own view, so silence during judgment is not evidence of consent, unlike fatwa, where objection is usual if it contradicts one's view. This is because the judge is feared and respected, so he is not objected to. And it is answered that this absence of objection to the judgment is after settling (of schools) i.e., after the schools are established and the judge's school is determined, while the discussion here is before that. Fatwa and judgment are then similar in objection when they contradict. Have you not seen how Mu'adh objected? I say: Judgment in matters of ijtihad is not overturned, so it is not objected to because there is no benefit in objection. So reflect and contemplate.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a detailed refutation of objections against using silence as evidence for ijma. The author begins by proving that Ibn Abbas was not intimidated by Umar, thus silence of later scholars cannot be attributed to fear. He cites multiple incidents from Umar's caliphate where Ibn Abbas contradicted him openly. Then he discusses the principle of silence (sukut) as proof of consent. The 'Zanniyyun' (those who consider ijma only probable) argue that silence might have other motives. The author rebuts by citing the habit of the Companions to speak the truth fearlessly. He then analyzes al-Jubba'i's condition of the generation's extinction, arguing that even after extinction the possibility of fear remains. The discussion concludes that silence in a context of research and debate, before schools of law are settled, is proof of consent and establishes definitive ijma.

Key Terms
الظنيون (al-zanniyyun): Those who hold that ijma is only probable (zanni) and not definitive (qat'i), especially when based on silence.
الانقراض (al-inqirad): The extinction of the generation of scholars who were contemporaries of the debated issue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the Hanafi school considers ijma based on silence to be definitive (qat'i)?
A) Because all scholars always agree
B) Because silence in a debate context would be a sin if the scholar disagreed, so it must indicate consent
C) Because silence is easier to prove than explicit statements
D) Because the Quran commands silence in disputes

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, silence of mujtahids during research and debate is not weak evidence; it is a strong indication of consent that establishes definitive ijma.

Al-Ijma — Consensus

Consensus by Action of All Mujtahids
Ruling on consensus established by unanimous action without verbal statement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on ijma by action, identify how the analogy to the Prophet's act is made, and apply the default of ibaha unless a context indicates otherwise.

Core Idea
Unanimous action of mujtahids is like the Prophet's act: default ibaha.

This passage addresses whether consensus (ijma) can be established by the unanimous action of all mujtahids in an era, without any verbal statement. The preferred Hanafi view treats such collective action analogously to the action of the Prophet (ﷺ) because the ummah is infallible. This raises important questions about the default legal ruling of such an act and whether non-verbal consensus requires stricter conditions than verbal ijma. We will unpack the reasoning, the objections raised by Ibn al-Sam'ani, and the implications for the extinction requirement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the Hanafi position
Recognize that the passage deals with whether unanimous action alone, without speech, can constitute ijma. The chosen (mukhtar) view is that it is analogous to the Prophet's act.
The key phrase: 'فالمختار أنه كفعل الرسول'
2
Trace the analogy (qiyas) justifying the position
The analogy is based on infallibility (isma): as the Prophet is infallible, so is the ummah collectively. Thus their unanimous action carries similar legal weight.
The proof is general (zanni both in transmission and meaning) but considered sufficient.
3
Apply the default ruling: ibaha unless a qarina
Imam Abu Hanifa's school interprets the act as permissible (ibaha) by default, unless there is a contextual indicator (qarina) suggesting obligation, prohibition, etc.
This mirrors the treatment of the Prophet's actions: asl al-fi'l al-ibaha.
4
Examine Ibn al-Sam'ani's objection
Ibn al-Sam'ani argues that only actions issued as rulings or explanations can form ijma; ordinary actions do not. The passage dismisses this objection as having no clear justification.
The refutation: 'ولا يظهر له وجه' – 'no justification for this is apparent.'
5
Understand the extinction condition (shart al-inqirad)
Those who require the extinction of the generation for verbal ijma should require it even more for action-based ijma, because actions are more easily retracted than words.
The passage ends with 'فافهم' – 'so understand,' indicating careful attention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: لو اتفقوا على فعل بأن عمل الكل فعلاً ولا قول هناك (فالمختار أنه كفعل الرسول صلى الله عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه وسلم لأن العصمة ثابتة لهم (الإجماعهم لعموم الدلائل التي مرت كثبوتها (له عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام، وإذا كان كفعله عليه السلام فتأتي المذاهب المذكورة سابقاً والإمام يحمل على الإباحة إلا بقرينة) وهو الأظهر (وابن السمعاني) قال : كل فعل لم يخرج مخرج الحكم والبيان لا ينعقد به الإجماع) ولا يظهر له وجه (ومن اشترط (الانقراض لعصر المجمعين في القولي، فالفعلي أولى بالاشتراط لقوة احتمال الرجوع فيه من القولي فافهم.
Issue: If they (the mujtahids) all agree on an action, meaning everyone performs an act and there is no speech, then the chosen view is that it is like the act of the Messenger (peace be upon him and his family and companions) because infallibility is established for them, due to the generality of proofs that have passed, like its establishment for him (peace and blessing be upon him and his family). And if it is like his act, then the previously mentioned schools apply: Imam (Abu Hanifa) interprets it as permissibility except with a context indicating otherwise, which is the most apparent view. Ibn al-Sam'ani said: Any act that does not come in the context of a ruling or explanation does not constitute ijma by it. And no justification for this is apparent. And whoever stipulated the extinction of the generation of the mujtahids in verbal ijma, then in action-based ijma the stipulation is even more necessary because of the stronger possibility of retraction from action than from speech. So understand.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This is a separate mas'ala on whether consensus can be established by unanimous action alone, without any verbal statements. The preferred view in the Hanafi school is that it is like the act of the Prophet, because the infallibility of the ummah is established by general proofs. Therefore, the same rules apply: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the default interpretation of such an action is permissibility (ibaha) unless there is a context indicating obligation, etc. Ibn al-Sam'ani objected that only acts that are evidently rulings or explanations can be a basis for ijma; otherwise not. The author dismisses this objection. Additionally, those who require extinction of the generation for verbal ijma should require it even more for action-based ijma, since actions can be retracted more easily than words.

Key Terms
فعل (fi'l): Action performed by all mujtahids of an era unanimously, considered as a form of consensus.
الإباحة (al-ibaha): Permissibility – the default legal ruling for an action unless there is a contextual clue indicating otherwise.
الانقراض (al-inqirad): The condition that the generation of mujtahids has passed away, required by some for the binding nature of ijma.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen (mukhtar) view in the passage, what is the default legal ruling of a unanimous action of mujtahids?
A) Obligatory (wajib)
B) Recommended (mandub)
C) Permissible (ibaha) unless a context indicates otherwise
D) Prohibited (haram) unless proven permissible

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts unanimous action of mujtahids as a valid form of ijma, analogous to the Prophet's act, with a default of permissibility unless a context indicates otherwise.

Al-Ijma — Consensus

Silence of Scholars as Evidence of Ijma'
Dispute on whether silence equals consent for ijma'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on whether the silence of a scholar (sukut) constitutes consent for ijma', and evaluate the counterarguments from al-Jubba'i.

Core Idea
Silence of a fearless scholar before madhhabs is consent—their habit proves agreement.

This lesson examines a key usul al-fiqh dispute: does the silence of a mujtahid during debate imply his consent, thereby contributing to ijma'? The Hanafi school argues that, given the scholars' known courage in speaking truth, their silence is a strong indicator of agreement. However, opponents like al-Jubba'i raise the possibility of fear or respect as reasons for silence. We will walk through the arguments and evaluate which position is stronger.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Dispute
Recognize that the issue is whether the silence (sukut) of a scholar at the time of debate indicates consent (rida) for ijma'. The context is before the establishment of madhhabs, when scholars are researching the truth.
The passage distinguishes between silence in a fatwa situation and silence in a judgment (qada).
2
State the Hanafi Position
The Hanafi position, supported by the 'zanniyun' (those who consider ijma' as zanni), is that silence is apparent consent because scholars have a known habit of not fearing any blamer when it comes to speaking the truth.
This is based on induction (istiqra) from the conduct of the Sahaba.
3
Examine the Dalil: Induction from Sahaba
The Hanafi proof cites examples like Mu'adh objecting to Umar, Ubayda to Ali, and Ibn Mas'ud to Abu Musa. These show that when truth was at stake, the Sahaba spoke up. Therefore, silence in similar contexts implies consent.
The author argues that this proof, if sound, would make ijma' qat'i (definitive).
4
Present the Opposing View: Al-Jubba'i
Al-Jubba'i argues that before the extinction of the era (inqirad al-asr), possibilities like fear or respect remain, so silence is not proof. After the era, these disappear, but even then, fear from later followers may persist.
Ibn Abi Huraira adds that people may not object to a judge out of fear and respect, unlike a fatwa where objection is expected.
5
Analyse the Hanafi Response and Tarjih
The response is that the discussion occurs before madhhabs are established, so fear of authority is not a factor. Even after, the silent scholars may still be considered consenting because their habit is to speak out. The text leans towards Hanafi view, concluding that silence can yield qat'i ijma.
The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf): if silence is proof, ijma' can be qat'i; if not, it remains zanni.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الثالث الإجماع عباده وقد يقال كما في التحرير) لا نسلم أن التعظيم بالسكوت عن الحق فسق بل (الفسق إنما هو السكوت عن منكر، وقول المجتهد ليس كذلك بل هو واجب العمل (أقول) في دفعه (الكلام) ههنا (قبل استقرار المذاهب، بل عند البحث والمناظرة فيها لتحقيق الحق (فالمقام مقام الاستفتاء، وعلى المفتي يجب إظهار (قوله وما هو الحق عنده فالسكوت عن إظهار الحق ترك الواجب وهو حرام، وكذا التعظيم (فافهم) وقد يقال مثله بالاستقراء) في أحوالهم الشريفة (كقول معاذ لعمر) أمير المؤمنين لما بجلد امرأة زنت (ما جعل الله على ما في بطنها سبيلا) والجلد يحتمل السراية إليه وقول عبيدة لعلي أمير المؤمنين رأيك في الجماعة أحب سكوتهم رضا فقد تم الإجماع بموافقتهم، وإن كانوا كتموا الحق وسكتوا ففسقوا، فقد خرجوا عن أهلية الإجماع فتم الإجماع بالقائلين فقط، فتحقق الإجماع عند سكوت البعض وفتوى البعض قطعي فافهم (الجبائي) قال (قبل الانقراض (الاحتمالات) المذكورة (قائمة) فلا يكون حجة (وبعده تضمحل بالكلية فيكون إجماعاً قاطعاً وربما يمنع) الاضمحلال (بل يضعف) بعده فإن احتمال الخوف باق ولو من المقلدين قال ابن أبي هريرة العادة أن لا ينكر الحكم ولو كان مخالفاً لرأيه فلا يكون السكوت عند القضاء دليل الرضا (بخلاف الفتوى) فإن العادة فيها الإنكار إن كان مخالفاً وذلك لأن الحاكم يهاب ويوقر) فلا ينكر عليه (ويجاب بأن ذلك أي عدم إنكار الحكم بعد الاستقرار أي بعد استقرار المذاهب وتعين مذهب الحاكم (والكلام) ههنا قبله والفتيا والحكم حينئذ سواء في الإنكار عليه عند المخالفة، ألم تر كيف رد معاذاً (أقول : الحكم في المجتهد فيه لا ينقض فلا ينكر عليه لعدم الفائدة في الإنكار (فتدبر) وتأمل فيه .
The third principle: ijma'. And it is said (as in al-Tahrir): We do not accept that showing respect by remaining silent about the truth is sin. Rather, sin is only silence about an evil. And the statement of a mujtahid is not like that; rather, it is obligatory to act. I say in response: The speech here is before the establishment of the schools, but rather at the time of research and debate to realize the truth. So the situation is that of seeking a fatwa, and the mufti must show his view and what is the truth according to him. So silence about showing the truth leaves the obligatory, which is forbidden, as is showing respect. Understand. And it is said: Showing what is the truth according to him is only obligatory when asked, and there is no question here. Perhaps he remained silent because the researchers are mujtahids and act on their own opinion, so there is no prohibition in that. Reflect on this, for the state of research is it a question in meaning? Then he must show, and that is apparent. Those who say ijma is zanni: their silence is apparent in their agreement with those who speak, even if it might be for another reason, but it is extremely far-fetched given their known habit that they do not fear any blamer in showing the truth. They do not remain silent in such matters, as is known by induction from their noble states, like the statement of Mu'adh to Umar, the Commander of the Faithful, when he was about to whip a woman who committed adultery: 'Allah has not made any way for what is in her womb' and whipping might lead to her death. And the statement of Ubayda to Ali, the Commander of the Faithful: 'Your opinion with the group is more beloved to me.' And other incidents like the statement of Ibn Mas'ud to Abu Musa al-Ash'ari when he judged the divorce of the Bedouin's wife due to drinking her milk: 'The period of breastfeeding is two years by text.' Know that if this proof is sound, it would indicate that ijma is qat'i, because if the silent ones' silence is consent, then ijma is completed by their agreement. If they concealed the truth and were sinful, then they are removed from the eligibility of ijma, and ijma is completed by those who spoke only. So ijma when some are silent and some give fatwa is qat'i; understand. Al-Jubba'i said: Before the extinction of the era, the mentioned possibilities exist, so it is not a proof. After extinction, they disappear entirely, so it becomes qat'i ijma. And perhaps it is prevented from disappearing; rather it weakens after extinction, because the possibility of fear remains even from laymen. Ibn Abi Huraira said: The habit is that people do not object to a judgment even if it contradicts their opinion, so silence in a judgment does not indicate consent, unlike a fatwa, because the habit is to object if it contradicts. That is because the judge is feared and respected, so he is not objected to. And the response is: That, i.e., the lack of objection to a judgment, occurs after the establishment of the schools and the specification of the judge's madhhab. The discussion here is before that. And fatwa and judgment are then the same in terms of objection when they contradict. Do you not see how Mu'adh refrained? I say: The judgment in a matter of ijtihad is not overturned, so it is not objected to because there is no benefit in objection. So reflect and ponder on it.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a dispute in usul al-fiqh about whether the silence of a scholar (sukut) at the time of debate indicates his agreement for purposes of ijma'. The author first refutes the claim that silence out of respect is sinful; rather, silence about an evil is sinful. The context is before the establishment of the madhahib, when scholars are researching. The mufti must speak the truth. If he is silent, it is prohibited. Then an objection is raised: speaking is only required when asked. The author notes that the research situation is effectively a question. The 'zanniyun' (those who consider ijma' as zanni) hold that silence is apparent agreement because of the scholars' habit of not fearing blame. Examples from the Sahaba are cited to show they spoke up when needed. The author concludes that if silence indicates consent, ijma' becomes qat'i—either the silent ones agree, or they become sinful and disqualified, so ijma' is formed by the speakers alone. Al-Jubba'i argues that before the generation passes, the possibilities (like fear) remain, so silence is not proof. After extinction, the possibilities disappear. But the author responds that fear may remain even from later followers, so silence does not become consent. A distinction is made between qada (judgment) and fatwa: in qada, people may not object due to respect for the judge, but in fatwa, objection is expected. However, the discussion here is before the schools were fixed, so fatwa and judgment are similar. The passage ends with reflection.

Key Terms
سكوت (sukut): Silence of a scholar when a ruling is being discussed or given; its legal implication for consensus is debated.
الظنيون (al-zanniyun): Those who hold that ijma' (consensus) is only zanni (speculative), not qat'i (definitive).
الانقراض (al-inqirad): The passing away of the entire generation of scholars involved in a debate; after this, the possibilities that prevented proof disappear.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the silence of a scholar at the time of debate considered consent rather than fear?
A) Because they are never afraid of anyone.
B) Because their habit is that they do not fear any blamer in showing the truth.
C) Because they are forced to agree silently.
D) Because the question is hypothetical.

Key Takeaway
Silence of a scholar in a pre-madhhab debate is a strong indicator of consent because of their established habit of speaking truth without fear.

Consensus by Action (Ijma' Fi'li)
Ruling on collective action as a form of consensus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on ijma' fi'li and apply its principles to determine the ruling of a collective scholarly action.

Core Idea
A collective action of all scholars is like the Prophet's action; it indicates permissibility unless contextual evidence suggests otherwise.

In Hanafi usul, consensus (ijma') is a primary source of law. While verbal consensus is straightforward, the status of consensus by action (ijma' fi'li) requires careful analysis. This passage from Musallam al-Thubut clarifies the Hanafi view that collective action of all scholars is equivalent to the Prophet's action and is interpreted as permissibility, setting the stage for understanding how silent practices inform the law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of consensus
The passage focuses on ijma' fi'li, which is when all scholars of an era perform a certain action without any verbal statement.
2
Recognize the Hanafi position
The preferred view is that it is like the action of the Prophet (peace be upon him) because the ummah is infallible (isma).
3
Determine the default legal interpretation
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that such an action indicates permissibility (ibaha) unless there is a contextual clue (qarina) indicating otherwise.
4
Understand the objection and its rejection
Ibn al-Sam'ani argued that only actions that are rulings or clarifications can form consensus, but the author dismisses this as baseless.
5
Note the condition of extinction of era
Those requiring extinction of the era for verbal consensus must require it even more for action-based consensus due to the stronger possibility of retraction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
· (مسألة: لو اتفقوا على فعل بأن عمل الكل فعلاً ولا قول هناك (فالمختار أنه كفعل الرسول صلى الله عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه وسلم لأن العصمة ثابتة لهم (الإجماعهم لعموم الدلائل التي مرت كثبوتها (له عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام، وإذا كان كفعله عليه السلام فتأتي المذاهب المذكورة سابقاً والإمام يحمل على الإباحة إلا بقرينة) وهو الأظهر (وابن السمعاني) قال : كل فعل لم يخرج مخرج الحكم والبيان لا ينعقد به الإجماع) ولا يظهر له وجه (ومن اشترط (الانقراض لعصر المجمعين في القولي، فالفعلي أولى بالاشتراط لقوة احتمال الرجوع فيه من القولي فافهم.
· (Issue: If they agree on an action - that all of them do an action without any statement there - then the preferred view is that it is like the action of the Messenger (peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions) because infallibility is established for them (the ummah) due to the general proofs that have passed attesting to its establishment for him (peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions). And if it is like his action, then the previously mentioned schools (of interpretation) apply. Imam (Abu Hanifa) interprets it as permissibility unless there is a context (qarina) indicating otherwise, and this is the most apparent. Ibn al-Sam'ani said: Any action that is not in the manner of a ruling or clarification does not constitute consensus. And this has no clear basis. And whoever requires the extinction of the era for consensus on a statement, then consensus by action is even more deserving of that condition because the possibility of retraction is stronger in action than in statement. Understand.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage states a rule about consensus by action (ijma' fi'li). When all scholars of an era perform a certain action without any verbal statement, this collective action is considered equivalent to the action of the Prophet (peace be upon him) because the ummah is protected from error (isma). Consequently, the same principles apply: the action is interpreted as permissibility (ibaha) unless there is a contextual clue (qarina) that indicates obligation, prohibition, etc. Imam Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) holds this view. Ibn al-Sam'ani differed, arguing that only actions that are clearly in the form of a ruling or explanation can form consensus, but the author dismisses this as baseless. Additionally, those who require the extinction of the era for consensus based on statements must require it even more for action-based consensus, because actions are more prone to be changed or retracted. Thus, the Hanafi position is that ijma' fi'li is a valid form of consensus with the same authority as the Prophet's action.

Key Terms
إجماع فعلي (ijma' fi'li): Consensus established through a collective action of all scholars without verbal statements.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view, what is the legal interpretation of a collective action by all scholars (ijma' fi'li)?
A) It indicates obligation.
B) It indicates permissibility unless there is a contextual clue indicating otherwise.
C) It is not considered consensus at all.
D) It is only valid if accompanied by a verbal statement.

Key Takeaway
Ijma' fi'li is a valid form of consensus in Hanafi usul, equivalent to the Prophet's action and indicating permissibility unless a qarina suggests otherwise.

The Rule on Introducing a Third Opinion When Two Exist
Explains rule and exceptions for introducing a third opinion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on introducing a third opinion when two opinions exist, identify when it is prohibited versus permissible, and apply the reasoning to examples like the waiting period of a pregnant widow.

Core Idea
A third opinion is prohibited if it overturns the universal agreement of the two earlier groups; otherwise it may be allowed.

In usul al-fiqh, consensus (ijma') is a primary source of law. A subtle issue arises when earlier scholars have only two opinions on a matter: can a later scholar introduce a third? The majority Hanafi position prohibits it if the third opinion contradicts what the two groups agreed upon. However, exceptions exist when the third opinion blends elements of the two without breaking their agreed universal ruling. This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut explains the rule, its evidence, and its application through concrete examples from Companions and Followers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule and Its Scope
State the majority view: if the scholars of an era have only two opinions on a matter, introducing a third is prohibited. Note that some restrict this to the Companions, while al-Amidi and al-Razi specify the prohibition only when the third contradicts what both groups agreed upon.
This is a dispute within the Hanafi school itself on the scope of the rule.
2
Examine Prohibited Cases (Third Raises the Agreed)
Look at the examples: 1) A buyer who has intercourse with a purchased slave-girl then finds a defect — agreement that he cannot return her without compensation. 2) Grandfather's inheritance — agreement that he inherits, so depriving him entirely is prohibited. 3) Pregnant widow's iddah — agreement that it is not only months, so saying only months is prohibited.
These examples show the 'third opinion' that contradicts the universal agreement of the two earlier groups.
3
Distinguish Permissible Third Opinions
Identify cases where a third opinion is permitted despite two existing opinions: detailed views in annulment by defects (e.g., saying some defects cause annulment, others not) and the mother's share when parents and spouse are present (e.g., one-third of total vs. one-third of remainder). These do not overturn the agreed universal but combine parts of each opinion.
The key is whether the third opinion 'raises' (reverses) the agreement or merely combines.
4
Analyze the Epistemological Basis
Understand the proof: the Quranic verse 'And whoever opposes the Messenger after guidance has become clear to him and follows other than the path of the believers' (4:115). The agreement of two groups on one aspect (even if incidental) is a proof because it prevents the ummah from agreeing on error. Therefore opposing that agreed aspect is prohibited.
This is the core dalil for the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : إذا اختلف و (لم يتجاوز أهل العصر عن قولين في مسألة لم يجز إحداث) قول (ثالث عند الأكثر) في التيسير» نص عليه الإمام محمد والشافعي رضي الله عنه في «رسالته» (وخصه بعض الحنفية بالصحابة وقالوا إذا اختلف الصحابة على قولين لم يجز إحداث ثالث، وأما إذا اختلف من بعدهم فيجوز إحداث ثالث ولا يظهر فارق فإن قلت : إذا لم يتجاوز التابعون عن القولين وتجاوز الصحابة فقولهم الثالث حي بدليله فلا يكون الإحداث مخالفاً للإجماع قلت : هذا إنما يصح إذا كان الخلاف السابق مانعاً للإجماع اللاحق على أنه يجوز ! ! إن لم يناظروا في المسألة التي لم يتجاوز التابعون عن قولين فيها بل سكتوا (وجاز) الإحداث (عند طائفة ثلث الباقي) بعد فرض الزوجين، وبالتفصيل لم يقل أحد، لكن غير رافع للمتفق عليه، بل في أحدهما موافق لمذهب، وفي آخر لآخر، فيجوز القول به واعلم أن هذا القول ليس مخالفاً لما عليه الجمهور، فإنهم إنما يقولون وهذا رافع لقوله : لو ثبت الإجماع لكن قد مر أن فيه للصحابة أقوالاً ثلاثة ، ثم المذكور في كتبنا علة التفريق في الجب، والعنة عدم قدرة الزوج على الإمساك بالمعروف، فلا بد من التسريح بالإحسان، وهو لا يتناول ما سواهما، وأيضاً : العيوب التي في الزوجة يمكن تخلص الزوج عنها بالتطليق، فلا تضرر له ببقاء النكاح، وعدم فسخ القاضي إياه فافهم (وأما الجواب) عن الدليل (بأن اتفاقهم على إنكار القول الثالث كان مشروطاً بعدمه، فلما حدث زال) الاتفاق على الإنكار فلا منع عن الإحداث (فمنقوض بالإجماع الوجداني) فإنه يمكن فيه أيضاً ذلك، فينبغي أن لا يمنع عن إحداث قول مخالف والاعتذار بأنه وإن جاز) إحداث قول مخالف والاعتذار وإن جاز إحداث قول مخالف للإجماع الوجداني (عقلاً لكن لم يعتبر فيه اجماعاً كما في المنهاج ضعيف لأن الفرق تحكم فتدبر واستدل بلزوم تخطئة كل فريق) يعني لو جاز إحداث ثالث لزم تخطئة كل فريق لكونه مخالفاً لهم (وفيه تخطئة كل الأمة) وأجيب بأن الممتنع تخطئة الكل فيما اتفقوا عليه (لا التخطئة مطلقاً) وههنا تخطئة فيما اختلفوا فيه، وهذا لا يغني من الحق شيئاً فإن دلائل امتناع التخطئة عامة كيف الممتنع وقوع ـة في الخطأ ولعل مقصود المستدل الاستدلال بالدليل العقلي وإلا فيرجع حاصله إلى الدليل المذكور سابقاً فرد بأن التخطئة في كل عصر إنما علمت لمن خالف فيما اتفق فيه لا فيما اختلف فيه فتأمل فيه. أصحاب الإحداث (قالوا : أولاً اختلافهم) في المسألة (دليل أنها اجتهادية) عندهم وإلا لما اختلفوا فلزم التسويغ فيها كل قوله فلا مانع من إحداث ثالث الأمة (، وهي باطلة
Issue: If the people of an era have differed and not exceeded two opinions on a matter, it is not permissible to introduce a third opinion according to the majority. In 'al-Taysir', Imam Muhammad and al-Shafi'i stated it in his 'Risala'. Some Hanafis restricted it to the Companions, saying if the Companions differed on two opinions, it is not permissible to introduce a third; but if those after them differed, it is permissible to introduce a third, and no difference appears. If you say: If the Followers did not exceed two opinions and the Companions exceeded, then the third opinion lives with its proof, so the introduction is not contrary to consensus. I say: This is valid only if the previous disagreement prevents later consensus from occurring, indeed if they did not deliberate on the matter in which the Followers did not exceed two opinions but remained silent, then introducing a third is permissible according to a group absolutely. The choice of al-Amidi and al-Razi is that if the third raises what they both agreed upon, its introduction is prohibited. Like the buyer who had intercourse with a virgin slave-girl purchased and then a defect appeared that was with the seller: some say the return is prevented, as from Ali and Ibn Mas'ud; some say return with compensation, as from Umar and Zayd ibn Thabit, and the compensation is one-tenth of the value. So returning without compensation was not permitted because there was agreement on not returning without compensation. In 'al-Taysir' quoting from some commentaries of 'al-Tahrir', the mentioned reports from the mentioned Companions are not confirmed. Yes, it is authentic from the Followers: prevention of return from the pole of poles Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz and Imam Hasan al-Basri, may Allah sanctify their souls; return with compensation from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyab, Shurayh, and Muhammad ibn Sirin; and return without compensation from al-Harith, from the jurists of Kufa, peers of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i. And similarly the division of the grandfather (the true grandfather) with the brother, as from Ali and Zayd ibn Thabit after they retracted their statement of depriving the grandfather and preventing him, i.e., the grandfather preventing the brother from inheritance, as from the Khalifa of the Messenger of Allah, Abu Bakr al-Siddiq al-Akbar, and Umar, Ibn al-Zubayr, and Ibn Abbas. He said: 'Does Zayd ibn Thabit not fear Allah, making the son's son a son when the son is absent, but not making the father's father a father when the father is absent?' They all agreed that the grandfather has inheritance, but they differed on the amount. So deprivation and complete removal of inheritance from the grandfather is contrary to consensus, so its introduction is not permitted. And similarly the waiting period of a pregnant woman whose husband has died: by giving birth, as from Ibn Mas'ud and Abu Hurayra, or the later of the two terms: giving birth and the months, as from Ali and Ibn Abbas, so they all agreed on negating the months, so one cannot say only months, otherwise it would raise what they agreed upon in the matter. So it does not prevent the introduction of a third? Like the detailed view in annulment due to defects: leprosy, elephantiasis, insanity in whichever spouse, and impotence and being castrated in the husband, and fusion and atresia in the wife. Some say they do not cause annulment at all; some say yes, they cause annulment in all cases. So the detailed view was not said by anyone, but it does not raise anything they agreed upon, rather in some parts it is with the statement of some, in others with the statement of others, so its introduction is permissible. In 'al-Taysir' quoting from some commentaries that the three opinions are famous from the Companions. And similarly in the case of husband and wife with parents: some say the mother gets one-third of the total, some say one-third of the remainder after the spouses' shares, and no one said a detailed view, but it does not raise the agreed-upon, rather in one it agrees with one school, in another with another, so it is permissible to say it. Know that this statement is not contrary to what the majority hold, for they only prohibit introducing a third because it raises what they agreed upon, and this also acknowledges that. Rather they deny in some partial cases the raising of the agreed-upon due to lack of commonality in the universal according to them, and that is another matter. So understand. If you say: The later mujtahid's opposition to earlier mujtahids has become widespread without any denial, and this is a consensus, so how can introducing a contrary opinion be prohibited? I say: It is only valid that the later opposes the earlier according to the majority after a prior speaker who states the later's opinion, even if that speaker is not famous. For us, the difference on two opinions without exceeding them is agreement on one of them by way of preventing voidness. And this agreement, even if incidental, is a proof because opposing it is following a path other than the believers', and because it is an agreement of the ummah like agreement on an opinion by agreement, just as that is a proof, so is this, because there is no difference in the indication of the proof. So the detailed view in annulment and the like, i.e., the case of parents with one of the spouses, is contrary to consensus on the absence of detail. What is said that the absence of detail is a matter of consensus is refuted because the absence of a statement is not a statement of absence, and here there is no statement of detail, but silence about it. This is refuted by the fact that the universality of the ruling absolutely, either annulment in all or not, is what both groups agreed upon, and detail contradicts it, for it invalidates the universality of each ruling. Making it multiple issues due to differing subjects is exiting from the dispute, for the dispute is when the issue is one. You have known that the opponent agreed with us when the issue was one and the third raised the agreed-upon; his dispute was only in some partial cases that it does not raise due to multiplicity in the issue, or raising unity. So making it multiple issues is not exiting from what he disputes. Rather, making it multiple issues is contrary to consensus because both groups agree on unity due to the unity of the commonality, which is the harm to one of them by the continuation of marriage. This refutes his statement: if the consensus were established. But it has passed that there are three opinions from the Companions on this. Then what is mentioned in our books is that the cause for separation in impotence and castration is the husband's inability to retain with kindness, so he must release with excellence, and this does not cover other defects. Also, defects in the wife can be avoided by the husband through divorce, so there is no harm to him by continuation of marriage and the judge not annulling it. So understand. As for the answer to the proof, namely that their agreement on denying the third opinion was conditional on its non-existence; when it occurred, the agreement on denial ceased, so there is no prohibition on introduction. It is rebutted by the found consensus, for the same could occur there, so it should not prevent introducing a contrary opinion. The excuse that it is permissible intellectually but was not considered a consensus as in al-Minhaj is weak because the distinction is arbitrary. So reflect. And he argued by the necessity of charging every group with error, i.e., if a third opinion were allowed, it would require charging every group with error because it opposes them, and that would be charging the entire ummah with error. The answer is that what is prevented is charging all with error on what they agreed upon, not absolutely. Here it is charging error in what they differed on, and this does not avail anything of the truth, for the proofs of preventing error are general. How is it prevented that the ummah falls into error? Perhaps the arguer intended a rational proof; otherwise its essence returns to the previous proof. It is refuted that charging error in every era is known only for one who opposes what was agreed upon, not what was differed upon. So ponder. The proponents of introduction said: Firstly, their disagreement on the matter is proof that it is a matter of ijtihad according to them; otherwise they would not have disagreed, so permissibility in it is necessary according to every statement, so there is no obstacle to introducing a third. But the ummah is false.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage from 'Fawatih al-Rahmut' comments on a rule in usul al-fiqh about consensus and the permissibility of introducing a third opinion in a matter where earlier scholars held only two opinions. The commentator begins by stating the rule as per the majority: if the scholars of an era do not go beyond two opinions on a matter, introducing a third is not allowed. This is affirmed by Imam Muhammad and Imam al-Shafi'i. However, some Hanafis restrict this to the Companions only, arguing it is permissible for later generations to introduce a third. The text then examines the logic: if the Followers (tabi'un) are limited to two opinions but the Companions had more, then the third opinion 'lives with its proof' and does not violate consensus. The commentator clarifies that this holds only if the earlier disagreement prevented later consensus. If the Followers remained silent on the matter, then a third opinion is permissible absolutely, according to some. Al-Amidi and al-Razi specify that if the third opinion contradicts what the two groups agreed upon, its introduction is prohibited. Several examples are given: the case of a buyer who has intercourse with a purchased slave-girl and then discovers a defect; scholars differed on whether he may return her without compensation or with compensation (arsh). Since it is agreed that he cannot return her without compensation, that opinion is prohibited. The reports from Companions on this are not confirmed, but from Followers there are three opinions, yet the prohibition stands on the agreed point. Another example is the grandfather's inheritance with the brother. The Companions agreed the grandfather inherits, but differed on the share; depriving him entirely is thus contrary to consensus. Similarly, the waiting period of a pregnant widow: all agreed to reject considering only the four months and ten days, so saying only months is prohibited. Then the passage introduces cases where a third opinion is allowed despite two existing opinions: detailed views in annulment due to defects (e.g., saying some defects cause annulment, others not) and the mother's share when parents and spouse are present. In these, the third does not overturn the agreed-upon universality but rather combines elements of the two existing opinions, so it is permissible. The commentator addresses objections: a later mujtahid may oppose earlier ones only if there is a prior speaker with that opinion; otherwise, the mere existence of two opinions constitutes an agreement on one of them (by preventing a vacuum). This agreement, though incidental, is a proof because opposing it is following a path other than the believers'. The detailed views in annulment and inheritance are argued to be contrary to consensus because the two groups agreed on a universal rule (e.g., all defects cause annulment or none do), and detail splits the issue, which amounts to introducing a third opinion that raises the agreed-upon. The passage ends with a lengthy rebuttal of the opponents' arguments, including the claim that disagreement itself proves the matter is open to ijtihad, thus allowing a third. The commentator dismisses this as false.

Key Terms
إحداث ثالث (ihdath thalith): Introducing a new (third) opinion in addition to two existing opinions from earlier scholars on the same matter.
اتفاق (itifaq): Agreement among scholars on a certain ruling, even if incidental; considered a proof.
إجماع (ijma'): Consensus of the mujtahids of the ummah; a primary source of law.
جامع (jami'): The commonality or unifying factor that makes a legal issue one single issue (e.g., harm from continuation of marriage in annulment defects).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of the pregnant widow's iddah, why is the opinion that her iddah is only four months and ten days prohibited?
A) Because the majority of Companions disagreed with that view.
B) Because all scholars agreed on negating the months, so that opinion contradicts the agreed universal.
C) Because the Quran explicitly forbids that view.
D) Because the Followers had only two opinions on the matter.

Key Takeaway
A third opinion is prohibited only when it contradicts the universal agreement of the two earlier groups; otherwise, it may be permissible.

Al-Ijma — Consensus
advanced
15 min
When Can a Later Mujtahid Contradict Consensus?
Debate on whether a later mujtahid can contradict earlier consensus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on when a later mujtahid may not contradict earlier consensus and apply the distinction between settled consensus and ongoing disagreement.

Core Idea
Once the ummah agrees on a single ruling, ijtihad is closed; no later mujtahid may contradict it.

This passage from 'Fawatih al-Rahamut' addresses a critical question in usul al-fiqh: can a later mujtahid initiate a new opinion that contradicts an earlier consensus (ijma')? The author explains that if earlier scholars agreed on one of two opinions, that agreement becomes binding. To oppose it is to follow a path other than the believers. The text also addresses objections and presents examples from inheritance law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State the central claim: a later mujtahid is prohibited from contradicting a consensus that has settled on a single ruling, even if that consensus arose from a prior disagreement on two opinions.
Look for the phrase 'إنما يصح مخالفة اللاحق السابق عند الأكثر بعد سبق قائل يقول بقوله اللاحق'.
2
Understand the condition of settlement
The prohibition applies only when scholars had two opinions and then agreed on one without moving beyond them. Accidental agreement (ittifaq sukkuti) still counts as binding because opposing it is 'following other than the path of believers'.
Key phrase: 'الاختلاف على قولين مع عدم التجاوز عنهما اتفاق على أحدهما'.
3
Examine the rebuttal to opponents
Opponents argue that disagreement itself proves the matter is open to ijtihad. The author responds that permissibility exists only before consensus is established; once consensus occurs, ijtihad is closed. Also, cases like the mother's inheritance show that apparent later contradictions either occurred before agreement or involved distinct issues.
Focus on the four responses (Firstly, Secondly, etc.) and the discussion of historical examples.
4
Apply the rule to the annulment example
The text uses the example of annulment (faskh) due to defects. Early scholars agreed on a universal ruling: either annul for all defects or none. A distinction between husband's and wife's defects contradicts that consensus and is therefore invalid.
Note the concept of 'kulliyya' (universality) and the argument that making multiple issues based on differing subject matter would be a departure from the dispute.

جمهورهم (للأم ثلث ما بقي فيهما أي في الزوج والزوجة من فرضهما (و) قال ابن عباس : ثلث الكل فيهما ( ثم) محمد بن سيرين) قال (إن الزوج) إذا كان مع الأم فللأم ثلث الكل كابن عباس ) أي كما قال هو (والزوجة)
