ابن سيرين وشريحاً كانا معاصرين للصحابة، وكانا يزاحمانهم في الفتوى، فيجوز افتاؤهما حين فتوى الصحابة ولا بعد فيه (و) قلنا (ثالثاً): لا نسلم أن الصحابة لم يتجاوزوا فيها عن قولين والعله مذهب صحابي اختاره تابعي) لكن لم يشتهر (و) قلنا :(رابعاً : كما قيل في حواشي ميرزاجان أنهما مسألتان متغايرتان حقيقة) لعدم وحدة المآل أو حكماً لعدم وحدة الجامع لإبداء كل من شريح وابن سيرين فارقاً (أقول) الصحابة (إنما أجمعوا على عدم الفصل بينهما أي الزوج والزوجة بناء على وحدة الجامع بعد الغاء الخصوصية، وهو التزوج فهو هل يرد الأم من ثلث الكل إلى ثلث الباقي أم لا فالمسألة متحدة حكماً هذا)
The third principle: consensus (ijma') is accepted in general, but it is denied in some partial cases, and raising what is agreed upon due to lack of commonality (al-jami') in his view. This is something else, so understand. If you say: The opposition of a later jurist to earlier jurisprudents (mujtahids) was widespread without disapproval, so this constitutes consensus. So how is initiating an opposing opinion prevented? I say: Opposition of a later jurist to an earlier one is only valid according to the majority after a previous proponent of the later jurist's opinion existed, even if not well-known. Argument: The disagreement on two opinions without moving beyond them, and agreement on one of them, as a way of preventing vacuum. This agreement, even if merely accidental, is a proof because opposing it is following a path other than that of the believers. Also, it is the agreement of the ummah, just like agreement on a single opinion—i.e., it is evidence, so too is this; no difference in the indication of the evidence. Thus, making a distinction in annulment (faskh) and similar matters—i.e., the issue of the parents of one of the spouses—contradicts consensus on not making a distinction. And what was said that not making a distinction is agreed upon is denied, because the absence of an opinion is not an opinion about absence. Here it is not an opinion about distinction but silence about it. This is rebutted by the fact that the universality of the ruling absolutely—either complete annulment or no annulment—is agreed upon by both groups, and the distinction contradict it. Making it multiple issues due to differing subject matter is a departure from the dispute, because the dispute is when the issue is unified. You have known that the opponent agreed with us when it is unified, and the third [opinion] raises what is agreed upon; his dispute was only about some partial cases that it does not raise the multiplicity of the issue or raises unification. Thus, making it multiple issues is not a departure from what is disputed; rather, it contradicts consensus because both groups agreed on unification due to the unity of the common factor (al-jami'), which is the harm to one of them by the continuation of marriage. This is a rebuttal to his saying: If consensus is established, but it has passed that the Companions had three opinions regarding it. Also, what is mentioned in our books is that the cause (illah) of separation in impotence (jabb) and 'innah is the husband's inability to retain [the wife] with kindness, so it is necessary to release her with goodness, which does not include what is other than these two. Also, defects in the wife can be dealt with by the husband through divorce, so he is not harmed by the continuation of marriage and the judge not annulling it. So understand. As for the response to the evidence—that their consensus on rejecting the third opinion was conditional on its non-existence, so when it occurred, the consensus on rejection ceased—so there is no prevention of innovation. This is refuted by experiential consensus (ijma' wujdani), because in that case the same can happen, so it should not prevent initiating a contrary opinion. The excuse that it is intellectually permissible to initiate a contrary opinion to experiential consensus, but it is not considered consensus, as in al-Minhaj, is weak because the distinction is arbitrary. So reflect. And he argued with the necessity of declaring each group erroneous, meaning if a third [opinion] were allowed, it would necessitate declaring each group erroneous because they are opposed to it. In this there is declaring the entire ummah erroneous. The response is that what is prohibited is declaring the whole erroneous in what they agreed upon, not absolute declaration of error. Here, the error is in what they differed upon, and this does not suffice against the truth at all. The proofs of the impossibility of declaring error are general; how? The impossibility of falling into error. Perhaps the intention of the one arguing is to use rational evidence; otherwise, the outcome returns to the previously mentioned evidence. It was rejected by the fact that declaring error in every age is only known for those who oppose in what is agreed upon, not in what is differed upon. So ponder. The proponents of innovation said: First, their disagreement in the issue is proof that it is open to ijtihad (ijtihadiyya) according to them, otherwise they would not have disagreed. Thus, permissibility of any opinion follows, so there is no prevention of initiating a third opinion for the ummah—and it is invalid due to the existence of permissibility. We say: This is also a permissibility of every opinion, but before their consensus is established on one of them. As for their consensus, there is no permissibility, just as if they differed in an issue then it was permissible, then they agreed—this nullifies the permissibility. And second, they said: If it were not permissible, it would not have occurred without disapproval, but it did occur and no one disapproved, otherwise it would have been transmitted and widespread among people. The Companions—i.e., the majority of them—said: For the mother, one-third of what remains from the spouse's share in both cases (i.e., with the husband or the wife). Ibn Abbas said: one-third of the whole in both cases. Then Muhammad ibn Sirin said: For the husband if the mother is with him, the mother gets one-third of the whole like Ibn Abbas; for the wife if the mother is with her, the mother gets one-third of what remains after the wife's share, like the Companions. Shuraih said the opposite: for the mother, one-third of what remains with the husband and one-third of the whole with the wife. We say: First, we do not acknowledge the lack of disapproval, and the necessity of transmission is denied. Even if the necessity of transmission is accepted, the necessity of widespread fame is denied, because there is no full motivation for transmission, so it is possible that the disapproval was transmitted by individuals and not well-known. Second, it is possible that the innovation of this opinion occurred before the Companions settled on two opinions, for Ibn Sirin and Shuraih were contemporaries of the Companions and would compete with them in giving fatwas, so it is possible that they gave fatwas while the Companions were giving fatwas, no problem. Third, we do not accept that the Companions did not go beyond two opinions in this; it might be the opinion of a Companion adopted by a tabi'i, but it did not become well-known. Fourth, as stated in the glosses of Mirzajan, they are two distinct issues in reality—due to the lack of unity of outcome (al-ma'al), or in ruling (hukman)—due to the lack of a common factor (al-jami'), because each of Shuraih and Ibn Sirin gave a different distinguishing factor. I say: The Companions only agreed on not distinguishing between the husband and the wife based on the unity of the common factor after disregarding particularities, which is marriage. So the issue is whether the mother is restricted from one-third of the whole to one-third of the remainder or not. Thus, the issue is unified in ruling. This.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul ruling about the permissibility of a later mujtahid contradicting earlier consensus. The author begins by affirming that ijma' is a valid source but can be challenged in specific partial cases. The core debate is whether a later scholar can initiate a new opinion that contradicts a previous consensus, provided there was no prior established opinion supporting it. The text clarifies that if scholars had two opinions without moving beyond them, agreement on one is considered a binding consensus, and to oppose it is to follow a path other than the believers, citing the Quran. The author then delves into an example regarding annulment (faskh) in marriage due to defects, where some made distinctions between defects of husband and wife. The text argues that the distinction contradicts the universality (kulliyya) of the ruling agreed upon by both groups—either complete annulment or no annulment. Therefore, any third opinion that splits the case is against consensus. The opponents, called 'proponents of innovation,' argue that the very existence of disagreement proves the matter is open to ijtihad, so a third opinion is permissible. The author responds that this permissibility is only before consensus is established; once consensus occurs, ijtihad is closed. They also present historical examples of the Companions, Ibn Abbas, Ibn Sirin, and Shuraih regarding a mother's share of inheritance to show that later opinions can arise without rejection, but the author counters that these cases either occurred before consensus, are distinct issues, or the disapproval was not transmitted. In summary, the passage teaches that once the ummah settles on a single ruling by consensus, no later mujtahid may contradict it; but if the matter remains subject to disagreement with no consensus, new opinions are permissible.

Key Terms
إجماع (ijmāʿ): Consensus of the mujtahids of the ummah on a religious ruling; in this passage it is discussed as binding once established on a single ruling.
إحداث (iḥdāth): Initiating a new opinion or ruling that contradicts a previous consensus or established view.
اتفاق سكوتي (ittifāq sukkūtī): Silent or accidental consensus—agreement on one opinion without explicit further discussion, after a period of disagreement.
كليّة الحكم (kulliyyat al-ḥukm): The universality of the ruling; the property of a ruling applying to all cases under a common category.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when can a later mujtahid not introduce a new opinion contradictory to earlier scholars?
A) When the earlier scholars had multiple opinions and did not settle on one.
B) When the earlier scholars agreed on a single ruling without moving beyond it.
C) Always, because ijtihad is never closed.
D) Only if the new opinion is based on a stronger evidence.

Key Takeaway
A later mujtahid cannot contradict a consensus that has settled on a single ruling, even if that consensus emerged from a prior disagreement.

Permissibility of New Evidence after Consensus
Ruling on introducing a new evidence or interpretation after consensus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that new evidence or interpretation may be introduced after consensus, provided it does not invalidate the consensus, and evaluate the opposing argument using the concept of 'path' (sabil).

Core Idea
Absence of opinion is not opinion of absence: new proofs after consensus are allowed unless they nullify the consensus itself.

This lesson examines a key usul question: once scholars have reached consensus on a specific evidence (dalil) or interpretation (ta'wil) for a ruling, can later scholars propose a different evidence or interpretation for the same ruling? The Hanafi school holds that it is permissible, as long as the new proposal does not undermine the agreed-upon basis. This principle keeps the door of ijtihad open while respecting the authority of consensus. Through this lesson, you will analyse the arguments presented in the text, including the rational proof and the rebuttal of the opponents' Quranic objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its exception
Read the first sentence: the majority allows introducing a new evidence/interpretation after consensus, unless it invalidates (yubtil) the consensus itself. Note the condition: the new evidence must not nullify the agreed-upon proof.
The Arabic says 'جاز احداث غيره' — it is permitted to introduce another.
2
Understand the first Hanafi proof: absence of opinion
The text argues that consensus on a particular evidence is merely the absence of stating other evidences; it is not a positive declaration that no other evidence exists. That is, 'عدم القول ليس قولاً بالعدم'. This rational principle is a cornerstone of the argument.
This distinguishes consensus on evidence from consensus on the ruling itself.
3
Analyse the opponent's first objection and the Hanafi response
The opponents cite Quran 4:115 about following a path other than the believers. The Hanafi response clarifies that the 'path' (sabil) refers to the ruling (madlul), not the evidence. Hence, new evidence supporting the same ruling is still the same path.
The text then quotes Quran 12:108 'قل هذه سبيلي' to support this interpretation.
4
Consider the second proof: historical practice
The text notes that later scholars (المتأخرون) never ceased to derive new strong evidences and interpretations for consensual rulings, and this was not criticized but praised. This tacit consensus (sukuti) reinforces the majority view.
This is a practical demonstration of the rule in action.
5
Assess the thamarah al-khilaf (practical impact)
If the opposing view were followed, ijtihad would effectively stop once consensus on a proof is reached. The Hanafi position allows continuous scholarly development while preserving the stability of established rulings.
This shows the real-world significance of the usul rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : إذا أجمع على دليل على حكم أو تأويل) في سمعي (جاز احداث غيره) من الدليل أو التأويل عند الأكثر إلا إذا أبطله أي أبطل هذا المحدث المجمع عليه خلافاً للبعض لنا أولاً) أحداث دليل أو تأويل كذلك اجتهاد لم يعارضه إجماع، لأن عدم القول ليس قولاً بالعدم والإجماع على دليل أو تأويل ليس إلا عدم القول بدليل أو تأويل آخر غيره لا أنه قول بعدمه بخلاف التفصيل) في نحو الفسخ بالعيوب فإنه ليس كالدليل) بل هو حكم معارض لكلية الحكمين اللذين لم يتجاوز عنهما (و) لنا (ثانياً) لو لم يجز احداث أحدهما لم يقع من غير نكير ووقع، إذ (المتأخرون لم يزالوا يستخرجون الأدلة والتأويلات) القوية لما أجمعوا عليه من الحكم (ولم ينكر عليهم، بل عد ذلك فضلاً) في حقهم المانعون (قالوا: أولاً) إحداث الدليل والتأويل إتباع غير سبيل المؤمنين لأنهم أجمعوا على دليل، وهذا غيره، وقد وقع الوعيد عليه قلنا : المتبادر منه خلاف سبيلهم وهذا ليس خلافه ومن ثمة يلزم بطلان ما لم يثبت بالإجماع لأنه غير سبيلهم أيضاً، إذ ليس لهم سبيل (أقول على أن لو منع كون الدليل سبيلاً) هو مراد في النص (بل) السبيل المراد هو المدلول لكان بسبيل) في الجواب (قال) الله تعالى: ﴿قُلْ هَذِهِ سَبِيلى ﴾ [يوسف: ۱۰۸] وأريد به المدلول فتدبر و) قالوا (ثانياً) : قال الله تعالى كُنتُمْ خَيْرَ أُمَّةٍ أُخْرِجَتْ لِلنَّاسِ تَأْمُرُونَ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ ﴾ [آل عمران: ١١٠] (أي بكل معروف فما
Issue: If there is consensus on an evidence for a ruling or an interpretation in a transmitted matter, it is permitted to introduce another evidence or interpretation according to the majority, unless it invalidates—i.e., this innovator nullifies what is agreed upon—contrary to some. Our proof: First, introducing an evidence or interpretation is an ijtihad that is not opposed by consensus, because the absence of an opinion is not an opinion about absence. Consensus on an evidence or interpretation is only the absence of an opinion with another evidence or interpretation other than it, not an opinion about its non-existence, unlike making a distinction in annulment due to defects, for it is not like evidence; rather it is a ruling opposing the universality of the two rulings that were not departed from. Second, if it were not permissible to introduce either, it would not have occurred without disapproval, but it did occur, for the later scholars never ceased to extract strong evidences and interpretations for what they had consensus on, and they were not disapproved but rather counted as virtuous for them. The opponents said: First, introducing evidence and interpretation is following a path other than the believers, because they agreed on an evidence, and this is different from it, and the threat [of punishment] is upon that. We say: The apparent meaning is contrary to their path, and this is not contrary to it. Hence, what is not established by consensus would be invalid because it is also not their path, as they have no path. I say: Even if one prevented the evidence from being a path—which is intended in the text—rather the intended path is the indicated meaning (al-madlul), it would suffice in the response. Allah said: "Say: This is my path" (12:108), and what is meant is the indicated meaning, so reflect. And they said second: Allah said: "You are the best community brought forth for mankind, enjoining what is right" (3:110), i.e., every good, so what...
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage presents a usul ruling: if the scholars have reached consensus on a particular evidence (dalil) or interpretation (ta'wil) for a ruling, it is permissible for later scholars to produce new evidences or interpretations, according to the majority of usul scholars. The exception is if the new evidence directly invalidates the consensus itself—that is, if it would nullify the agreed-upon evidence, then it is not allowed. The author provides two proofs for the majority opinion. First, introducing a new evidence or interpretation is an ijtihad that is not opposed by consensus, because consensus on a particular evidence is merely the absence of another evidence—it is not an assertion that no other evidence exists. This is unlike the earlier example of making a distinction in annulment due to defects, which was a substantive ruling that contradicted a universal consensus. Second, historical practice shows that later scholars continuously extracted new evidences and interpretations for rulings that were already established by consensus, and this was not condemned but praised. The opponents argue that introducing a new evidence contradicts consensus and is thus following a path other than the believers, citing the Quranic threat. The author rebuts that the 'path' refers to the meaning (madlul) of the evidence, not the evidence itself. The Quranic verse 'Qul hadhihi sabili' means 'this is my path' referring to the message, not the specific evidence. Therefore, as long as the new evidence supports the same ruling (the path), it is permissible. The second opponent proof from 'You are the best community...' commanding every good is also addressed, but the text cuts off. In summary, the ruling permits new evidence and interpretations after consensus as long as they do not undermine the agreed-upon ruling itself; this is a practical principle that allows continuous ijtihad within the framework of established legal tradition.

Key Terms
دليل (dalīl): An evidence or proof used to derive a legal ruling; in this context, it refers to a specific textual or rational proof that scholars agree upon.
تأويل (ta'wīl): An interpretation of a text that yields a ruling; here it is considered alongside dalil as something that can be new after consensus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is never permissible after consensus.
B) It is permissible only if it is based on a stronger evidence.
C) It is permissible as long as it does not invalidate the consensus.
D) It is permissible only for those who are not part of the consensus.

Key Takeaway
New evidence after consensus is permissible as long as it supports the same ruling and does not nullify the consensus; this keeps ijtihad alive while respecting scholarly agreement.

The Permissibility of Introducing New Evidence After Consensus: A Hanafi Usul Dispute
Usul dispute on introducing new evidence after consensus on one opinion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi argument for allowing new evidence after consensus on one opinion and distinguish it from the opposing view.

Core Idea
After consensus on one opinion, new evidence for the other may still be valid if it predates consensus or was not widely disapproved.

This lesson explores a key usul al-fiqh dispute: once the Companions have reached consensus on one of two opinions, can later scholars introduce new evidence supporting the other opinion? The passage presents the Hanafi position that it is permissible under certain conditions, using the famous inheritance case of the mother's share with a spouse as an example. You will analyze the competing arguments and understand the Hanafi rationale for preserving the vitality of ijtihad even after apparent consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What exactly is being disputed? Understand that the debate is about the permissibility of introducing new evidence or interpretation after the Companions have agreed on one of two conflicting opinions.
The issue is not consensus itself, but the scope of its finality.
2
Examine the opposing view and its proof
The opposing scholars argue that if it were permissible to introduce new evidence after consensus, it would not have occurred without disapproval; since it did occur without known disapproval, it must be impermissible. Identify this empirical argument.
This is a foundational critique of the Hanafi position.
3
State the Hanafi position and its evidence
The Hanafis permit introducing new evidence, citing the actions of Successors like Ibn Sirin and Shuraih. Note that the Hanafi position does not reject consensus but allows exceptions.
The Hanafis rely on the possibility that disapproval was not transmitted.
4
Analyze the four Hanafi responses
Work through each response: (1) disapproval may have been unrecorded, (2) the new opinion may predate the consensus, (3) the Companions may have had more than two opinions, (4) the cases may be legally distinct. Understand how each rebuts the opposing proof.
These responses show the Hanafi method of preserving consensus while allowing new ijtihad.
5
Apply the dispute to the inheritance example
See how the theoretical dispute manifests in a real case: the mother's share with a spouse. The Companions majority gave 1/3 of the remainder; Ibn Sirin and Shuraih differed. Evaluate how the Hanafi responses excuse their rulings.
This concrete example clarifies the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لوجود التسويغ (قلنا : كذلك أنه تسويغ كل قول (لكن قبل تقرر إجماعهم) على أحدهما وأما إجماعهم فلا تسويغ كما لو اختلفوا في مسألة فكان تسويغاً ثم أجمعوا) فيبطل التسويغ (و) قالوا (ثانياً) لو لم يجز لم يقع من غير نكير ووقع ولم ينكر من أحد (وإلا نقل) واشتهر بين الناس (قال الصحابة) أي جمهورهم (للأم ثلث ما بقي فيهما أي في الزوج والزوجة من فرضهما (و) قال ابن عباس : ثلث الكل فيهما ( ثم) محمد بن سيرين) قال (إن الزوج) إذا كان مع الأم فللأم ثلث الكل كابن عباس ) أي كما قال هو (والزوجة) إذا كانت معها فلها ثلث ما بقي بعد فرض الزوجة ( كالصحابة) أي كقولهم (و) قال شريح بالعكس) أي للأم ثلث ما بقي مع الزوج وثلث الكل مع الزوجة قلنا : (أولاً) لا نسلم عدم النكير و(لزوم النقل) إياه (ممنوع ولو سلم لزوم النقل (فلزوم الشهرة ممنوع إذ لا توفر للدواعي) على النقل فيجوز أن يكون النكير منقولاً بآحاد ولم يشتهر (و) قلنا :(ثانياً : يجوز أن يكون الإحداث) لهذا القول (قبل استقرار الصحابة على قولين فإن ابن سيرين وشريحاً كانا معاصرين للصحابة، وكانا يزاحمانهم في الفتوى، فيجوز افتاؤهما حين فتوى الصحابة ولا بعد فيه (و) قلنا (ثالثاً): لا نسلم أن الصحابة لم يتجاوزوا فيها عن قولين والعله مذهب صحابي اختاره تابعي) لكن لم يشتهر (و) قلنا :(رابعاً : كما قيل في حواشي ميرزاجان أنهما مسألتان متغايرتان حقيقة) لعدم وحدة المآل أو حكماً لعدم وحدة الجامع لإبداء كل من شريح وابن سيرين فارقاً (أقول) الصحابة (إنما أجمعوا على عدم الفصل بينهما أي الزوج والزوجة بناء على وحدة الجامع بعد الغاء الخصوصية، وهو التزوج فهو هل يرد الأم من ثلث الكل إلى ثلث الباقي أم لا فالمسألة متحدة حكماً هذا).
Due to the existence of permission (We said: likewise that it is permission for every saying (but before the establishment of their consensus) on one of them, and as for their consensus, there is no permission, like if they differed in a matter and there was permission, then they reached consensus, so the permission is nullified. (And) they said (secondly): If it were not permissible, it would not have occurred without disapproval, and it occurred and no one disapproved (otherwise it would have been transmitted) and become famous among people. (The Companions said) i.e., their majority, (for the mother one-third of what remains in both, i.e., in the case of the husband and wife from their fixed shares. (And) Ibn Abbas said: one-third of the whole in both. (Then) Muhammad ibn Sirin) said (Indeed the husband) if he is with the mother, then for the mother one-third of the whole, like Ibn Abbas, i.e., as he said. (And the wife) if she is with her, then for her one-third of what remains after the wife's fixed share, like the Companions, i.e., like their statement. (And) Shuraih said the opposite, i.e., for the mother one-third of what remains with the husband and one-third of the whole with the wife. We said: (Firstly) we do not accept the lack of disapproval and (the necessity of transmission) of it is denied, and even if transmission is granted, (the necessity of fame is denied, for there is no complete motivation for transmission, so it is possible that the disapproval was transmitted as isolated reports and did not become famous. (And) We said: (Secondly) it is possible that the innovation (of this statement) was before the Companions settled on two opinions, for Ibn Sirin and Shuraih were contemporaries of the Companions and they competed with them in issuing fatwas, so it is possible that they issued fatwas at the time of the Companions' fatwas and there is no objection. (And) We said: (Thirdly) we do not accept that the Companions did not go beyond two opinions in this matter, and it may be the madhhab of a Companion chosen by a Successor, but it did not become famous. (And) We said: (Fourthly) as was said in the margins of Mirzajan, that they are two different cases in reality, due to the lack of unity of outcome, or legally due to the lack of unity of the common ground, because each of Shuraih and Ibn Sirin introduced a distinction. (I say) The Companions indeed reached consensus on not distinguishing between them, i.e., the husband and wife, based on the unity of the common ground after removing particularity, which is marriage. So the question is: does the mother revert from one-third of the whole to one-third of the remainder or not? So the case is legally unified. This.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage presents a usul dispute about whether after the Companions reach consensus on one of two opinions (e.g., in the mother's inheritance with a spouse), it is permissible for later scholars to introduce new evidence or interpretation that supports the other opinion. The author (as commentator) first states the objection: if it were permissible, then consensus on one opinion would not render the other opinion invalid, which contradicts the nature of consensus. Then the objectors argue that if it were not permissible, it would not have occurred without disapproval, and since it did occur without known disapproval, it must be permissible. The author responds with four points: (1) disapproval may have occurred but not been transmitted widely; (2) the new opinion may have been introduced before the Companions' consensus was settled; (3) the Companions may have had more than two opinions; (4) the two cases (husband vs wife) may be legally distinct. The author then asserts that the Companions did reach consensus on not distinguishing between husband and wife, considering marriage as the common ground. This is a Hanafi position allowing new evidence as long as it does not contradict a definitive consensus.

Key Terms
تسويغ (taswigh): Permission or allowance; here it refers to allowing each of two differing opinions to be acted upon before consensus.
إجماع (ijma'): Consensus of the mujtahidun of the Ummah on a Shar'i ruling.
دليل (dalil): Evidence or proof from the Shari'ah, such as a verse, hadith, or logical reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did Ibn Sirin rule for the mother's share when the husband is present?
A) One-third of the whole estate
B) One-third of the remainder after the husband's share
C) One-sixth of the whole estate
D) One-eighth of the estate

Key Takeaway
Hanafi usul permits introducing new evidence supporting a non-consensus opinion if it can be shown that the evidence predated the consensus, or that the cases are legally distinct.

Introducing New Evidence After Consensus on a Proof
Dispute on whether new evidence or interpretation can be introduced after consensus on a specific proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on introducing new evidence after consensus and evaluate the opposing argument using Quranic verses.

Core Idea
Consensus on a proof does not preclude later proofs; absence of statement is not statement of absence.

A common question in usul al-fiqh is whether, after the mujtahidun have agreed on a specific proof or interpretation for a ruling, later scholars can introduce different evidence for the same ruling. The Hanafi school holds that it is permissible, as long as the new evidence does not invalidate the consensus. This lesson examines the proofs for this position and the objections raised by other scholars.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the exact question: Can a new proof or interpretation be introduced after consensus on a specific proof/interpretation?
2
Identify Hanafi position and first proof
The Hanafi school says yes. Their first proof: 'Absence of statement is not statement of absence' – consensus on one proof does not mean denial of others.
3
Identify second proof
Second proof: Later scholars have always extracted new proofs and were not disapproved, showing permissibility.
4
State first opposing argument and Hanafi response
Opponent: Quran 4:115 forbids following 'other than the path of believers'. Hanafi response: 'path' refers to the indicated meaning (madlul), not the proof itself.
5
State second opposing argument and Hanafi response
Opponent: Quran 3:110 says the Ummah commands the right; a new proof not commanded is not right. Hanafi response: The same verse also says 'forbidding wrong', and the new proof is not forbidden; also, the generality of 'right' is not absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اجتهاد لم يعارضه إجماع، لأن عدم القول ليس قولاً بالعدم والإجماع على دليل أو تأويل ليس إلا عدم القول بدليل أو تأويل آخر غيره لا أنه قول بعدمه بخلاف التفصيل) في نحو الفسخ بالعيوب فإنه ليس كالدليل) بل هو حكم معارض لكلية الحكمين اللذين لم يتجاوز عنهما (و) لنا (ثانياً) لو لم يجز احداث أحدهما لم يقع من غير نكير ووقع، إذ (المتأخرون لم يزالوا يستخرجون الأدلة والتأويلات) القوية لما أجمعوا عليه من الحكم (ولم ينكر عليهم، بل عد ذلك فضلاً) في حقهم المانعون (قالوا: أولاً) إحداث الدليل والتأويل إتباع غير سبيل المؤمنين لأنهم أجمعوا على دليل، وهذا غيره، وقد وقع الوعيد عليه قلنا : المتبادر منه خلاف سبيلهم وهذا ليس خلافه ومن ثمة يلزم بطلان ما لم يثبت بالإجماع لأنه غير سبيلهم أيضاً، إذ ليس لهم سبيل (أقول على أن لو منع كون الدليل سبيلاً) هو مراد في النص (بل) السبيل المراد هو المدلول لكان بسبيل) في الجواب (قال) الله تعالى: ﴿قُلْ هَذِهِ سَبِيلى ﴾ [يوسف: ۱۰۸] وأريد به المدلول فتدبر و) قالوا (ثانياً) : قال الله تعالى كُنتُمْ خَيْرَ أُمَّةٍ أُخْرِجَتْ لِلنَّاسِ تَأْمُرُونَ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ ﴾ [آل عمران: ١١٠] (أي بكل معروف فما ليس بمأمور ليس بمعروف فالدليل المحدث ليس بمعروف، فيكون باطلاً (قلنا : عورض بقوله تعالى ﴿وَتَنْهَوْنَ عَنِ الْمُنكَرِ ﴾ أي عن كل منكر فما ليس بمنهي ليس بمنكر وهذا الدليل ليس بمنهي فيجوز إحداثه أقول) على أن تجويز الإحداث أمر فهو مأمور به، لأنا أمرنا بطلب ما لم نعلم فإن طلب العلم مأمور به فيكون معروفاً (والتفصيل) فيما أجمع على عدم التفصيل (إنما يكون بعد العلم بعدم التفصيل فيكون مبطلاً لما علم، فلا يكون مأموراً بل منهياً، وقد يمنع عموم المعروف فإنه من البين أنه لم يؤمر بكل معروف بل أكثر الوقائع سكوت عنها، وقد يستدل بهذه الآية على حجية الإجماع، فإن الخيرية والأمر بكل معروف والنهي عن كل منكر يوجب أن لا يبقى معروف ولا منهي يؤمر به أو ينهي عنه فيكون ما أجمعوا عليه حقاً، واعترض عليه بأن الخيرية لا تقتضي إصابة الحق والحكم المستخرج، وإن كان خطأ ليس منهياً عنه وأن لا عموم للمعروف والمنكر. فقرر بأن المتبادر من الآية المدح، بأن أمرهم ليس إلا بالمعروف ونهيهم ليس إلا عن المنكر، فوجب أن يكون ما أجمعوا عليه معروفاً وخلافه منكراً والخطأ بما هو خطأ لا يصلح المدح على الأمر به، فيكون صواباً عنه الله هذا تقرير حسن، لكن يرد عليه أن هذا التأويل مظنون لا يثبت به حجة قاطعة، وأيضاً الخطاب الشفاهي لا يتناول إلا الموجودين زمن الخطاب فلا يجري في إجماع حدث بعد الصحابة إلا بدلالة النص فتأمل فيه .
Issue: When consensus has been reached on a proof for a ruling or an interpretation (in my hearing) it is permissible to introduce another (proof or interpretation) according to the majority, unless it invalidates, i.e., this new thing invalidates what is agreed upon, contrary to some. For us: (firstly) introducing a proof or interpretation in this way is ijtihad that does not contradict consensus, because the absence of a statement is not a statement of absence, and consensus on a proof or interpretation is only the absence of a statement on another proof or interpretation other than it, not that it is a statement of its absence, unlike detailing (like in the case of annulment due to defects) for that is not like a proof, but rather a ruling that contradicts the generality of the two rulings that were not exceeded. (And) for us (secondly): if it were not permissible to introduce either, it would not have occurred without disapproval, and it did occur, for later scholars have always extracted strong proofs and interpretations for what they have consensus on the ruling, and they were not disapproved, but rather that was considered a merit for them. The objectors said: (firstly) introducing a proof and interpretation is following other than the path of the believers, because they reached consensus on a proof, and this is different, and the threat has been made against it. We said: what is understood from it is contrary to their path, and this is not contrary to it, and from this it would follow that what is not established by consensus is invalid because it is also not their path, since they have no path. (I say: If one prevents that the proof is a path, that is intended in the text, but the intended path is the indicated meaning, then it would be a path in the response. Allah said: “Say: This is my path” [Yusuf 108] and what is intended is the indicated meaning, so reflect. And) they said (secondly): Allah said: “You are the best community brought forth for mankind, commanding what is right” [Al-Imran 110], i.e., every right, so what is not commanded is not right, so the new proof is not right, thus it is invalid. We said: It is countered by His saying: “and forbidding what is wrong” i.e., every wrong, so what is not forbidden is not wrong, and this proof is not forbidden, so it is permissible to introduce it. I say: that permitting introduction is a command, so it is commanded, for we are commanded to seek what we do not know, for seeking knowledge is commanded, so it is right. (And detailing) in what is agreed upon not to detail only occurs after knowing that there is no detail, so it invalidates what is known, thus it is not commanded but forbidden, and the generality of ‘right’ may be prevented, for it is clear that not every right is commanded, but most incidents are passed over in silence. This verse may also be used as evidence for the authority of consensus, for the goodness and commanding every right and forbidding every wrong require that there remains no right or wrong that is commanded or forbidden, so what they agree upon is truth. It was objected that goodness does not necessitate hitting the truth, and the derived ruling, even if mistaken, is not forbidden, and that there is no generality for right and wrong. So it was established that what is understood from the verse is praise: that their command is only of right and their prohibition only of wrong, so it must be that what they agree upon is right and its opposite is wrong, and error as error is not suitable for praise for commanding it, so it is correct. This is a good explanation, but it is objected that this interpretation is speculative and does not establish a definitive proof. Also, the oral address only includes those present at the time of address, so it does not apply to consensus that occurs after the Companions except by indication of the text, so ponder.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage addresses a second usul dispute: after the mujtahidun have reached consensus on a specific proof or interpretation for a ruling, is it permissible for later scholars to introduce a different proof or interpretation for the same ruling? The majority (including Hanafis) say yes, as long as the new proof does not invalidate the consensus. The two main proofs for the permissibility are: (1) introducing a new proof is ijtihad that does not contradict the consensus because the consensus was not a statement denying other proofs; (2) later scholars have always done so without disapproval, which indicates permissibility. The objectors raise two Quranic arguments: (a) that to introduce a new proof is to follow 'other than the path of the believers' (Quran 4:115), which is forbidden; (b) that the Ummah is described as 'commanding the right' (Quran 3:110), so anything not commanded is not right, and a new proof not part of the original consensus is not commanded, so it is not right. The author counters each: (a) the 'path' refers to the indicated meaning, not the proof itself; (b) the same verse also says 'forbidding the wrong', and the new proof is not forbidden, so it is permissible. The author also notes that the verse can be used to support the authority of consensus itself, but this is not definitive. This reflects the Hanafi approach of allowing new ijtihad as long as it does not contradict a definitive consensus.

Key Terms
تأويل (ta'wil): Interpretation of a textual evidence to derive a ruling, especially when the text is not explicit.
سبيل المؤمنين (sabil al-mu'minin): The path of the believers, referring to the consensus of the Ummah.
معروف (ma'ruf): That which is recognized as good and commanded by Shari'ah.
إجماع على دليل (ijma' 'ala dalil): Consensus on a specific proof or interpretation for a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, what is the primary reason that introducing a new proof after consensus on a specific proof does not invalidate the consensus?
A) Because consensus is not binding.
B) Because the new proof is also part of the consensus.
C) Because consensus on a proof is not a statement denying other proofs, so new proofs do not contradict it.
D) Because the new proof is stronger.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, consensus on a specific proof does not preclude later scholars from citing different evidences for the same ruling, as long as the ruling remains unchanged.

Al-Ijma — Consensus
intermediate
6 min
Consensus Must Be Based on a Shari' Source
Establishing the necessity of a Shari' basis for valid consensus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that valid consensus must be based on a Shari' source and evaluate the two rational arguments (dawr and customary impossibility) that support it.

Core Idea
Consensus without a Shari' basis is either circular or practically impossible – a rational proof for the necessity of evidence.

This lesson explores a fundamental principle in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: that consensus (ijma) is not an independent source but must be anchored in prior Shari' evidence. The passage presents two logical arguments – circularity (dawr) and customary impossibility – to refute the idea that agreement alone can generate authority. You will learn how the Hanafi school defends the necessity of a mustanad shar'i and why divine inspiration (ilm daruri) cannot substitute for it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
The text opens with the ruling: 'No consensus except based on a Shari' source' (لا إجماع إلا عن مستند شرعي). Recognize that this is the chosen view (al-mukhtar) and that some scholars disagree.
The opposite view is that consensus is authoritative regardless of evidentiary basis.
2
Analyze the first argument: circularity (dawr)
If consensus were based only on agreement, each mujtahid's ruling would depend on everyone else's agreement, and that agreement would depend on each ruling – a clear circularity. Hence, an external basis is needed.
The text addresses a counterargument that consensus itself ensures correctness, but the Hanafi response is that without evidence, the participants are not truly mujtahidun.
3
Analyze the second argument: customary impossibility
It is practically impossible for all mujtahidun to independently agree on a ruling without a common motivating factor (the Shari' proof). The analogy given is agreement on a specific food – without a shared reason, such agreement is impossible.
The text anticipates an objection: divine inspiration could cause such agreement. But it responds that if inspiration is itself a proof, then consensus is redundant; if not, it cannot be relied upon.
4
Evaluate the objection from necessary knowledge (ilm daruri)
Some might argue that Allah can create necessary knowledge (ilm daruri) in the hearts of all mujtahidun, leading to agreement. The Hanafi response is twofold: either this knowledge is a proof, in which case agreement is irrelevant, or it is not a proof, in which case a Shari' source is still required.
The text notes that the inspiration of a single saint is not a Shari' proof, so even if it occurs, it does not constitute a valid basis for consensus.
5
Recognize the dispute and its implications
The opposing view holds that the hadith 'My Ummah will not agree upon error' guarantees infallibility without requiring a prior basis. The Hanafi school insists that the hadith itself is a Shari' source, but it does not eliminate the need for evidence – rather, it confirms that when the Ummah agrees on a ruling that is derived from evidence, they are protected from error.
The practical implication: a claim of consensus must always be verified by identifying the underlying evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: لا إجماع إلا عن مستند شرعي على المختار) خلافاً للبعض (لنا أولاً: الفتوى بلا دليل شرعي حرام وإذا ليس ههنا دليل غير الاتفاق فقول : كل يتوقف على قول الكل وبالعكس وهو ظاهر فلزم الدور ( فتدبر) وقد يقال : إنما يلزم من الفتوى لا عن دليل احتمال الخطأ لا وقوعه وأيضاً لا يلزم من حرمة الإفتاء من غير دليل الخطأ في الحكم المفتى به، بل للإجماع تأثير في الإصابة، وأجيب بأن حجية الإجماع ليست إلا لأنه اتفاق المجتهدين من هم مجتهدون، وإذ كان الفتوى لا عن دليل واجتهاد فليس هو قول المجتهد من حيث هو مجتهد، وفيه نحو من الخلفاء فإن الخصم لا يسلم أن الحجية لذلك، بل لأن اتفاق المفتين هذه الأمة المرحومة لا يكون على خطأ سواء قالوا بالاجتهاد أم لا، تكريماً لهذه الأمة، فالأولى أن يقال : إن الفتوى لا عن دليل لما كان حراماً لا يجترى عليه عدل، ولو اجترأ صار فاسقاً، فلم يبقى أهلاً للإجماع ولا للتكريم فلا اعتداد بقولهم فافهم (و) لنا (ثانياً يستحيل عادة اتفاق الكل لا لداع فلا يوجد اتفاق من غير دليل (كعلي طعام) أي كما يستحيل عادة اتفاق الكل على طعام واحد لعدم الداعي وتجويز العلم الضروري أي يحدث العلم الضروري فيقع الاتفاق عليه أو توفيقهم للصواب) بأن يقع في قلبهم ما هو صوابا (أبعد) فإن قلت : خلق العلم الضروري ليس ببعيد. فإن الأولياء الكرام يلهمون بأحكام وحقائق ومعارف بحيث لا يتطرق إليه الخطأ أصلاً ، قلت : لا شك في حدوث العلم الضروري فيهم ولا ينكره إلا سفيه، لكنه إن كان حجة فلا دخل للاتفاق والاجتماع، وإلا فلا بد من دليل شرعي إلا أن يقال إن حجيته مشروطة بالإجماع وإلهام الواحد لا يكون حجة وتأمل فيه وارتقب كلاماً مستوفى، ولئن
Issue: No consensus except based on a Shari' basis according to the chosen view, contrary to some. For us (firstly): Issuing a fatwa without a Shari' proof is forbidden. And since there is no proof here other than agreement, then the statement of each depends on the statement of all and vice versa, which is clear, so circularity is required. So ponder. It may be said: The harm from a fatwa without proof is only the possibility of error, not its occurrence. Also, the prohibition of issuing a fatwa without proof does not entail error in the ruling itself; rather consensus has an effect in hitting the truth. And it was answered that the authority of consensus is only because it is the agreement of the mujtahidun who are truly mujtahidun. And if the fatwa is without proof and ijtihad, then it is not the statement of a mujtahid as a mujtahid. And there is something of circularity here, for the opponent does not accept that authority is for that reason, but rather because the agreement of the muftis of this merciful Ummah cannot be upon error, whether they spoke by ijtihad or not, as an honor to this Ummah. So it is better to say: Issuing a fatwa without proof is forbidden, so a just person would not dare to do it, and if he dares, he becomes a sinner, so he is no longer qualified for consensus nor for honor, so his statement is not considered. So understand. (And) for us (secondly): It is customarily impossible for all to agree without a motive, so there is no agreement without a proof, just as it is customarily impossible for all to agree on one food without a motive. And allowing necessary knowledge (i.e., that necessary knowledge occurs so that agreement happens) or their being guided to the truth (by having what is correct placed in their hearts) is more improbable. If you say: The creation of necessary knowledge is not improbable, for the noble saints are inspired with rulings, truths, and knowledge such that error does not approach them at all. I say: There is no doubt about the occurrence of necessary knowledge in them, and only a fool denies it, but if it is a proof, then agreement and assembly have no role, and if not, then a Shari' proof is necessary, unless it be said that its authority is conditioned upon consensus, and the inspiration of one is not a proof. Ponder and expect a comprehensive discussion.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage establishes the principle that valid consensus must be based on a Shari' source (such as the Quran, Sunnah, or valid ijtihad). This is the chosen view (al-mukhtar). Two arguments are given: (1) Circularity: If consensus itself were the only basis, then each mujtahid's ruling would depend on the agreement of all, and the agreement of all would depend on each ruling, creating circularity. (2) Customary impossibility: It is impossible for all mujtahidun to independently agree on a ruling without some common motivator (the Shari' proof). The text also addresses an objection: that necessary knowledge (divine inspiration) could produce agreement without a Shari' proof. But the response is that if such inspiration is a proof, then the agreement (consensus) is superfluous; if it is not a proof, then it cannot be relied upon. Therefore, a Shari' proof is necessary. This reflects the Hanafi insistence that ijma' is based on evidence, not mere agreement.

Key Terms
مستند شرعي (mustanad shar'i): A Shari' source or proof that provides the basis for the ruling agreed upon in consensus.
دور (dawr): Circular reasoning; here it refers to the logical problem if consensus is based solely on agreement without external evidence.
علم ضروري (ilm daruri): Necessary knowledge that occurs in the heart without acquisition, often through inspiration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is a Shari' basis necessary for a valid consensus?
A) Because without it, consensus would be based on circular reasoning or be practically impossible.
B) Because the Quran explicitly requires it.
C) Because consensus is always infallible.
D) Because consensus is only for matters of worship.

Key Takeaway
Consensus in the Hanafi school is not an independent source but a confirmation of a ruling already supported by evidence.

Al-Ijma — Consensus
intermediate

Ijma Requires a Shar'i Basis (Mustanad)
Establishing that ijma requires a shar'i evidence and addressing objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that ijma requires a shar'i basis (mustanad) and evaluate the rational arguments for and against this condition.

Core Idea
Ijma without a shar'i basis is invalid — agreement alone cannot create a ruling from nothing.

This lesson examines a fundamental Hanafi usul principle: that ijma (consensus) is not valid unless it is grounded in a shar'i evidence (mustanad). The passage presents rational arguments, responds to opposing views, and explains the unique benefit of ijma in raising a ruling from speculative (zanni) to certain (qat'i). Understanding this principle is crucial for grasping how the Hanafi school views the authority of consensus relative to other sources.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi rule
Read the opening sentence: 'There is no ijma except upon a shar'i basis, according to the chosen position.' This is the rule you must internalize.
The phrase 'ala al-mukhtar' indicates this is the relied-upon position.
2
Identify the rational proof (dalil aqli)
The first proof: issuing a fatwa without evidence is forbidden (haram). If ijma had no basis, its ruling would be a fatwa without evidence, which is prohibited. Also, without evidence, a circular dependency arises.
The argument uses the principle 'al-fatwa bi-la dalil haram'.
3
Consider the opposing view
Some scholars argue that ijma is protected from error by divine grace, so no external basis is needed. Their objection: the prohibition of fatwa without proof does not necessitate error in the ruling itself.
This view attributes authority to the mere fact of agreement of the Ummah.
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The response refines: a person who issues a fatwa without evidence becomes sinful and loses eligibility to be a mujtahid, thus his opinion cannot count towards ijma. Furthermore, it is rationally impossible for all mujtahids to agree on a ruling without a common cause.
The second proof uses the impossibility of universal agreement without a motive (like all people agreeing on one food without reason).
5
Explain the benefit of ijma
Even when a basis is required, ijma adds value by converting a speculative (zanni) basis into a certain (qat'i) ruling. This is the key benefit: certainty.
Some Hanafis even hold that the basis itself must be speculative, otherwise ijma would be redundant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: لا إجماع إلا عن مستند شرعي على المختار خلافاً للبعض لنا أولاً: الفتوى بلا دليل شرعي حرام وإذا ليس ههنا دليل غير الاتفاق فقول : كل يتوقف على قول الكل وبالعكس وهو ظاهر فلزم الدور فتدبر وقد يقال : إنما يلزم من الفتوى لا عن دليل احتمال الخطأ لا وقوعه وأيضاً لا يلزم من حرمة الإفتاء من غير دليل الخطأ في الحكم المفتى به، بل للإجماع تأثير في الإصابة، وأجيب بأن حجية الإجماع ليست إلا لأنه اتفاق المجتهدين من هم مجتهدون، وإذ كان الفتوى لا عن دليل واجتهاد فليس هو قول المجتهد من حيث هو مجتهد، وفيه نحو من الخلفاء فإن الخصم لا يسلم أن الحجية لذلك، بل لأن اتفاق المفتين هذه الأمة المرحومة لا يكون على خطأ سواء قالوا بالاجتهاد أم لا، تكريماً لهذه الأمة، فالأولى أن يقال : إن الفتوى لا عن دليل لما كان حراماً لا يجترى عليه عدل، ولو اجترأ صار فاسقاً، فلم يبقى أهلاً للإجماع ولا للتكريم فلا اعتداد بقولهم فافهم و لنا ثانياً يستحيل عادة اتفاق الكل لا لداع فلا يوجد اتفاق من غير دليل كعلي طعام أي كما يستحيل عادة اتفاق الكل على طعام واحد لعدم الداعي وتجويز العلم الضروري أي يحدث العلم الضروري فيقع الاتفاق عليه أو توفيقهم للصواب بأن يقع في قلبهم ما هو صوابا أبعد فإن قلت : خلق العلم الضروري ليس ببعيد. فإن الأولياء الكرام يلهمون بأحكام وحقائق ومعارف بحيث لا يتطرق إليه الخطأ أصلاً ، قلت : لا شك في حدوث العلم الضروري فيهم ولا ينكره إلا سفيه، لكنه إن كان حجة فلا دخل للاتفاق والاجتماع، وإلا فلا بد من دليل شرعي إلا أن يقال إن حجيته مشروطة بالإجماع وإلهام الواحد لا يكون حجة وتأمل فيه وارتقب كلاماً مستوفى ولئن ساعدنا التوفيق فسنوفي القول فيه إن شاء الله تعالى. مجيز والإجماع من غير مستند قالوا: لو لزم المستند فما فائدة الإجماع إذ يكفي المستند حينئذ قلنا الفائدة القطعية للحكم بعدما كان ظنياً، فإنه يجوز أن يكون المستند ظنياً ومن ههنا ذهب بعض الحنفية إلى قطع عدم قطعية المستند وإلا لما كان للإجماع فائدة وليس بشيء لأن الفائدة ليست منحصرة فيه، بل تعاضد الدليل بدليل من الفوائد ثم إن دليلهم لو تم لدل على عدم تحقق إجماع ما عن مستند قطعي، وهو خلاف مذهبهم أيضاً، بل خلاف الواقع فافهم.
Issue: There is no ijma except upon a shar'i basis, according to the chosen position, contrary to some. For us: First: Issuing a fatwa without a shar'i proof is forbidden. And if there is no proof here except agreement, then each statement depends on the statement of all, and vice versa, which is apparent, so circularity is necessary. Ponder. And it may be said: The consequence of a fatwa without proof is only the possibility of error, not its occurrence. Also, the prohibition of issuing a fatwa without proof does not necessitate error in the ruling given; rather ijma has an effect in hitting the truth. And it was answered that the authority of ijma is only because it is the agreement of the mujtahids who are mujtahids. And if the fatwa is without proof and ijtihad, then it is not the say of a mujtahid as a mujtahid. And there is something of sophistry. For the opponent does not accept that authority is for that reason, but rather because the agreement of the muftis of this merciful Ummah cannot be upon error, whether they based it on ijtihad or not, as an honor for this Ummah. So it is better to say: A fatwa without proof, since it is forbidden, a righteous person would not venture it. If he does venture, he becomes a sinner, so he is no longer eligible for ijma nor for honor. So their say is not considered. Understand. And for us: Second: It is normally impossible for all to agree without a cause. So no agreement exists without a proof. Like the unlikely normal agreement of all upon one food due to lack of motive. And the possibility of necessary knowledge (i.e., that necessary knowledge occurs and they agree upon it or they are guided to correctness) is more remote. For if you say: The creation of necessary knowledge is not unlikely, because the noble saints are inspired with rulings and truths and knowledge that error cannot reach at all. I say: There is no doubt that necessary knowledge occurs in them, only a fool denies it. But if it is a proof, then agreement and gathering have no role. Otherwise, there must be a shar'i proof, unless it is said that its authority is conditional upon consensus, and the inspiration of one is not a proof. Ponder and await a complete statement. And if we are aided by success, we will fully discuss it, Allah willing. The one who allows ijma without a basis said: If a basis is necessary, what is the benefit of ijma, since the basis itself would suffice? We say: The benefit is certainty of the ruling after it was speculative, for the basis may be speculative. Hence some Hanafis held that the basis is not definitive, otherwise ijma would have no benefit. This is nothing. Because the benefit is not limited to that; rather, strengthening one proof with another is among the benefits. Moreover, if their argument were valid, it would lead to the non-existence of any ijma based on a definitive basis, which contradicts their own school and reality. Understand.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition that ijma must be based on a shar'i evidence (mustanad). The Hanafi position is that ijma without evidence is invalid. Two proofs are given: First, issuing a fatwa without evidence is forbidden, and if ijma had no basis, it would be a fatwa without evidence. The opponent argues that ijma itself provides protection from error, so evidence is not needed. The response refines: even if ijma is protected from error, the individuals' statements must be based on ijtihad, not random opinion. Second, it is rationally impossible for all mujtahids to agree on a ruling without a common cause—whether that cause is a shar'i evidence or necessary knowledge (inspiration). But necessary knowledge in individuals is not itself a proof unless agreed upon. The passage then addresses an objection: if evidence is required, why is ijma needed? The answer: ijma transforms a speculative basis into a certain ruling. This is a core Hanafi usul principle: ijma adds certainty.

Key Terms
المستند (al-mustanad): The shar'i evidence upon which a ruling or ijma is based.
القطعية (al-qat'iyya): Definitive knowledge or certainty.
الظن (al-zann): Speculative (preponderant opinion without certainty).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is one benefit of ijma even when the mustanad is speculative?
A) It makes the ruling obligatory for all Muslims.
B) It transforms the speculative basis into a certain ruling.
C) It eliminates the need for ijtihad.
D) It allows later scholars to change the ruling.

Key Takeaway
Ijma adds certainty to a ruling derived from a shar'i basis; it does not create rulings from mere agreement.

The Benefit of Ijma' Even with a Sanad
Debate on whether ijma' has benefit when a sanad already exists

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi argument that ijma' adds qat'iyya to a zanni sanad and evaluate the objection that ijma' is redundant when a sanad exists.

Core Idea
Ijma' elevates a zanni ruling to qat'i certainty — that is its unique benefit.

This passage tackles a classic objection to the authority of ijma': if every valid ijma' is based on a sanad (proof), then the sanad alone suffices, making ijma' redundant. The Hanafi response hinges on the distinction between zanni and qat'i evidence. We will unpack the argument and its internal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's argument
The objector claims that if ijma' requires a sanad, the sanad alone is sufficient, so ijma' has no benefit.
This is a common objection to the necessity of ijma'.
2
State the Hanafi response
The benefit is qat'iyya (definitiveness) for the ruling, which was previously zanni if the sanad is zanni.
The passage states: 'الفائدة القطعية للحكم بعدما كان ظنياً'.
3
Understand the internal Hanafi debate
Some Hanafis went further, denying qat'iyya of any sanad to preserve the benefit of ijma', but the author refutes this.
The author calls this extreme view 'ليس بشيء' (nothing).
4
Appreciate the author's additional point
Benefit also includes mutual reinforcement of dala'il, and the objection would lead to absurdity (negating ijma' on a qat'i sanad).
The author says 'تعاضد الدليل بدليل من الفوائد'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ساعدنا التوفيق فسنوفي القول فيه إن شاء الله تعالى. مجيز والإجماع من غير مستند (قالوا: لو لزم المستند (فما فائدة (الإجماع إذ يكفي المستند حينئذ (قلنا) الفائدة (القطعية) للحكم بعدما كان ظنياً، فإنه يجوز أن يكون المستند ظنياً (ومن ههنا ذهب بعض الحنفية إلى قطع عدم قطعية (المستند) وإلا لما كان للإجماع فائدة وليس بشيء) لأن الفائدة ليست منحصرة فيه، بل تعاضد الدليل بدليل من الفوائد ثم إن دليلهم لو تم لدل على عدم تحقق إجماع ما عن مستند قطعي، وهو خلاف مذهبهم أيضاً، بل خلاف الواقع فافهم.
May success help us, and we will fulfill the statement concerning it, if Allah wills. The one who permits consensus without a proof (said: if a proof is necessary, what benefit does consensus have? For the proof would suffice then. We said: The benefit is definitiveness for the ruling after it was speculative, for it is possible that the proof is speculative. And from this, some Hanafis went to the extent of denying the definitiveness of the proof, otherwise consensus would have no benefit, and that is nothing. Because the benefit is not confined to it, rather mutual support of evidence is among the benefits. Then their evidence, if complete, would indicate that no consensus exists with a definitive proof, which contradicts their school as well, rather contradicts reality, so understand.)
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a dispute about the benefit of ijma' (consensus) when there is already a sanad (proof). The objectors argue that if ijma' requires a sanad, then the sanad alone suffices and ijma' adds nothing. The Hanafi response is that ijma' elevates the ruling from zanni (speculative) to qat'i (definitive), because the sanad may itself be zanni. Some Hanafis even deny the definitiveness of any sanad to preserve the benefit of ijma'. The author refutes this by stating that benefit also includes mutual reinforcement of evidence, and that the objectors' argument would absurdly preclude any consensus based on a definitive sanad.

Key Terms
إجماع (ijma'): Consensus of the mujtahidun of the Ummah after the Prophet's era on a religious ruling.
مستند (sanad): The evidentiary basis (Qur'an, hadith, qiyas) that supports the ruling before consensus.
قطعية (qat'iyya): Definitive certainty of a ruling, as opposed to zanni (speculative).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this passage, what is the primary benefit of ijma' when a proof already exists?
A) It provides a new proof independent of the original.
B) It makes the ruling qat'i (definitive) after it was zanni (speculative).
C) It abrogates the original proof.
D) It is only beneficial when there is no proof at all.

Key Takeaway
Ijma' is not redundant even with a sanad; it elevates the ruling from speculative to definitive certainty.

Qiyas as Basis for Ijma: The Wine Hadd Example
Analyzing the companion's qiyas on wine hadd and its role in ijma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'musannad' (basis) and 'muthbit' (establisher) in Hanafi usul and explain how qiyas can serve as a basis for ijma without directly establishing a ruling.

Core Idea
Qiyas does not establish hudud, but it can serve as a basis for ijma, which then establishes the ruling.

In this passage, the author explores a critical episode in early Islamic jurisprudence: the Companions' consensus on the punishment for wine drinking. Through the example of 'Ali's analogy, we learn how qiyas can lead to ijma, and why the Hanafi school insists that the hadd is established by ijma, not by qiyas alone. This distinction between a 'basis' (musannad) and an 'establisher' (muthbit) is central to understanding the Hanafi theory of evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The Companions needed a ruling on the hadd (fixed punishment) for wine drinking. There was no explicit nass from Quran or Sunnah, so they resorted to ijtihad.
2
Analyze ‘Ali’s analogy
‘Ali (r) analogized the drunkard to a slanderer (qadhf) because intoxication leads to slander. He proposed 80 lashes, the same as for qadhf.
The chain: drink -> intoxication -> raving -> slander.
3
Recognize the objection
Some argued that this was istidlal (direct inference) rather than qiyas, implying a stronger form of evidence.
4
Apply the Hanafi critique
The author shows that istidlal would require a universal premise that every slanderer receives 80, which is not established. Therefore it is actually qiyas.
The major premise (كل مفتر فعليه ثمانون) is not universal.
5
Understand the musannad vs muthbit distinction
The hadd is established by ijma of the Companions, not by the qiyas itself. Qiyas only served as the basis (musannad) for that consensus.
This resolves the apparent contradiction that hudud cannot be established by qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
· ٢٩١ مستنداً) الأصل الثالث الإجماع مقامك لو أمرت ،عمر، وهذا يدل دلالة ظاهرة على أن تقديمه للصلاة لئلا يقول أحد : أنا أولى بالإمامة، فاحفظ وتحقق به فإنه هو الحق، وينفعك يوم القيامة (وقد وقع قياس حد الشرب على حد القذف (قال أمير المؤمنين (علي) كرم الله وجهه ووجوه آله الكرام حين استشار أمير المؤمنين عمر في الخمر يشربها الرجل نرى أن نحده ثمانين، فإنه إذا شرب سكر، وإذا سكر هذى وإذا هذى افترى فأرى عليه حد المفترين) قيل (هذا استدلال لا قياس أقول : الاستدلال إنما يتم لو ثبت أن كل مفتر قطعاً أو ظناً، فعليه ثمانون) لأنه لا بد من كلية الكبرى (ولم يثبت ، نعم يصح أن الشارب كأنه قاذف لأن المظنة كالمئنة) فأعطى ما يفضي إلى الشيء حكمه كتحريم مقدمات الزنا) لكن لا بد حينئذ من اثبات أن حكم القذف ثابت فيما يفضي إليه وفي المشهور أنه قياس الشرب على القذف بجامع الافتراء، وفيه أنه يلزم أن يثبت الحد في كل افتراء، وجوابه أنه قياس بجامع الافتراء الخاص فتأمل فيه (ثم أقول المستند أعم من (المثبت لأن الشيء ربما يكون مستنداً، ولا يكون مثبتاً (كقطعي سنده ظني) فإن هذا السند لا يكون مثبتاً للقطع ومن ههنا لا يكون القياس مثبتاً للحد عندنا للحد وذلك لأن الإجماع رافع للشبهة المانعة عن إثبات الحدّ فالحد ههنا ثبت بالإجماع والقياس مستند (فاندفع توهم التناقض بين الكلامين الحدود لا تثبت بالقياس، والقياس يصلح سنداً للإجماع لاثبات الحدود (كما في التقرير وهذا لا يسمن ولا يغني من جوع. فإن الفتوى لما كان حراماً من غير دليل، فأهل الإجماع من أين علموا الحدّ من القياس؟ فهو المثبت أو من غيره، وهو مفروض الانتفاء، وإن قيل : القياس ليس بمثبت بل مظهر، قلت: الكلام في هذا الإظهار، فإن الحنفية يمنعونه في الحدود، بل نقول : الصحابة أجمعوا بهذا القياس على حد الشرب، فإثباته الحدّ مجمع عليه ولا مخلص إلا أن يمنعوا كونه قياساً يقولوا إنه حكم بأن هذه المظنة قائمة مقام المئنة بالسماع، فإنه قد ثبت إقامة الحد في زمن الرسول ، فهذا نقل بحاصله فتأمل فيه المنكرون
The third foundation is ijma. [Example:] Your position is like if you ordered 'Umar. This clearly indicates that he was put forward for prayer so that no one could say 'I am more entitled to leadership.' So memorize and realize it, for it is the truth and will benefit you on the Day of Judgment. (And there occurred the analogy of the hadd of drinking on the hadd of qadhf. 'Ali, may Allah honor his face, said when 'Umar consulted him about wine: 'We think we should punish him with eighty lashes, because if he drinks he becomes intoxicated, and if he becomes intoxicated he raves, and if he raves he slanders, so I consider he should receive the penalty of the slanderer.') It was said: 'This is istidlal, not qiyas.' I say: Istidlal is only valid if it is established that every slanderer, certainly or probably, receives eighty, because the major premise must be universal, and it is not established. Rather, it is valid that the drinker is like a slanderer because the indication is like the actual cause. So one gives the ruling of the thing to what leads to it, like prohibiting the preliminaries of fornication. But then it is necessary to prove that the ruling of qadhf is established for what leads to it. And in the well-known view, it is qiyas of drinking on qadhf due to the common factor of slander. There is an objection that it would necessitate establishing the hadd for every slander; the answer is that it is qiyas with the specific common factor of slander, so reflect on it. Then I say: The basis (musannad) is more general than the establisher (muthbit), because something may be a basis but not an establisher, like a qat'i chain of transmission is zanni [in some aspect] – such a chain does not establish certainty. Hence qiyas does not establish the hadd according to us, because ijma removes the doubt that prevents establishing the hadd. So the hadd here is established by ijma, and qiyas is the basis. Thus the apparent contradiction between 'hudud are not established by qiyas' and 'qiyas can serve as a basis for ijma to establish hudud' is dispelled. But this does not satisfy the need. For if giving a ruling without proof is forbidden, how did the people of ijma know the hadd from qiyas? It is either the establisher or something else, and the latter is assumed non-existent. And if it is said that qiyas is not an establisher but a revealer, I say: The discussion is about this revelation, for the Hanafis prohibit it in hudud. Rather, we say: The Companions formed ijma based on this qiyas on the hadd of drinking, so its establishment is agreed upon. There is no escape unless they deny that it is qiyas and say that it is a ruling that this indication stands in place of the actual cause by hearing, for the hadd was established at the time of the Prophet. So this is a transmission of its substance. Reflect on this, O deniers.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage discusses the third source of Islamic law, ijma (consensus), and examines whether qiyas (analogical reasoning) can serve as a valid basis for establishing an ijma. The author uses the historical incident where Caliph Umar consulted Ali about the punishment for wine drinking. Ali made an analogy between a drunkard and a slanderer, arguing for 80 lashes. The text then debates whether this reasoning is istidlal (direct evidence from text) or qiyas. The Hanafi position is that ijma itself establishes the ruling, and qiyas is only the basis (musannad) that leads to ijma, not the direct establisher of the hadd. This resolves the apparent contradiction that hudud cannot be established by qiyas, yet qiyas is used here—because the hadd is actually established by ijma, and qiyas only served as the basis for that ijma.

Key Terms
مستند (musannad): A basis or source that leads to a ruling but does not directly establish it.
مثبت (muthbit): A proof that directly establishes a ruling or certainty.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to extend a ruling from an original case to a new case based on a common effective cause ('illah).
استدلال (istidlal): A broader term for inference, sometimes considered more direct than qiyas, but here distinguished as requiring a universal premise.
إجماع (ijma): Consensus of the mujtahidun of the ummah on a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position discussed in this passage, how is the hadd for wine drinking established?
A) By direct qiyas from qadhf
B) By ijma of the companions, with qiyas as a basis
C) By the Quran alone
D) By the hadith on wine punishment

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, qiyas can serve as a basis (musannad) for ijma, but the ruling itself is established by ijma, not by qiyas.

Objections to Qiyas as Basis for Ijma and Hanafi Responses
Addressing two objections against using qiyas as a valid basis for ijma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the two objections against using qiyas as a basis for ijma and state the Hanafi responses using the principle that permissibility changes after ijma and that only qualified mujtahids block consensus.

Core Idea
Opposing qiyas is permissible before ijma, but after ijma it becomes prohibited; not all deniers block ijma.

This passage addresses two challenges to the validity of ijma based on qiyas. The first objection argues that since qiyas can be opposed, ijma based on it would conflict with previous ijma allowing opposition. The second doubts that ijma can be formed given persistent disagreement on qiyas. The Hanafi responses refine our understanding of when opposition is valid and who can prevent consensus.

Guided Path
1
State the first objection
Identify the claim that if qiyas becomes the basis, then opposing it becomes opposing ijma, but ijma already permits opposing qiyas.
2
Analyze the Hanafi response to the first objection
Understand that the permissibility to oppose qiyas existed only before ijma was formed; after ijma, the ruling becomes binding.
The distinction is between opposing qiyas as qiyas (permitted) and opposing it after it becomes ijma (prohibited).
3
State the second objection
Identify the claim that qiyas is disputed, so there will always be deniers (nufat) preventing ijma.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the second objection
Note that deniers may not exist in every age; if they do, they may be innovators or non-mujtahids who do not block ijma.
The Hanafi position requires that opponents be qualified mujtahids, not just any dissenter.
5
Synthesize the principle
Combine both responses: ijma can validly be based on qiyas because the nature of opposition changes after consensus, and the existence of unqualified dissenters does not hinder it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالوا: (أولاً) لو وقع القياس سنداً لما مخالفته، لأن المخالفة حينئذ مخالفة الإجماع و(الإجماع منعقد على جواز مخالفته، قلنا لا نسلم الإجماع على جواز مخالفته مطلقاً، بل على جواز مخالفته قبل: الإجماع، أقول) انعقد الإجماع على جواز مخالفته من حيث إنه قياس وههنا إنما امتنع مخالفته من حيث إنه مجمع عليه (و) قالوا (ثانياً) القياس اختلف فيه فلا يخلو عصر ما من نفاته) فلا ينعقد على طبقه الإجماع، فإن النافي لا يستدل به قلنا : الخلاف حادث فلا نسلم عدم خلو العصر عن نفاته (و) أيضاً الدليل (منقوض بالعموم فإنه أيضاً مختلف فيه أقول : على أن عدم الخلو ممنوع) بعد تسليم الاختلاف من القديم أيضاً، فإنه يجوز أن لا يبقى في عصر من يتمذهب بنفيه تأمل فيه، والأولى أن يقال : إن عدم خلو عصر عن نفاته لا يلزم أن يكون النافي ممن هو أهل الإجماع، بل يجوز أن يكون من المبتدعة أو غير مجتهد فافهم.
They said: (First) If qiyas becomes the basis of a ruling, then opposing it would be opposing ijma, but ijma is established on the permissibility of opposing qiyas. We say: We do not concede absolute permissibility of opposing it; rather, it is permissible before ijma. I say: Ijma was formed on the permissibility of opposing it in its capacity as qiyas, but here opposition is prohibited because it is agreed upon (by ijma). (And) they said: (Second) Qiyas is disputed, so no era is free of its deniers, thus ijma based on it cannot be formed, because the denier cannot use it as evidence. We say: The disagreement is recent, so we do not concede that every era is free of its deniers. (And) also the proof is refuted by general texts (umum), which are also disputed. I say: The claim that no era is free of deniers is false even after conceding the antiquity of the disagreement, because it is possible that in some era no one follows the denial. Reflect on this. Better to say: The fact that no era is free of its deniers does not necessarily mean that the denier is among those qualified to form ijma; rather, the denier may be an innovator or a non-mujtahid. Understand this.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage presents two objections raised against using qiyas as a basis for ijma and the Hanafi responses. First objection: if qiyas is the basis, then after ijma is formed, opposing the ruling would be opposing ijma; but ijma itself has established that qiyas can be opposed (because qiyas is only zanni). The response: the permissibility to oppose qiyas exists only before ijma is formed; after ijma, it becomes binding. Second objection: since qiyas is a contested source, there will always be some mujtahid who denies qiyas, so ijma cannot be established. The Hanafi response: the existence of deniers is not guaranteed in every age; even if there were deniers in the past, a later generation may have no denier. Moreover, those who deny qiyas may be innovators or non-mujtahids, so they do not prevent the formation of ijma.

Key Terms
مخالفة (mukhalafah): Opposition or disagreement with a ruling or source.
نفاة (nufat): Those who deny the validity of qiyas as a source.
إجماع منعقد (ijma mun'aqid): A formed consensus that becomes binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the Hanafi respond to the objection that there is always someone who denies qiyas, preventing ijma?
A) They accept the objection and give up using qiyas
B) They say that the denier might be an innovator or non-mujtahid, not affecting ijma
C) They say that ijma cannot be formed if there is any dispute
D) They say that the objection is based on a false premise

Key Takeaway
Qiyas can serve as a basis for ijma, and once ijma is formed, opposition becomes impermissible; the presence of some deniers does not automatically block ijma if they are not qualified mujtahids.
