The Scope of Ijma: Does Agreement on a Minimum Prove Exclusivity?
Refuting the claim that ijma can establish a minimum exclusive ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi usul holds that ijma on a minimum amount does not establish that amount as the exclusive ruling and identify the logical flaw in claiming exclusivity from partial agreement.

Core Idea
Ijma proves the minimum is wajib, but not that no additional amount beyond it is required.

This passage tackles a subtle but important methodological point: when scholars agree on a minimum value (e.g., one-third diyah for a Jew), does that consensus prove that the minimum is the complete ruling? Some argue yes, because the minimum is included in all views. The Hanafi response is a careful logical distinction: ijma only proves the obligation of the minimum, but it does not exclude the possibility of additional payment beyond that minimum. Without a separate proof, you cannot claim the minimum is the exclusive ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
Recognize that the opponent argues that since the one-third diyah is included in all opinions (full, half, third), it is therefore agreed upon by everyone and thus the exclusive ruling.
The opponent uses the fact that the minimum is present in all views to argue for its uniqueness.
2
Understand the opponent's reasoning
The opponent's reasoning is that if a value appears in every possible ruling, then that value must be the agreed-upon ruling. Here, one-third appears in the full (as part), half (as part), and third (as whole), so it is common to all.
This is a form of argument from 'lowest common denominator'.
3
Identify the Hanafi critique
The Hanafi response distinguishes between 'proving the obligation of the minimum' and 'proving that the minimum is the only amount without addition'. Ijma only does the former; the latter requires an independent proof (dalil akhar).
The critique hinges on the scope of what ijma can establish.
4
Apply the principle to the diyah example
Ijma establishes that at least one-third diyah is wajib, but it does not tell us whether the full diyah is required (which includes the one-third). The claimant of one-third as the exclusive amount must bring evidence that no additional amount is due.
This shows that ijma on a minimum does not negate the possibility of a higher obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الحق أن مثل قول الشافعي رضي الله عنه : دية اليهودي الثلث، لا التمسك يصح فيه بالإجماع يعني إذا اختلفت الأقوال في تحديد الشيء فلا يصح في الحد الأقل بالإجماع خلافاً للبعض، والدعوى ضرورية، وإنما الأهم كشف شبهة الخصم فقال (قالوا: الأمة إما قائل بالكل أو النصف أو الثلث والثلث موجود في النصف والكل، فثبت على كل تقدير، فهو لازم من قول الكل، فهو مجمع عليه (قلنا : (دل) الإجماع على وجوب الثلث) أعم من أن يكون مع الزيادة أو بدونه فلا يجوز التنقيص عنه ( أما ) دلالته عليه فقط من غير زيادة (فلا) يلزم إلا بدليل آخر هذا خلف) لأن المفروض أن الدليل هو الإجماع والحاصل أن القائل بالأقل ينفي الزيادة وذا غير لازم من الإجماع فافهم.
Issue: The truth is that the statement of al-Shafi'i, 'The diyah of a Jew is one-third,' cannot be upheld by ijma in that minimal amount. That is, if opinions differ in determining something, it is not valid to claim ijma on the minimal amount, contrary to some. The matter is obviously self-evident, but the important thing is to refute the opponent's doubt. They said: The ummah either says the diyah is full, half, or one-third. The one-third is included in the half and the full, so it is established on every estimate. Thus it is logically entailed by everyone's statement, so it is agreed upon. We say: Ijma proves the obligation of one-third, but it proves it without specifying whether it is with or without addition. It does not prove it alone without addition; that requires a separate proof. The claimant of the minimum intends to negate additional payment, which is not necessitated by ijma.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This short but significant mas'alah explains a subtle point about the scope of ijma. The example is from Imam al-Shafi'i's ruling that the blood-money (diyah) for a Jew is one-third of the diyah of a Muslim. Some opponents argue that since some scholars say it's full diyah, some say half, and some say one-third, then at least one-third is agreed upon by all. The Hanafi refutation: ijma only establishes that one-third is wajib; it does not establish that one-third is the only amount and that no additional amount is required. To assert that the minimum is one-third without any increase requires a separate proof that excludes the possibility of more. The ijma only gives certainty on the minimal obligation, but not on its exclusivity. Therefore, one cannot claim 'the ruling is the one-third' as an exclusive ruling from ijma alone. This shows that ijma does not necessarily prove the minimum in a way that denies other possibilities.

Key Terms
إجماع (ijma): Consensus of the mujtahidun on a ruling. Here, it is argued that ijma on a part of a ruling does not exclude other possibilities.
أقل (aqall): The minimum amount that is agreed upon.
دية (diyah): Blood-money, monetary compensation for homicide or bodily harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because there is no ijma on the diyah of a Jew at all
B) Because ijma only proves the obligation of one-third, not that it is the only amount without addition
C) Because the Shafi'i view is not based on ijma
D) Because the Hanafi rejects qiyas in hudud

Key Takeaway
Ijma on a minimum value only establishes that the minimum is obligatory; it does not by itself exclude the possibility of additional requirements.

The Binding Nature of Ijma Ahad
Proof that ijma ahad is binding, with proofs and rebuttals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on ijma ahad, cite its two main proofs, and defend it against the objection of improbability.

Core Idea
Ijma ahad is binding because it yields probability, and acting on probability is obligatory.

This passage addresses a key usul dispute: is a consensus reported by a single narrator authoritative? The Hanafi school affirms it, using analogical reasoning from ahad hadith and the prophetic principle of judging by the apparent. We'll walk through the proofs and the rebuttals to common objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the dispute
The passage states that ijma reported by single narrators (ijma ahad) must be acted upon, according to the chosen Hanafi position, contrary to al-Ghazali and some Hanafis.
Note that the opposing view is not only from Shafi'is but also from within the Hanafi school.
2
Analyze the first proof (qiyas on ahad hadith)
The author argues: if a zanni (probable) report of a hadith obliges action definitively, then a qat'i (definitive) matter like ijma, even when transmitted singly, is even more deserving of obligating action.
The strength of the analogy lies in the fact that ijma is originally certain (qat'i al-thubut in its own right), unlike ahad hadith which are zanni from the start.
3
Analyze the second proof (hadith 'We judge by the apparent')
The Prophet said, 'We judge by the apparent.' The author argues this indicates a continuous custom (al-dawam wa al-ittifaq) of acting on apparent evidence, which includes ahad ijma. This establishes obligation, not just permissibility.
The author refutes the claim that the hadith only permits action; he shows it implies obligation because rejection would make it forbidden, and there is no third option.
4
Address the objection of improbability
Opponents say it is improbable for one person to know the consensus of all scholars. The Hanafi response: ijma can be known gradually through multiple fatwas over time, and a single person may infer it from indicative signs (amarat) or reports.
The passage also mentions that even if many know, they may not transmit due to lack of motives (tawafur al-dawa'i).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة الإجماع (الأحادي أي المنقول بأخبار الواحد (يجب العمل به) في المختار (خلافاً للغزالي) الإمام حجة الإسلام قدس سره. وبعض الحنفية، ومثل بما قيل) قائله عبيدة السلماني (ما اجتمع أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم على شيء كاجتماعهم على محافظة الأربع قبل الظهر، والإسفار بالفجر وتحريم نكاح الأخت في عدة الأخت في التيسير» نقلاً عن بعض شروح التحرير»، هكذا يورده المشايخ رحمهم الله تعالى والله أعلم به . نعم : أخرج ابن أبي شيبة عن عمرو بن ميمون: لم يكن أصحاب رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم يتركون أربع ركعات قبل الظهر على حال وعن إبراهيم: ما اجتمع أصحاب محمد صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه واسلم على شيء ما اجتمعوا على التنوير بالفجر ولعله لذلك قال بصيغة التمريض، أو لأن الظاهر من هذا اجماع الأكثر تأمل فيه (لنا أولاً: نقل الظني آحاداً ( كالخبر ) المؤوّل مثلاً (موجب) للعمل (قطعاً، فالقطعي) المنقول أحاداً الذي الإجماع (أولى) بأن يوجب العمل، وهذا ظاهر جداً (و) لنا :(ثانياً : أنه ظاهر لإفادته الظن) بالضرورة، كالخبر المنقول آحاداً وقال صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم: «نحن نحكم بالظاهر» وقد ثبت معناه فوجب الحكم بهذا الإجماع (أقول : (وهو) أي لفظ الحديث للدوام والاتفاق أي عادتنا دائماً أن نحكم بالظاهر وذلك دليل الوجوب) وإلا لم يدم ولم يتفق (فاندفع ما في شرح الشرح أنه لا دلالة فيه على وجوب العمل بل غاية ما لزم منه الجواز (وما قيل إنه دل على بطلان الحرمة) وهو ظاهر (فتحقق الوجوب إذ الكل متفقون على أنه واجب أو حرام) لأن قال من بحجيته قال بوجوب العمل، ومن لا قال بحرمة العمل، فالكل متفقون بالإجماع على أنه ليس جائز ،العمل، وإذا أبطل الحرمة تعين الوجوب (فأقول: فيه مصادرة) فإن القائل بالوجوب إنما استدل بهذا الدليل فقبله لا قول بالوجوب، فالقول به موقوف على صحته، وصحته إن كانت موقوفة على القول بالوجوب دار وإن أثبت الوجوب بدليل آخر فلا كلام فيه (فتأمل) فإنه دقيق (وقد استبعد إفادة هذا النقل الظن لبعد إطلاعه عليهم أجمعين (وعلى إجماعهم وحده من بين جماعة متشاركة في سبب العلم (كما مر عن الإمام (أحمد) من ادعى الإجماع فهو كاذب (بخلاف الخبر ) فإنه يمكن أن يكون في المجلس واحد فيسمع دون غيره، ولا كذلك الذين كثروا غاية الكثرة، وجواب: أن الإجماع لا يجب أن يكون بقول الكل معاً بل قد يكون بافتاء واحد في بيته، ثم بافتاء آخر في بيته، فيمكن أن يكون عند فتوى واحد أو أكثر هو وحده ثم اطلع هو وحده أو مع غيره على فتوى سائر الناس قولاً منهم أو بأمارات مفهمة موقعة للعلم أو الظن فحينئذ قد أطلع على الإجماع واحد من غير استبعاد، وأيضاً يجوز أن يطلع أكثرون ، لكن لم ينقلوا لعدم توفر الدواعي فافهم (وما في التحرير من دفع الاستبعاد بعدالة الناقل فخبره يفيد الظن فأقول : منفوض بخبر الواحد فيما يعم البلوى به فإنه غير مقبول مع كون الناقل عدلاً فتدبر . ثم الحق أن المسألة مبنية على أنه : هل يشترط القطع في الأصول أم لا؟ فمن اشترط القطع لا يقبل هذا الإجماع، ومن لا يشترط يقبل لعدم الدليل القطعي من الإجماع على حجية هذا الإجماع فيه تأمل فإن أدلة حجية الإجماع غير فارقة بل الإجماع على اتباع الراجح يفيد الحجية أيضاً فافهم .
Issue: Ijma (ahad, i.e., transmitted by single narrators) must be acted upon, according to the chosen position, contrary to al-Ghazali, the Imam Hujjat al-Islam (may Allah sanctify his secret) and some Hanafis. An example is given: what was said by its speaker, Ubayda al-Salmani: 'The Companions of the Messenger of Allah (صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم) never agreed on anything like their agreement on preserving four rak'ahs before Zuhr, and performing Fajr in darkness, and prohibition of marrying a sister during the 'idda of a sister' – as quoted from some commentaries on al-Tahrir. Thus the scholars (may Allah have mercy on them) mention it, and Allah knows best. Yes, Ibn Abi Shayba narrated from 'Amr ibn Maymun: 'The Companions of the Messenger of Allah (صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم) would not leave four rak'ahs before Zuhr under any circumstance.' And from Ibrahim: 'The Companions of Muhammad (صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم) never agreed on anything as they agreed on performing Fajr in darkness.' Perhaps for that reason he used the phrasing of doubt, or because the apparent meaning is consensus of the majority – contemplate. For us: First: The transmission of the probable singly (like a hadith that is interpreted, for example) necessitates action definitively, so the transmitted about ijma is more deserving of necessitating action. And this is very apparent. And for us: Second: It is apparent because it yields probability by necessity, like a hadith transmitted singly. And he (صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم) said: 'We judge by the apparent.' And its meaning has been established, so it is necessary to judge by this ijma. I say: The wording of the hadith indicates continuity and agreement, i.e., our custom is always to judge by the apparent, and that is the proof of obligation; otherwise it would not be continuous and agreed. So what is in Sharh al-Sharh is refuted: that there is no indication in it of obligation of action, rather the utmost that follows from it is permissibility. And what is said that it indicates the invalidity of prohibition – it is apparent, so obligation is established, since all agree that it is either obligatory or forbidden. Because he who says it is authoritative says it is obligatory to act, and he who does not says it is forbidden to act. So all agree by consensus that it is not permissible to act; and if prohibition is invalidated, then obligation is determined. I say: There is circular reasoning in this, for the one who argues for obligation has used this evidence, so before acceptance there is no statement of obligation. So the statement of obligation depends on its validity, and its validity, if it depends on the statement of obligation, is circular; and if obligation is proven by another evidence, then there is no discussion. So contemplate, for it is subtle. And it is unlikely that this transmission yields probability due to the difficulty of one person knowing all of them and their consensus alone from among a group sharing in the cause of knowledge, as has passed from Imam Ahmad: 'Whoever claims ijma is a liar.' Unlike a hadith, for it can be that a single person in a gathering hears without others, but not so with those who are extremely numerous. The answer: Ijma does not have to be by the statement of all together; rather, it may be by one mufti in his house, then another mufti in his house. So it is possible that at the time of one or more fatwas, he alone or with others knows the fatwas of all the people, either by their statement or by indicative signs that produce knowledge or probability. Then a single person may have knowledge of ijma without improbability. Also, many may know but not transmit due to lack of motives – understand. And what is in al-Tahrir about removing the improbability by the reliability of the transmitter – his report yields probability. I say: It is refuted by a single report in matters of general necessity, for it is not accepted even if the transmitter is reliable – so ponder. Then the truth is that the issue depends on whether certainty is required in fundamentals or not. He who requires certainty does not accept this ijma; he who does not require it accepts, because there is no definitive evidence from ijma about the authority of this ijma – there is contemplation in it, for the proofs of the authority of ijma are not differentiating; rather, ijma on following the preponderant also yields authority – so understand.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the binding nature of ijma transmitted by a single narrator (ijma ahad). The author states the chosen Hanafi position that such ijma is obligatory to act upon, contrary to al-Ghazali and some Hanafis. The author provides proofs: First, if a zanni (probable) report of a hadith obliges action definitively, then a qat'i (definitive) matter like ijma when transmitted singly is even more deserving. Second, such ijma yields probability (zann) like ahad hadith, and the Prophet said 'We judge by the apparent,' which indicates obligation. The author then addresses objections: that the hadith only indicates permissibility, not obligation; and that there is a circular argument. The author also discusses the practical difficulty of one person knowing the ijma of all scholars, but responds that ijma can be known gradually from fatwas of individuals, and it is possible for one person to have knowledge of it. Finally, the issue is linked to whether certainty is required in usul al-fiqh; those who require certainty reject ahad ijma, while those who accept probability accept it.

Key Terms
الإجماع الأحادي (al-ijma al-ahadi): Consensus reported by a single narrator, known through ahad transmission.
الظن (al-zann): Probable knowledge, less than certainty, but sufficient for practical obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main proof used by the Hanafis for the obligation to act on ahad reports of ijma?
A) The report itself is mutawatir.
B) Qiyas on ahad hadith and the hadith 'We judge by the apparent.'
C) Consensus of the Companions on the matter.
D) The report is narrated by a reliable transmitter.

Key Takeaway
Ijma ahad is binding in the Hanafi school because it provides probability (zann), and acting on the apparent is obligatory.

intermediate
12 min
Authority of Consensus via Single Reports
Establishing proof for authority of consensus transmitted as ahad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qiyas-based argument to defend the authority of ijma' transmitted via ahad reports and evaluate the epistemological grounds of the opposing position.

Core Idea
If a zanni khabar ahad obligates action, then a qat'i ruling transmitted as ahad (ijma') is even more worthy of being followed.

This passage addresses a foundational debate in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: whether ijma' (consensus) retains its binding authority when transmitted through non-mutawatir (ahad) chains. The author presents two proofs: a qiyas from khabar ahad and the hadith 'We judge by the apparent'. Objections from al-Ghazali and others are systematically refuted, showing the Hanafi commitment to acting on probabilistic evidence. This lesson unpacks the arguments and clarifies the epistemological premise underlying the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
What exactly is the khilaf about? Is it about the authority of ijma' itself, or its transmission through ahad routes? Note that the author mentions al-Ghazali and some Hanafis as opponents.
The disagreement is over whether ijma' reported by a single chain (khabar ahad) can be a valid hujja, given that consensus should be known with certainty.
2
Extract the Hanafi proofs
Two proofs are given: (1) Qiyas: If a zanni khabar ahad is accepted as binding, then a qat'i content (like ijma') transmitted as ahad is awla (more worthy). (2) The hadith 'We judge by the apparent' indicates obligation to act on apparent knowledge.
The first proof is qiyas jaliy; the second is a direct textual argument.
3
Analyze the objections and responses
The opponents argue that ijma' ahad cannot produce reliable zann due to the impossibility of knowing all mujtahids. The author counters by explaining that ijma' can be known gradually through fatwas or indications, and that the hadith commands action on what is apparent.
Note the author's refutation of the claim that the hadith only permits action: he argues that negation of prohibition implies obligation.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if ijma' ahad is accepted, then rulings based on such consensus are binding; if rejected, they are not. This affects issues like the examples given (four rak'ahs before Zuhr, isfar, etc.)
The author ends by linking the dispute to the broader question of whether certainty is required in usul.
5
Trace the epistemological premise
The passage concludes that the issue hinges on whether certainty is required in usul. Those who require certainty reject ijma' ahad; those who accept probability accept it. This highlights the Hanafi method of combining zanni evidence.
This step connects to the broader Hanafi usul principle of accepting zanni adillah for action.

عدلاً فتدبر . ثم الحق أن المسألة مبنية على أنه : هل يشترط القطع في الأصول أم لا؟ فمن اشترط القطع لا يقبل هذا الإجماع، ومن لا يشترط يقبل لعدم الدليل القطعي من الإجماع على حجية هذا الإجماع فيه تأمل فإن أدلة حجية الإجماع غير فارقة بل الإجماع على اتباع الراجح يفيد الحجية أيضاً فافهم .
The third principle: consensus (contrary to al-Ghazali, the Imam Hujjat al-Islam, and some Hanafis). And he gave an example with what was said by its speaker, Ubaydah al-Sulmani: 'The Companions of the Messenger of God agreed on nothing like their agreement on preserving four rak'ahs before Zuhr, doing Fajr in the dark [isfar], and prohibiting marriage to the sister during the iddah of the sister.' ...
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a dispute about the authority of consensus transmitted through single reports (khabar ahad). The author first states that consensus is the third source, contrary to al-Ghazali and some Hanafis. He gives examples of consensus from the Companions, like maintaining four rak'ahs before Zuhr, performing Fajr in darkness (isfar), and prohibiting marriage to a woman's sister during her iddah. Then he presents two proofs for the authority of such consensus: First, if a zanni ruling from a single report obligates action definitively, then a qat'i ruling transmitted as ahad (i.e., consensus) is even more worthy. Second, consensus yields zann by necessity like khabar ahad, and the Prophet said, 'We judge by what is apparent,' which indicates obligation. The author then addresses objections, including the claim that the hadith only shows permissibility, not obligation. He refutes this by noting that action is either obligatory or forbidden, and if the hadith negates prohibition, obligation is established. He also counters the objection that knowing all mujtahids is impossible by explaining that consensus can be known through gradual fatwas or indications. He concludes that the issue hinges on whether certainty is required in usul; those who require certainty reject this consensus, while those who accept probability accept it, as no certain proof exists against it.

Key Terms
إجماع (ijmāʻ): Consensus of mujtahids of the ummah after the Prophet's death on a religious ruling
ظنّي (ẓannī): Probabilistic, not certain; used here to describe the mode of transmission of consensus
خبر واحد (khabar wāḥid): A hadith with a chain of transmission that does not reach the level of tawatur

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi argument in this passage, why is consensus transmitted via ahad reports more worthy of obligating action than a khabar ahad?
A) Because consensus is always qat'i while khabar ahad is zanni
B) Because the subject matter of consensus is always fard
C) Because if a zanni report obligates action, then a qat'i report transmitted as ahad (consensus) is even more worthy
D) Because consensus is proven by multiple hadiths

Key Takeaway
Consensus transmitted via ahad reports is binding in the Hanafi school because its content is qat'i and its transmission produces zann, analogous to khabar ahad.

Al-Ijma — Consensus

Denial of Definitive Consensus and the Role of Ta'wil in Takfir
Determining when denial of definitive consensus constitutes disbelief

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that denying a definitive consensus (ijma' qat'i) is kufr unless the denier has a plausible ta'wil, and distinguish this from denial of daruriyyat al-din which is always kufr.

Core Idea
Denial of definitive consensus is kufr unless a sincere ta'wil prevents it.

In this passage, we explore a critical usul al-fiqh question: when does denial of a consensus amount to disbelief? The Hanafi school's answer is nuanced, balancing the definitive nature of ijma' with the possibility of interpretive error. The text addresses a key exception: those who deny a consensus due to a false but sincerely held interpretation (ta'wil) are not declared disbelievers, as illustrated by the Rafidah's denial of the caliphate of Abu Bakr.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act under discussion
The act is 'inkar hukm al-ijma' al-qat'i' (denial of a ruling established by definitive consensus). Recognize that the consensus here is transmitted by tawatur without prior opposition.
The consensus must be qat'i in both transmission and meaning.
2
State the default ruling
Most Hanafis and a group of other scholars hold that such denial is kufr because it rejects what is definitively established as God's ruling.
The dalil is the definitiveness itself: denial of certainty is akin to rejecting divine command.
3
Identify the opposing view and its basis
Some scholars argue that the authority of consensus is theoretical (nazari), so denial does not automatically become kufr; error is excusable.
They analogize to disagreements like the basmalah.
4
Understand the exception for ta'wil
The text uses the Rafidah as an example: they deny the consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate but have a ta'wil (that Ali consented under duress). Because they do not deliberately reject a consensus they acknowledge, they are not declared kafir.
The ta'wil must be sincere, even if false; it prevents the denial from being conscious rejection.
5
Contrast with daruriyyat al-din
Denial of matters essential to the religion (prayer, zakat, hajj, etc.) is unanimously kufr, regardless of any ta'wil. These are exceptions to the leniency.
This shows that not all denials are treated equally; the severity depends on the subject.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Issue: Denial of the ruling of definitive consensus - and it is that transmitted via tawatur without any prior opposition - is disbelief according to most Hanafis and a group besides them, because it is denial of what is definitively established as God's ruling. Contrary to a group who said: Its authority, though definitive, is theoretical, so it falls into the realm of ambiguity from the realm of clarity like the basmalah. From here - that is, because denial of its ruling is not disbelief - the Rafidah were not declared disbelievers despite their denial of the caliphate of the rightly guided caliph of the Messenger of God, upon which consensus was held without doubt. ...
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on denying a definitive consensus (ijma' qat'i). Most Hanafis hold that such denial is kufr because it rejects what is certainly from God. However, a minority view argues that the authority of consensus is based on theoretical reasoning (nazari), so denial does not reach the level of kufr, analogous to differing over the basmalah. The text then discusses why the Rafidah (a Shia group) are not declared kafir despite their denial of the consensus on Abu Bakr's caliphate. The correct Hanafi position, as detailed in works like al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, is that the early imams did not takfir the Rafidah. Their denial stems from a false ta'wil (interpretive belief) that Ali pledged allegiance under duress, which, though baseless, prevents takfir because they do not deliberately reject a consensus they acknowledge. True kufr requires denying a known consensus while admitting it is consensus without any ta'wil. The Khawarij are cited as an example: they deny clear virtues of Ali and rights of Muslims, but even they were not prevented from entering mosques. However, matters essential to the religion (daruriyat al-din) like prayer, zakat, hajj, etc., are excluded; denial of those is unanimously kufr.

Key Terms
إنكار حكم الإجماع القطعي (inkār ḥukm al-ijmāʻ al-qaṭʻī): Rejecting a ruling established by definitive consensus (transmitted tawatur)
تأويل (taʼwīl): Interpretation that removes apparent meaning; here, a false but plausible explanation that prevents takfir
داروريات الدين (ḍarūriyyāt al-dīn): Necessities of religion: fundamental beliefs and practices known by everyone, e.g., prayer, fasting

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in this passage, why are the Rafidah not declared kafir despite denying the consensus on the caliphate of Abu Bakr?
A) Because they accept the consensus but interpret it differently
B) Because they are considered Muslims by default
C) Because they offer a ta'wil that Ali consented under duress, and they do not admit it is consensus
D) Because consensus on the caliphate is not definitive

Key Takeaway
Denial of a definitive consensus is kufr only when the denier acknowledges it as consensus and rejects it without any ta'wil; otherwise, takfir is not applied.

Ranking of Ijma' and Its Impact on Takfir
Classification of ijma' and takfir implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the ranks of ijma' according to the Hanafi school and identify the ruling regarding the denier of each rank, including the role of obstacles like ta'wil.

Core Idea
Not all ijma' is equal; only the highest rank triggers takfir in principle, but obstacles may prevent its application.

This lesson explores the Hanafi classification of ijma' into hierarchical ranks. While all qat'i ijma' is theoretically binding, the school distinguishes between the consensus of the Companions and later consensuses in terms of the consequence for its denier. Understanding this ranking is crucial for grasping the school's approach to takfir and the respect for scholarly disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the highest rank of ijma'
The textual ijma' of the Companions (those who explicitly agreed on a ruling) is the highest rank, akin to a mutawatir text. Its denier is declared kafir in principle.
This is based on the certainty of transmission and lack of disagreement among the Companions.
2
Understand the qualification 'in principle'
The author states 'in principle' (fi al-asl) to indicate that the ruling of takfir is theoretical. In practice, obstacles (mawani') such as a valid interpretation (ta'wil) may block its application.
This explains why Rawafidh and Khawarij are not declared kafir despite denying some ijma'.
3
Differentiate lower ranks
Silent ijma' (sukuti) is disputed in authority; its denier is not declared kafir. Ijma' of later generations without prior disagreement leads to tadhlil (declaring astray), while ijma' after prior disagreement yields zann and does not warrant takfir.
The ranking reflects the level of certainty and the presence of potential counterarguments.
4
Respond to the critique of ranking
Some argue that definitive proofs do not admit degrees. The Hanafi response is that the ranking is about practical application due to obstacles, not about the inherent authority of the ijma'.
This preserves the principle that all qat'i ijma' is binding, while acknowledging real-world complexities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كآية من الأصل الثالث الإجماع المذاهب التكفير وعدم التكفير ثالثها التكفيران كان نحو الصلاة وإلا لا (كما في المختصر تدليس إذ لا يليق بحال أحد من المسلمين أن يقول : إن إنكار الصلاة ليس كفراً (قال) الإمام فخر :الإسلام إجماع الصحابة كالمتواتر فيكفر (جاحده لفظه الشريف هكذا فصار الإجماع الكتاب أو حديث متواتر في وجوب العلم والعمل، فيكفر جاحده في الأصل ثم هو على مراتب فإجماع الصحابة مثل الآية والخبر المتواتر، ومثل لهذا الإجماع في «التحرير» بالإجماع على خلافة أمير المؤمنين إمام الصديقين بعد المرسلين أفضل الأولياء المكرمين أبي بكر الصديق رضي الله تعالى عنه وبالإجماع على قتال مانعي الزكاة مع سكوت بعضهم، فزعم أن الإجماع السكوت أيضاً كذلك، مع أن حجيته مختلف فيها بين أهل الحق، فلا يصلح مكفراً، وقال أيضاً: مطابقاً لما العلامة النسفي في المنار (والحق أن السكوتي ليس كذلك لذلك ولعل مراد صاحب «التحرير» تسوية السكوتي الذي علم بقرائن الحال أن سكوت من سكت لأجل الموافقة علماً قطعياً مع القولي والسكوت على قتال مانعي الزكاة القبيل، وإجماع من بعدهم كالمشهور فيضلل جاحده إلا ما فيه خلاف) كالإجماع بعد استقرار الخلاف فإنه يفيد الظن و (كالمنقول آحاداً ولفظه الشريف هكذا. وإجماع من بعدهم بمنزلة المشهور من الحديث، وإذا صار الإجماع مجتهداً في السلف كان كالصحيح من الإخبار، وقرروا كلامه بأن الأعلى إجماع الصحابة نصاً بحيث يكفر جاحده، ثم إجماعهم السكوتي ، ثم إجماع من بعدهم بحيث لم يسبق فيه خلاف، ثم إجماعهم وقد استقر خلاف سابق، ووجهوه بأن إجماع الصحابة غير مختلف فيه أصلاً لدخول أهل المدينة والعترة والخلفاء والشيخين والسكوتي قد اختلف فيه ثم إجماع من بعدهم لقوة الاختلاف فيه، ثم إجماعهم بعد استقرار الخلاف قد قوي فيه الاختلاف كذا قالوا وفيه نظر .

أما :أولاً: فلأن هذا يقتضي تكفير الروافض والخوارج مع قبوله شهادتهم، بل رواية الخوارج إن لم تدع إلى بدعتهم على ما هو المشهور من مذهبه .

وأما ثانياً : فلأن الأدلة الدالة على حجية الإجماع غير فارقة بين إجماع وإجماع. وأما ثالثاً : فلأن الخلاف لا يخرج القطعي عن القطعية فإنه لم يخرج فضيلة أمير المؤمنين الصديق الأكبر وخلافته، بخلاف الروافض عن القطعية وكذا فضيلة أمير المؤمنين علي، بخلاف الخوارج، والقطعيات لا تقبل شدة وضعفاً، فلا ترجيح لإجماع على آخر . أما رابعاً : فلأنه ينبغي أن يفصل في الإجماع بين ما قبل الانقراض وما بعده. وجوابه أنه لا فائدة فيه لأنه ليس إجماعهم إلا وقد انقرض عصرهم ولم يرجع أحد بما أجمعوا عليه، هذا والذي يظهر لهذا العبد في تقرير كلام هذا الخبر الإمام وإن كان أمثاله عن فهم ما أودعه هو من المرام قاصرين أن مقصوده قدس سره أن الإجماع مطلقاً في القطعية، كالآية والخبر المتواتر وأصله أن يكفر جاحده، لأنه إنكار لحكم مقطوع، إلا أنه لا يكفر لعروض عارض، وأشار إليه بتقييده بقوله في الأصل، ولذا لم يكفر الروافض والخوارج، ثم بين مراتب الإجماع، فالأعلى في القطعية إجماع الصحابة المقطوع اتفاقهم بتنصيص الكل بالحكم أو بدلالة توجب أنهم
Like a verse from the third principle: Ijma'. The schools (of thought) on takfir and non-takfir: third of them is that takfir is if it is like prayer and otherwise not (as in al-Mukhtasar, which is a deception because it is not befitting for any Muslim to say that denying prayer is not kufr). Imam Fakhr said: The ijma' of the Companions is like the mutawatir, so its denier is declared kafir. His noble words thus: so ijma' became like the Book or mutawatir hadith in obligating knowledge and action, so its denier is declared kafir in principle. Then it is of ranks: the ijma' of the Companions is like the verse and mutawatir hadith. An example of this ijma' is given in al-Tahreer: the consensus on the caliphate of the commander of the faithful, the imam of the truthful after the messengers, the best of the awliya, Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, and the consensus on fighting the zakat rejecters despite the silence of some. So he claimed that silent ijma' is also like that, although its authority is disputed among the people of truth, so it is not suitable for takfir. And he also said, matching what 'Allama al-Nasafi said in al-Manar: 'The truth is that the silent (ijma') is not like that, and perhaps the intent of the author of al-Tahreer is to equate the silent (ijma') that is known by circumstantial evidence that the silence of those who remained silent was for agreement with certainty, with the verbal, and the silence on fighting the zakat rejecters is of that type. And the ijma' of those after them is like the mashhur, so its denier is led astray except regarding what contains disagreement, like the ijma' after the establishment of disagreement, for it yields zann, and like what is transmitted singly.' And his noble words: 'And the ijma' of those after them is at the level of mashhur hadith. And ijma' becomes ijtihad among the salaf, like sahih reports.' They interpreted his words as: the highest is the ijma' of the Companions explicitly, such that its denier is declared kafir; then their silent ijma'; then the ijma' of those after them where no prior disagreement existed; then their ijma' after a prior disagreement had been established. And they justified this by saying that the ijma' of the Companions is not disputed at all because it includes the people of Madina, the 'Itra, the caliphs, and the two shaykhs; the silent ijma' has been disputed; then the ijma' of those after them due to the strength of disagreement in it; then their ijma' after the establishment of disagreement, the disagreement in it is strong. Thus they said, but there is consideration in it. As for first: because this necessitates declaring the Rawafidh and Khawarij kafir while accepting their testimony, rather the narration of the Khawarij if they do not call to their innovation, according to what is well-known of his madhhab. And as for second: because the evidences indicating the authority of ijma' do not differentiate between one ijma' and another. And as for third: because disagreement does not remove the definitive from definitiveness; for it did not remove the virtue of the commander of the faithful, the greater truthful one, and his caliphate, contrary to the Rawafidh from definitiveness; and similarly the virtue of 'Ali, contrary to the Khawarij. And definitives do not accept intensity and weakness, so no preference of one ijma' over another. As for fourth: because one should differentiate in ijma' between before extinction and after it. And the response: there is no benefit in it because there is no ijma' of theirs except that their era has ended and no one has retracted what they agreed upon. Here, and what appears to this slave in explaining the statement of this imam, even though his likes are deficient in understanding what he embedded of the intent, is that his intention (may he sanctify his secret) is that ijma' absolutely in definitiveness is like the verse and mutawatir hadith, and its origin is that its denier is declared kafir because it is denial of a definitive ruling, except that he is not declared kafir due to an intervening obstacle. He indicated this by restricting it with his saying 'in principle'. Hence the Rawafidh and Khawarij were not declared kafir. Then he explained the ranks of ijma': the highest in definitiveness is the ijma' of the Companions, whose agreement is definitively established, by explicit statement of all regarding the ruling or by an indication that necessitates that they...
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage deals with the classification of ijma' into different ranks and the corresponding ruling for those who deny each rank. The text begins by quoting Imam Fakhr al-Islam (al-Pazdawi) that the ijma' of the Companions is like a mutawatir text, making its denier a kafir. However, this is qualified by saying 'in principle' (fi al-asl), meaning that in theory denial is kufr, but practical application may be withheld due to intervening excuses. The author then presents a ranking from al-Tahreer: the highest is the textual ijma' of the Companions (declared kufr), then silent ijma' of the Companions (disputed, not kufr), then ijma' of later generations without prior disagreement (causes tadhlil 'going astray'), then ijma' after prior disagreement (yields zann, not kufr). The text then provides a critique of this ranking in four points: (1) It would require declaring Rawafidh and Khawarij kafir, which is not done; (2) The evidences for the authority of ijma' do not differentiate; (3) Definitive matters do not admit degrees; (4) Distinguishing before and after the extinction of an era is pointless. The author resolves by explaining that the ranking is not about the intrinsic authority, but about the practical application of takfir. All ijma' that is qat'i in dalala can theoretically obligate takfir, but obstacles like ta'wil (as with Rawafidh) prevent it. Thus the highest rank (ijma' of Sahaba) is simply the most clearly qat'i, but deniers may still be excused if they have a valid interpretation.

Key Terms
إجماع الصحابة (ijma' al-sahaba): Consensus of the Companions of the Prophet, considered the highest rank of ijma', transmitted explicitly or by silent agreement.
الإجماع السكوتي (al-ijma' al-sukuti): Silent consensus where some Companions remain silent while others express a ruling, indicating implicit agreement.
تضليل (tadhlil): Declaring someone astray, a lesser sanction than takfir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling in principle for one who denies the ijma' of the Companions?
A) He is declared a kafir unconditionally.
B) He is declared a kafir in principle, but obstacles may prevent actual takfir.
C) He is merely led astray (tadhlil).
D) He is not punished because it is not qat'i.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ranking of ijma' is a practical distinction that acknowledges the possibility of obstacles without undermining the authority of definitive consensus.

Al-Ijma — Consensus
intermediate
8 min
Levels of Ijma and Their Consequence
Explains hierarchy of ijma and whether rejection leads to takfir or tadlil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four levels of ijma according to Hanafi usul and determine which level, when denied, leads to takfir versus tadlil.

Core Idea
Only explicit ijma of the Companions warrants takfir; other ijma yields tadlil at most.

Ijma (consensus) is a primary source of law, but not all consensus carries the same authority. The Hanafi school carefully grades ijma by its strength of transmission and certainty. This lesson examines the hierarchy: explicit ijma of the Companions, silent ijma, ijma of later generations, and ijma after prior disagreement. Understanding this hierarchy is crucial because the legal consequence of denying an ijma—whether it be takfir (declaring disbelief) or tadlil (deeming misguided)—depends on its level.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four levels
From the passage, extract the four levels of ijma: (1) explicit ijma of the Companions, (2) silent ijma of the Companions, (3) ijma of later generations with no prior disagreement, (4) ijma after a prior disagreement or transmitted as ahad.
The passage also mentions that ijma of the Companions is like mutawatir, later ijma like mashhur, and the last two like ahad.
2
Determine epistemological weight
For each level, classify whether it provides qat'i (certain) or zanni (probable) knowledge. Explicit ijma of the Companions is qat'i; silent ijma is disputedly qat'i (but text says it requires qara'in); later ijma without prior disagreement is like mashhur (strong zann); ijma after disagreement or ahad transmission is zanni.
Hanafi principle: only qat'i evidence can justify takfir.
3
Apply the consequence principle
State the consequence: Denial of a qat'i ijma → takfir (in principle, but may be blocked by an intervening cause like the Rawafid/Khawarij). Denial of a zanni ijma → tadlil (the denier is misguided but not a disbeliever).
The passage clarifies that even for the Companions' explicit ijma, takfir is 'in principle' but not always applied due to impediments.
4
Analyze the silent ijma dispute
Examine why silent ijma is not equal to explicit ijma. The Hanafi position: silent ijma only holds if there are definitive circumstantial indications (qara'in) that silence implies approval; otherwise, it remains zanni.
The author of al-Tahrir claimed silent ijma is like explicit, but the passage refutes this by noting its disputed authority among Ahl al-Haqq.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The difference in practice: A person who denies a ruling based solely on silent ijma is not declared a disbeliever in the Hanafi school, whereas in the opposing view he might be. Hence the mufti must be careful in applying takfir.
This underscores the mercy and caution of the Hanafi school in matters of iman.

فتعلم أقوالهم بالبحث والتفتيش، فإذا أخبر جماعة عدد التواتر حصل العلم باتفاقهم قطعاً، وأما من بعدهم فتكثروا ووقع فيهم نوع من الانتشار فوقع شبهة في اتفاقهم واحتمل أن يكون هناك مجتهد لم يطلع على قوله الناقلون لكن لما كان هذا الاحتمال بعيد العدم وقوع الانتشار كون الناقلين جماعة تكفي للعلم صار بمنزلة الخبر المشهور الذي فيه احتمال بعيد، مع وصار أدون درجة من اجماع الصحابة، ثم الإجماع الذي وقع بعد تقرر الخلاف السابق حجيته ظنية لاحتمال حياة القول السابق بالدليل، وكذا الإجماع المنقول آحاداً للاحتمال في ثبوته، وكذا الإجماع الذي وقع عن سكوت ولا قرينة تدل قطعاً على أن السكوت للرضا لاحتمال عدم الموافقة، المشهور أو الآحاد والكل مقدم على الرأي.
The third principle: Ijma. The schools (madhahib) regarding takfir and non-takfir; the third is takfir if it is like prayer, otherwise not. (As in al-Mukhtasar, it is deception, for it is not befitting for any Muslim to say that denying prayer is not kufr.) (Imam Fakhr said: Islam? The ijma of the Companions is like mutawatir, so its denier is declared a disbeliever. His noble words are thus. So ijma is like the Book or mutawatir hadith in obligating knowledge and action, so its denier is declared a disbeliever in principle. Then it has levels: ijma of the Companions is like the verse and mutawatir hadith. He gave an example for this ijma in al-Tahrir: the ijma on the caliphate of the Commander of the Faithful, the Imam of the Truthful after the messengers, the best of honored saints, Abu Bakr al-Siddiq (may Allah be pleased with him), and the ijma on fighting the refusers of zakat despite the silence of some. So he claimed that silent ijma is also like that, while its authority is disputed among the people of truth, so it is not suitable as a cause for takfir. He also said, in agreement with Allamah al-Nasafi in al-Manar: The truth is that silent ijma is not like that. Therefore, perhaps the intention of the author of al-Tahrir is to equate silent ijma, when it is known through circumstantial evidence that the silence of the silent one was for agreement with certain knowledge, with explicit ijma, and the silence on fighting the refusers of zakat is of that type. And the ijma of later generations is like mashhur, so its denier is declared misguided except in cases of disagreement. Like ijma after the establishment of previous disagreement, which yields zann, and like that transmitted as ahad. And his noble words are thus. And the ijma of later generations is like mashhur hadith. And when ijma becomes a mujtahid among the early generations, it is like sahih reports. They explained his words: the highest is the ijma of the Companions by explicit statement such that its denier is declared a disbeliever; then their silent ijma; then ijma of later generations with no preceding disagreement; then their ijma after a prior disagreement was established. They justified it by saying: The ijma of the Companions is undoubted because it includes the people of Medina, the Ahl al-Bayt, the caliphs, the two shaykhs; silent ijma is disputed; then ijma of later generations due to stronger disagreement; then their ijma after stable disagreement has stronger disagreement. So they said, but there is consideration. He explicitly stated from this: Firstly: because this would necessitate declaring the Rawafid and Khawarij as disbelievers while accepting their testimony, indeed the narration of the Khawarij if they do not call to their innovation according to what is well-known of his school. Secondly: because the proofs indicating the authority of ijma do not differentiate between one ijma and another. Thirdly: because disagreement does not remove the certain from certainty, for it did not remove the virtue of the Commander of the Faithful, the Greatest Truthful, and his caliphate, unlike the Rawafid, from certainty; likewise the virtue of the Commander of the Faithful Ali, unlike the Khawarij. Certainties do not admit strength and weakness, so no preference of one ijma over another. Fourthly: because one should distinguish between ijma before extinction and after. Its response: There is no benefit in it, because their ijma only occurs after their era has ended and no one has retracted what they agreed upon. This and what appears to this servant in explaining the words of this virtuous imam, even if his likes are incapable of understanding what he deposited of intent, is that his intention (may his secret be sanctified) is that ijma absolutely is in certainty, like the verse and mutawatir hadith, and its origin is that its denier is declared a disbeliever, because it is a denial of a certain ruling, except that he is not declared a disbeliever due to an intervening cause. He indicated this by restricting his statement 'in principle.' Hence the Rawafid and Khawarij are not declared disbelievers. Then he explained the levels of ijma: the highest in certainty is the ijma of the Companions whose agreement is certain by explicit statement of all or by an indication requiring that they agreed thus, certainly. This is apparent. Then ijma of later generations; the difference is that the Companions were known individually, so their statements can be learned by research and investigation. If a group of tawatur number reports, knowledge of their agreement is attained certainly. As for later generations, they became numerous and some dispersion occurred, so doubt arose about their agreement, and it is possible that there was a mujtahid whose statement the transmitters did not know. But because this possibility is remote, and the existence of dispersion, and the transmitters being a group sufficient for knowledge, it became like mashhur hadith which has a remote possibility, and it became lower in degree than the ijma of the Companions. Then the ijma that occurred after the establishment of previous disagreement: its authority is zanni due to the possibility that the earlier opinion lives on with evidence. Similarly, ijma transmitted as ahad due to possibility in its thubut. Similarly, ijma that occurred through silence with no qarina definitively indicating that the silence was for approval, due to possibility of non-agreement. So these three possibilities become zanni proofs like a sahih ahad hadith. And to this he pointed by his statement: 'And when ijma becomes a mujtahid among the early generations,' meaning that there is no definitive proof for its authority due to the lack of certainty in the agreement therein. That is ijma after stable disagreement, ahad ijma, and silent ijma with no definitive evidence indicating it was for approval. So understand. And all ijmas are given precedence over opinion and qiyas according to most usul scholars, because it is either like mutawatir, or mashhur, or ahad, and all are given precedence over opinion.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion on the levels of ijma (consensus) and their authority. The author begins by stating that ijma is the third principle of Islamic jurisprudence. He addresses the issue of takfir (declaring disbelief) for rejecting ijma. There are three positions: some say rejection of any ijma is kufr, others say none, and a third says it depends—if the ijma is on something like prayer (clearly known from religion by necessity) then rejection is kufr, otherwise not. The author then explains that the ijma of the Companions is like a mutawatir text; its denier is a disbeliever in principle. However, not all ijma is equal. He lists the hierarchy: (1) explicit ijma of the Companions, which is definitive and its denier is a disbeliever; (2) silent ijma of the Companions, which is disputed in authority; (3) ijma of later generations with no prior disagreement, which is like mashhur hadith and its denier is misguided; (4) ijma after a prior disagreement, which yields only zann. The author critiques the view that silent ijma is equally definitive, since its authority is disputed. He then discusses the view of al-Nasafi that silent ijma is not equal to explicit ijma unless there are clear indications that silence was for approval. The passage also addresses objections: that the Rawafid and Khawarij would be declared disbelievers, but the author clarifies that they are not because of an intervening cause. The key point is that ijma is not all equal; only the definitive ijma of the Companions warrants takfir of the denier. Other forms of ijma have lesser authority, and their denier is only misguided or not blamed. The passage concludes that all forms of ijma are preferred over personal opinion and qiyas.

Key Terms
الإجماع (al-ijmā‘): The agreement of the mujtahids of the Ummah after the death of the Prophet on a religious ruling.
الإجماع السكوتي (al-ijmā‘ al-sukūtī): Consensus where some mujtahids remain silent while others explicitly state a ruling, and it is not known whether the silence indicates approval or dissent.
التضليل (al-taḍlīl): Declaring someone to be misguided (not a disbeliever) for rejecting a proof of lesser certainty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of ijma is considered definitive enough that its denier is declared a disbeliever?
A) Silent ijma of the Companions
B) Explicit ijma of the Companions
C) Ijma of later generations with no prior disagreement
D) Ijma after a prior disagreement

Key Takeaway
Only the explicit ijma of the Companions is qat'i; deniers of lesser ijma are not declared disbelievers but may be considered misguided.

The Scope of Ijma: Rational, Worldly, and Future Matters
Dispute on the scope of ijma in rational, worldly, and future matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the scope of ijma in rational, worldly, and future matters, and evaluate the opposing arguments.

Core Idea
Ijma applies to all matters—rational, worldly, and future—but practical need varies.

In this passage, the author examines a key usul dispute: does ijma's authority extend beyond purely religious rulings? Three domains are considered: rational matters (al-‘aqliyyāt), worldly matters (al-umūr al-dunyawiyya), and future/eschatological matters (al-mustaqbalāt). The Hanafi position, supported by general proofs, is that ijma is authoritative in all these areas, though the need to invoke it may differ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three domains under dispute
The passage considers rational matters (known by reason), worldly matters (e.g., military strategy), and future matters (eschatological events). For each, there is a debate about whether ijma applies.
These domains are distinct from purely religious (shar‘ī) matters, where ijma is uncontroversial.
2
Analyze the argument against ijma in rational matters
Some argue that reason suffices for knowledge in rational matters, so ijma is unnecessary. The author notes this does not deny ijma's authority, only its need.
The key distinction: 'not needed' does not mean 'not authoritative'.
3
Analyze the argument against ijma in worldly matters
Opponents cite the hadith 'You know better about your worldly affairs' to suggest that worldly matters are outside religious authority. The Hanafis respond that the hadith only shows delegation, not removal of authority, and that ijma's general proofs cover all matters.
The story of the Prophet consulting Sa‘d b. Mu‘ādh is used to show that consensus on worldly matters was considered binding.
4
Understand the Hanafi view on future matters
Hanafis say there is 'no ijma' in future matters, meaning no need to cite it, not that it is unauthoritative. Because these matters are known through definitive texts, ijtihad has no role, so ijma is not required. The author clarifies that ijma could theoretically be valid if transmitted.
This is a subtle point: ijma is still authoritative in principle, but practically unnecessary.
5
Synthesize the tarjīḥ
For all three domains, the text sides with the majority Hanafi view: ijma is authoritative (ḥujja) due to the generality of proofs. The text says 'هو الحق' (this is the truth) for each position.
The exception is rational matters that are prerequisites for ijma itself, to avoid circularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: قال (جمع) منا (لا إجماع في العقليات لأن العقل هناك كافٍ في إفادة العلم، فلا حاجة إلى الإجماع وهذا لا يدل على عدم الحجية بل غاية ما لزم عدم الحاجة إلى الإجماع لكفاية العقل (و) قال (جم) منا : يجري فيها الإجماع أيضاً (كالشرعيات) وهو الحق لعموم أدلة الحجية (إلا ما يتوقف عليه أي إلا العقليات التي يتوقف عليها الإجماع وإلا لزم الدور (وفي) الأمور الدنيوية كتدبير الجيوش لعبد الجبار المعتزلي فيه (قولان) أحدهما عدم جريان الإجماع فيه، وهو قول البعض زعماً منهم أنه لا يزيد على قول رسول الله صلى الله عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه وسلم وليس قوله حجة في الأمور الدنيوية لما قال: «أنتم أعلم بأمور دنياكم (و) ثانيهما (مختار الجماهير الإجماع فيها (حجة) أيضاً (إلى بقاء المصالح) التي أجمعوا لأجلها، وهو الحق لعموم الأدلة، وليس هو إلا كالوحي في الحجية، والوحي في الكل، ألا ترى أنه صلوات الله عليه وآله وأصحابه كيف قال حين هم بصلح الأحزاب على الثمار وشاور سعد بن معاذ وسعد بن عبادة فقالا : إن كان من الله ،فامض، قال: «لو كان من الله ما سألتكما فقالا: لا نعطي إلا السيف فلم يصالح، كذا في «الاستيعاب» (وأما في المستقبلات كأشراط الساعة وأمور الآخرة فلا إجماع عند الحنفية) يعني لا حاجة إلى الاحتجاج به لا إنه ليس حجة فيها، كيف لا والدلائل عامة لأن الغيب لا مدخل فيه للاجتهاد والرأي إذ لا يكفي فيه الظن فلا بد من دليل قطعي يدل عليه، وحينئذ لا حاجة إلى الإجماع في الاحتجاج، والحق أنه يصح الاحتجاج فيها أيضاً لتعاضد الدلائل، ولأنه احتمل أن يسمعوا كل منفرداً، فاجمعوا على ما سمعوا ولم ينقلوا لوجود الاتفاق، فيفيد هذا الإجماع لنا ولا يفيد ذلك القاطع لعدم بقاء تواتره فالحق إذن إنّ المستقبلات من الأخبار كالشرعيات في الثبوت بالإجماع (هذا) والله يقول الحق وهو يهدي السبيل.
Issue: A group among us said: There is no ijma in rational matters because reason there is sufficient for acquiring knowledge, so there is no need for ijma. This does not indicate the absence of authority, but rather the utmost required is the lack of need for ijma due to the sufficiency of reason. Another group among us said: Ijma applies in rational matters too, like in religious matters. And this is the truth because of the generality of the proofs of authority, except for those rational matters that ijma depends on, otherwise circularity would occur. Regarding worldly matters such as army management, from Abd al-Jabbar al-Mutazili there are two opinions: one is the non-applicability of ijma therein, which is the view of some, claiming that it does not exceed the statement of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him and his family and companions) and that his statement is not authoritative in worldly matters because he said: 'You know better about your worldly affairs.' The second opinion is the choice of the majority: ijma is authoritative in worldly matters too, as long as the interests for which they agreed remain. This is the truth because of the generality of proofs, and it is like revelation in authority, and revelation applies to everything. Do you not see that he (peace be upon him and his family and companions) said when he intended to make peace with the Confederates over dates and consulted Sa'd b. Mu'adh and Sa'd b. Ubada, and they said: 'If it is from Allah, then proceed.' He said: 'If it were from Allah, I would not have asked you.' So they said: 'We will not give anything except the sword,' and he did not make peace. That is in al-Isti'ab. As for future matters such as the signs of the Hour and matters of the Hereafter, there is no ijma according to the Hanafis, meaning there is no need to argue by it, not that it is not authoritative therein. Why not, since the proofs are general? Because the unseen has no room for ijtihad and opinion, as zann is not sufficient, so a definitive proof is necessary. In that case, there is no need for ijma in arguing. The truth is that arguing by it is valid in these matters too, due to the mutual support of proofs, and because it is possible that they each heard something individually and agreed upon what they heard, and they did not transmit it due to the existence of agreement. So this ijma benefits us, but it does not benefit that definitive proof due to its tawatur not remaining. Therefore the truth is that future matters among the reports are like religious matters in being established by ijma. This, and Allah says the truth and He guides to the path.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (usul dispute) regarding the scope of ijma. It begins by stating an issue: whether ijma applies in rational matters (aqliyat). The text presents two views: some scholars say no, because reason alone can provide knowledge, so ijma is unnecessary. Others say yes, ijma applies just as in religious matters, due to the general proofs establishing ijma's authority. The author sides with the latter, calling it the truth, but makes an exception for those rational matters that are prerequisites for ijma itself, to avoid circular reasoning. Then the discussion moves to worldly matters (such as military strategy). Here the text references Abd al-Jabbar al-Mutazili, who reports two opinions: some say ijma does not apply because the Prophet's statement 'You know better about your worldly affairs' indicates that such matters are left to human expertise and not subject to religious authority. The majority, however, hold that ijma is authoritative in worldly matters as long as the interests (masalih) for which the consensus was formed remain. The author supports the majority view, arguing that revelation applies to all matters and citing the incident of the Prophet consulting his companions about the peace treaty with the Confederates. Finally, the passage discusses future matters (mustaqbalat) such as eschatological signs. The Hanafi position is that ijma is not used in these matters, not because it lacks authority, but because there is no need for it: such matters are known through definitive textual proofs (naqli qati), and ijtihad/opinion has no role. However, the author clarifies that in reality, ijma can still be valid in these areas if the Companions heard reports individually and agreed upon them, but this would not be a transmitted ijma that benefits later generations. So the truth according to the author is that future matters are like religious matters in that they can be established by ijma, but in practice the need for it is absent because definitive texts already exist.

Key Terms
العقليات (al-‘aqliyyāt): Matters that can be known through reason alone, such as the existence of the Creator or the necessity of prophethood.
المستقبلات (al-mustaqbalāt): Future matters, especially eschatological events like the signs of the Hour and affairs of the Hereafter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Ijma is completely invalid in future matters.
D) Ijma is the only source for future matters.

Key Takeaway
Ijma's authority extends to all domains—rational, worldly, and future—but the need to invoke it varies based on the availability of other proofs.

Types of Ijma and Their Evidentiary Value
Classifies ijma into types with different levels of authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the types of ijma based on their evidentiary weight (qat'i vs zanni) and explain why each type has the authority it does.

Core Idea
Ijma varies in strength: companions' consensus is qat'i, later consensus is like mashhur, and consensus after disagreement or silence is zanni.

In this passage, the author classifies ijma (consensus) into different categories based on the level of certainty they provide. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for evaluating legal evidence in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. Not all ijma carries the same weight; some are definitive (qat'i) while others are probable (zanni), akin to different types of hadith reports.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classification criterion
The author distinguishes ijma based on the historical period (Companions vs. later scholars) and the mode of transmission (tawatur, mashhur, ahad, silence). This determines the epistemological certainty.
The Companions were known individually, making their consensus verifiable.
2
Analyze the evidentiary weight of each type
Companions' consensus reported by tawatur is qat'i (definitive). Later scholars' consensus is like mashhur (zanni but strong). Consensus after prior disagreement, reported by ahad, or silent consensus without qarinah are zanni like a solitary hadith.
The author compares each type to a category of hadith.
3
Understand the reasoning behind the weight
For later scholars, dispersion raises doubt. For consensus after disagreement, the earlier opinion might have been revived. For ahad reports, transmission uncertainty. For silent consensus, silence may not indicate approval.
Each zanni type has a specific reason for its lower certainty.
4
Apply the classification
When encountering a claim of ijma, first determine which category it falls into to assess its probative value. This is essential for istidlal (legal reasoning).
The mufti or mujtahid must weigh the ijma accordingly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قطعاً. وهذا ظاهر، ثم اجماع من بعدهم وجه الفرق أن الصحابة كانوا معلومين بأعيانهم فتعلم أقوالهم بالبحث والتفتيش، فإذا أخبر جماعة عدد التواتر حصل العلم باتفاقهم قطعاً، وأما من بعدهم فتكثروا ووقع فيهم نوع من الانتشار فوقع شبهة في اتفاقهم واحتمل أن يكون هناك مجتهد لم يطلع على قوله الناقلون لكن لما كان هذا الاحتمال بعيد العدم وقوع الانتشار كون الناقلين جماعة تكفي للعلم صار بمنزلة الخبر المشهور الذي فيه احتمال بعيد، مع وصار أدون درجة من اجماع الصحابة، ثم الإجماع الذي وقع بعد تقرر الخلاف السابق حجيته ظنية لاحتمال حياة القول السابق بالدليل، وكذا الإجماع المنقول آحاداً للاحتمال في ثبوته، وكذا الإجماع الذي وقع عن سكوت ولا قرينة تدل قطعاً على أن السكوت للرضا لاحتمال عدم الموافقة، فصارت هذه للاحتمالات الثلاثة حجة ظنية كخبر الواحد الصحيح، وإلى هذا أشار بقوله : وإذا صار الإجماع مجتهداً في السلف يعني لا يكون على حجيته، دليل قاطع لعدم ثبوت الاتفاق فيه قطعاً، وهو الإجماع بعد استقرار الخلاف، والاجماع الأحادي والإجماع السكوتي مع عدم دلالة الدليل على القاطع على كونه بالرضا فافهم (والكل) من الاجماعات مقدم على الرأي والقياس (عند الأكثر من أهل الأصول، لأنه إما بمنزلة الخبر المتواتر أو المشهور أو الآحاد والكل مقدم على الرأي.

حجة
مسألة: قال (جمع) منا (لا إجماع في العقليات لأن العقل هناك كافٍ في إفادة العلم، فلا حاجة إلى الإجماع وهذا لا يدل على عدم الحجية بل غاية ما لزم عدم الحاجة إلى الإجماع لكفاية العقل (و) قال (جم) منا : يجري فيها الإجماع أيضاً (كالشرعيات) وهو الحق لعموم أدلة الحجية (إلا ما يتوقف عليه أي إلا العقليات التي يتوقف عليها الإجماع وإلا لزم الدور (وفي) الأمور الدنيوية كتدبير الجيوش لعبد الجبار المعتزلي فيه (قولان) أحدهما عدم جريان الإجماع فيه، وهو قول البعض زعماً منهم أنه لا يزيد على قول رسول الله صلى الله عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه وسلم وليس قوله حجة في الأمور الدنيوية لما قال: «أنتم أعلم بأمور دنياكم (و) ثانيهما (مختار الجماهير الإجماع فيها (حجة) أيضاً (إلى بقاء المصالح) التي أجمعوا لأجلها، وهو الحق لعموم الأدلة، وليس هو إلا كالوحي في الحجية، والوحي في الكل، ألا ترى أنه صلوات الله عليه وآله وأصحابه كيف قال حين هم بصلح الأحزاب على الثمار وشاور سعد بن معاذ وسعد بن عبادة فقالا : إن كان من الله ،فامض، قال: «لو كان من الله ما سألتكما فقالا: لا نعطي إلا السيف فلم يصالح، كذا في «الاستيعاب» (وأما في المستقبلات كأشراط الساعة وأمور الآخرة فلا إجماع عند الحنفية) يعني لا حاجة إلى الاحتجاج به لا إنه ليس حجة فيها، كيف لا والدلائل عامة لأن الغيب لا مدخل فيه للاجتهاد والرأي إذ لا يكفي فيه الظن فلا بد من دليل قطعي يدل عليه، وحينئذ لا حاجة إلى الإجماع في الاحتجاج، والحق أنه يصح الاحتجاج فيها أيضاً لتعاضد الدلائل، ولأنه احتمل أن يسمعوا كل منفرداً، فاجمعوا على ما سمعوا ولم ينقلوا لوجود الاتفاق، فيفيد هذا الإجماع لنا ولا يفيد ذلك القاطع لعدم بقاء تواتره فالحق إذن إنّ المستقبلات من الأخبار كالشرعيات في الثبوت بالإجماع (هذا) والله يقول الحق وهو يهدي السبيل.
Definitely. And this is apparent, then the consensus of those after them—the difference is that the Companions were known individually, so their statements are learned by research and investigation. So if a group of the number of tawatur report, knowledge of their agreement is obtained definitively. As for those after them, they multiplied and a type of dispersion occurred among them, so a doubt arose about their agreement, and it is probable that there is a mujtahid whose statement the transmitters did not come across. But because this probability is far from non-occurrence of dispersion, and the transmitters are a group sufficient for knowledge, it becomes like the mashhur report which contains a remote probability, and it becomes lower in degree than the consensus of the Companions. Then the consensus that occurs after the establishment of previous disagreement—its probative value is zanni due to the possibility that the previous opinion was abandoned by evidence. Likewise, consensus reported by ahad due to the possibility regarding its establishment. Likewise, consensus that occurs through silence without a definitive indication that silence is for approval, due to the possibility of lack of agreement. So these three types become a zanni proof like a sound solitary report. And to this the author referred by his statement: 'When consensus becomes a mujtahid among the Salaf' meaning it does not have definitive probative value due to lack of certain establishment of agreement—that is consensus after settlement of disagreement, consensus reported by ahad, and silent consensus without definitive evidence indicating that it is for approval. So understand. (And all) types of consensus take precedence over opinion and analogy (according to most of the scholars of usul, because it is either like the mutawatir report, the mashhur, or the ahad, and all take precedence over opinion.)

Proof: Issue: A group of us said: No consensus in intellectual matters because the intellect is sufficient there for producing knowledge, so there is no need for consensus. This does not indicate lack of probative value; rather, all that follows is no need for consensus due to sufficiency of intellect. And a group of us said: Consensus applies in them as well, like in legal matters, and that is the truth due to generality of proofs of probative value, except what depends on it—i.e., except intellectual matters that consensus depends on, otherwise circularity would occur. And in worldly matters like the management of armies, according to Abd al-Jabbar al-Mu'tazili, there are two opinions: one is that consensus does not apply, which is the view of some, claiming that it does not add to the statement of the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions) and his statement is not proof in worldly matters because he said: 'You know better your worldly affairs.' The second opinion is the choice of the majority: consensus is proof in them as well, due to preservation of the interests for which they agreed, and it is the truth due to generality of proofs. It is only like revelation in probative value, and revelation applies to all. Do you not see that he (peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions) when he intended to make peace with the clans over dates and consulted Sa'd ibn Mu'adh and Sa'd ibn 'Ubada, they said: 'If it is from Allah, proceed.' He said: 'If it were from Allah I would not have asked you.' So they said: 'We will give nothing but the sword,' and he did not make peace. As in al-Isti'ab. (As for future matters like signs of the Hour and matters of the Hereafter, there is no consensus according to the Hanafis) meaning there is no need to argue by it, not that it is not proof in them. How could that be when the proofs are general? Because the unseen has no room for ijtihad and opinion, since zann is not sufficient for it; so a definitive proof must indicate it. In that case, there is no need for consensus in argumentation. The truth is that it is valid to argue by it in these matters as well, due to mutual support of proofs, and because it is possible that they heard each individually and agreed on what they heard and did not transmit due to the existence of agreement. So this consensus benefits us, but that definitive benefit does not remain because its tawatur is not preserved. So the truth is that future reports are like legal matters in being established by consensus. This. And Allah says the truth and He guides to the way.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a classification and evaluation of different types of ijma (consensus) in terms of their epistemological weight. The author first distinguishes between the consensus of the Companions (Sahaba) and that of later generations. The Companions were known individually, so if a tawatur-level number reports their agreement, it yields definitive knowledge (qat'i). Later scholars are numerous and dispersed, so their consensus is like a mashhur report—zanni but strong. Then the author lists three types of ijma that are only zanni: (1) consensus after a prior disagreement (possibility that the earlier opinion may have been revived), (2) consensus reported through ahad transmissions (uncertainty of report), and (3) silent consensus without clear evidence that silence indicates approval (possibility of silent dissent). All these are like a sound solitary report in probative value. The author also discusses whether consensus applies to intellectual, worldly, and future matters. The Hanafi position is that consensus is not needed for intellectual matters (intellect suffices) but it can apply; in worldly matters, most say it is proof; in future matters (e.g., eschatology), Hanafis say no need for consensus because only definitive proof suffices, but some argue it can still be used if supported. The author affirms that all types of consensus take precedence over personal opinion and qiyas.

Key Terms
إجماع (ijma'): Consensus of the mujtahids of the ummah on a legal ruling after the death of the Prophet ﷺ.
إجماع سكوتي (ijma' sukuti): Consensus where some mujtahids remain silent after others speak, without explicit approval.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Ijma varies in authority: companions' consensus is qat'i, later types are zanni, and all types are superior to opinion and qiyas.

The Scope of Ijma: Intellectual, Worldly, and Future Matters
Discusses the scope of ijma in intellectual, worldly, and future matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether ijma applies to intellectual, worldly, and future matters, and state the Hanafi position on each.

Core Idea
Ijma is valid in legal matters, not needed in intellectual matters because intellect suffices, and not used in future eschatological matters because only definitive proof works.

Having established the types of ijma, the passage now addresses the proper domains of consensus. There is disagreement among scholars whether ijma can operate in non-legal areas such as intellectual truths, worldly affairs like military strategy, and future events like the signs of the Hour. The Hanafi position is nuanced: ijma is not needed where intellect or definitive proof exists, but it can still be used as corroboration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three domains discussed
The author discusses: (1) intellectual matters (aqliyat), (2) worldly/administrative matters (dunya), (3) future/eschatological matters (mustaqbalat). Each has a different treatment regarding the applicability of ijma.
These are extra-legal areas where ijma's role is debated.
2
Analyze the Hanafi position on intellectual matters
The Hanafis hold that ijma is not needed in intellectual matters because intellect alone suffices for knowledge. However, this does not negate the probative value of ijma; it simply indicates no need.
Some scholars, like the author, argue that ijma does apply in intellectual matters as well.
3
Examine the dispute on worldly matters
There are two opinions: some say ijma does not apply because the Prophet ﷺ said 'You know better your worldly affairs.' The majority (including the author) hold that ijma is proof in worldly matters to preserve interests, and they support it with the incident of the Prophet consulting Sa'd ibn Mu'adh.
The majority view is that ijma in worldly matters is valid as long as the interests remain.
4
Understand the Hanafi view on future matters
The Hanafis say there is no need for ijma in future matters (eschatology) because only definitive proof (qat'i) suffices, and ijma in these matters is not established due to lack of tawatur. But the author notes that ijma could still be used if it is supported by multiple proofs.
The truth, according to the author, is that ijma can be valid in future matters as well.

Source Text
