قطعاً. وهذا ظاهر، ثم اجماع من بعدهم وجه الفرق أن الصحابة كانوا معلومين بأعيانهم فتعلم أقوالهم بالبحث والتفتيش، فإذا أخبر جماعة عدد التواتر حصل العلم باتفاقهم قطعاً، وأما من بعدهم فتكثروا ووقع فيهم نوع من الانتشار فوقع شبهة في اتفاقهم واحتمل أن يكون هناك مجتهد لم يطلع على قوله الناقلون لكن لما كان هذا الاحتمال بعيد العدم وقوع الانتشار كون الناقلين جماعة تكفي للعلم صار بمنزلة الخبر المشهور الذي فيه احتمال بعيد، مع وصار أدون درجة من اجماع الصحابة، ثم الإجماع الذي وقع بعد تقرر الخلاف السابق حجيته ظنية لاحتمال حياة القول السابق بالدليل، وكذا الإجماع المنقول آحاداً للاحتمال في ثبوته، وكذا الإجماع الذي وقع عن سكوت ولا قرينة تدل قطعاً على أن السكوت للرضا لاحتمال عدم الموافقة، فصارت هذه للاحتمالات الثلاثة حجة ظنية كخبر الواحد الصحيح، وإلى هذا أشار بقوله : وإذا صار الإجماع مجتهداً في السلف يعني لا يكون على حجيته، دليل قاطع لعدم ثبوت الاتفاق فيه قطعاً، وهو الإجماع بعد استقرار الخلاف، والاجماع الأحادي والإجماع السكوتي مع عدم دلالة الدليل على القاطع على كونه بالرضا فافهم (والكل) من الاجماعات مقدم على الرأي والقياس (عند الأكثر من أهل الأصول، لأنه إما بمنزلة الخبر المتواتر أو المشهور أو الآحاد والكل مقدم على الرأي.

حجة
مسألة: قال (جمع) منا (لا إجماع في العقليات لأن العقل هناك كافٍ في إفادة العلم، فلا حاجة إلى الإجماع وهذا لا يدل على عدم الحجية بل غاية ما لزم عدم الحاجة إلى الإجماع لكفاية العقل (و) قال (جم) منا : يجري فيها الإجماع أيضاً (كالشرعيات) وهو الحق لعموم أدلة الحجية (إلا ما يتوقف عليه أي إلا العقليات التي يتوقف عليها الإجماع وإلا لزم الدور (وفي) الأمور الدنيوية كتدبير الجيوش لعبد الجبار المعتزلي فيه (قولان) أحدهما عدم جريان الإجماع فيه، وهو قول البعض زعماً منهم أنه لا يزيد على قول رسول الله صلى الله عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه وسلم وليس قوله حجة في الأمور الدنيوية لما قال: «أنتم أعلم بأمور دنياكم (و) ثانيهما (مختار الجماهير الإجماع فيها (حجة) أيضاً (إلى بقاء المصالح) التي أجمعوا لأجلها، وهو الحق لعموم الأدلة، وليس هو إلا كالوحي في الحجية، والوحي في الكل، ألا ترى أنه صلوات الله عليه وآله وأصحابه كيف قال حين هم بصلح الأحزاب على الثمار وشاور سعد بن معاذ وسعد بن عبادة فقالا : إن كان من الله ،فامض، قال: «لو كان من الله ما سألتكما فقالا: لا نعطي إلا السيف فلم يصالح، كذا في «الاستيعاب» (وأما في المستقبلات كأشراط الساعة وأمور الآخرة فلا إجماع عند الحنفية) يعني لا حاجة إلى الاحتجاج به لا إنه ليس حجة فيها، كيف لا والدلائل عامة لأن الغيب لا مدخل فيه للاجتهاد والرأي إذ لا يكفي فيه الظن فلا بد من دليل قطعي يدل عليه، وحينئذ لا حاجة إلى الإجماع في الاحتجاج، والحق أنه يصح الاحتجاج فيها أيضاً لتعاضد الدلائل، ولأنه احتمل أن يسمعوا كل منفرداً، فاجمعوا على ما سمعوا ولم ينقلوا لوجود الاتفاق، فيفيد هذا الإجماع لنا ولا يفيد ذلك القاطع لعدم بقاء تواتره فالحق إذن إنّ المستقبلات من الأخبار كالشرعيات في الثبوت بالإجماع (هذا) والله يقول الحق وهو يهدي السبيل.
Definitely. And this is apparent, then the consensus of those after them—the difference is that the Companions were known individually, so their statements are learned by research and investigation. So if a group of the number of tawatur report, knowledge of their agreement is obtained definitively. As for those after them, they multiplied and a type of dispersion occurred among them, so a doubt arose about their agreement, and it is probable that there is a mujtahid whose statement the transmitters did not come across. But because this probability is far from non-occurrence of dispersion, and the transmitters are a group sufficient for knowledge, it becomes like the mashhur report which contains a remote probability, and it becomes lower in degree than the consensus of the Companions. Then the consensus that occurs after the establishment of previous disagreement—its probative value is zanni due to the possibility that the previous opinion was abandoned by evidence. Likewise, consensus reported by ahad due to the possibility regarding its establishment. Likewise, consensus that occurs through silence without a definitive indication that silence is for approval, due to the possibility of lack of agreement. So these three types become a zanni proof like a sound solitary report. And to this the author referred by his statement: 'When consensus becomes a mujtahid among the Salaf' meaning it does not have definitive probative value due to lack of certain establishment of agreement—that is consensus after settlement of disagreement, consensus reported by ahad, and silent consensus without definitive evidence indicating that it is for approval. So understand. (And all) types of consensus take precedence over opinion and analogy (according to most of the scholars of usul, because it is either like the mutawatir report, the mashhur, or the ahad, and all take precedence over opinion.)

Proof: Issue: A group of us said: No consensus in intellectual matters because the intellect is sufficient there for producing knowledge, so there is no need for consensus. This does not indicate lack of probative value; rather, all that follows is no need for consensus due to sufficiency of intellect. And a group of us said: Consensus applies in them as well, like in legal matters, and that is the truth due to generality of proofs of probative value, except what depends on it—i.e., except intellectual matters that consensus depends on, otherwise circularity would occur. And in worldly matters like the management of armies, according to Abd al-Jabbar al-Mu'tazili, there are two opinions: one is that consensus does not apply, which is the view of some, claiming that it does not add to the statement of the Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions) and his statement is not proof in worldly matters because he said: 'You know better your worldly affairs.' The second opinion is the choice of the majority: consensus is proof in them as well, due to preservation of the interests for which they agreed, and it is the truth due to generality of proofs. It is only like revelation in probative value, and revelation applies to all. Do you not see that he (peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions) when he intended to make peace with the clans over dates and consulted Sa'd ibn Mu'adh and Sa'd ibn 'Ubada, they said: 'If it is from Allah, proceed.' He said: 'If it were from Allah I would not have asked you.' So they said: 'We will give nothing but the sword,' and he did not make peace. As in al-Isti'ab. (As for future matters like signs of the Hour and matters of the Hereafter, there is no consensus according to the Hanafis) meaning there is no need to argue by it, not that it is not proof in them. How could that be when the proofs are general? Because the unseen has no room for ijtihad and opinion, since zann is not sufficient for it; so a definitive proof must indicate it. In that case, there is no need for consensus in argumentation. The truth is that it is valid to argue by it in these matters as well, due to mutual support of proofs, and because it is possible that they heard each individually and agreed on what they heard and did not transmit due to the existence of agreement. So this consensus benefits us, but that definitive benefit does not remain because its tawatur is not preserved. So the truth is that future reports are like legal matters in being established by consensus. This. And Allah says the truth and He guides to the way.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a classification and evaluation of different types of ijma (consensus) in terms of their epistemological weight. The author first distinguishes between the consensus of the Companions (Sahaba) and that of later generations. The Companions were known individually, so if a tawatur-level number reports their agreement, it yields definitive knowledge (qat'i). Later scholars are numerous and dispersed, so their consensus is like a mashhur report—zanni but strong. Then the author lists three types of ijma that are only zanni: (1) consensus after a prior disagreement (possibility that the earlier opinion may have been revived), (2) consensus reported through ahad transmissions (uncertainty of report), and (3) silent consensus without clear evidence that silence indicates approval (possibility of silent dissent). All these are like a sound solitary report in probative value. The author also discusses whether consensus applies to intellectual, worldly, and future matters. The Hanafi position is that consensus is not needed for intellectual matters (intellect suffices) but it can apply; in worldly matters, most say it is proof; in future matters (e.g., eschatology), Hanafis say no need for consensus because only definitive proof suffices, but some argue it can still be used if supported. The author affirms that all types of consensus take precedence over personal opinion and qiyas.

Key Terms
عقليات (aqliyat): Intellectual matters that can be known by reason alone, independent of revelation.
دنيويات (dunyawiyat): Worldly or administrative matters such as military strategy, agriculture, etc.
مستقبلات (mustaqbalat): Future matters, especially eschatological events like the signs of the Hour.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi position on ijma in worldly matters?
A) Ijma is not applicable because worldly matters are left to human expertise.
B) Ijma is applicable and is a proof according to the majority.
C) Ijma is valid only if the Prophet's statement is not involved.
D) Ijma is applicable only in legal matters, not in worldly affairs.

Key Takeaway
Ijma extends beyond strictly legal matters: it is not needed in intellectual matters, is valid in worldly affairs, and may be used in future matters if properly supported.

The Condition: 'Illah Must Not Contradict Nass or Ijma
Condition on the validity of 'illah in qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition that the 'illah must not contradict a text or consensus to evaluate the validity of a qiyas argument.

Core Idea
The 'illah that defies nass or ijma is null — qiyas cannot override revelation.

In Hanafi usul, the cause ('illah) used for analogical reasoning must satisfy several conditions. One crucial condition is that the 'illah does not contradict an explicit text (nass) or consensus (ijma). This lesson unpacks that condition and shows why violating it invalidates the qiyas, using the example of a hypothetical 'illah that restricts expiation fasting to a rich king.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
The passage states a condition: the 'illah (cause) in qiyas must not contradict a nass (clear text) or ijma (consensus). If it does, the qiyas is invalid.
This is one of several conditions for a valid 'illah.
2
Understand the example
The example given: someone proposes that only a rich king must fast for expiation, using the 'illah of deterrence (because the king needs stronger deterrence). However, the general texts on expiation (e.g., Quran 5:89) do not single out any person; they apply broadly.
The 'illah of deterrence, when applied only to the king, contradicts the general ruling.
3
Explain the conflict
The 'illah attempts to restrict a general ruling (expiation fasting for all) to a specific person. This contradicts the nass which is general. Since the nass is clear and unambiguous, the 'illah is invalid.
Hanafi usul holds that qiyas cannot restrict or override a nass.
4
Apply the condition
When evaluating a qiyas, check whether the proposed 'illah would lead to a ruling that contradicts any explicit text or established consensus. If it does, the qiyas is rejected.
This condition safeguards the primacy of revelation over human reasoning.
5
Note epistemological weight
The condition shows that nass and ijma are qat'i (certain) in authority, while qiyas is zanni (speculative). Therefore, any conflict must be resolved in favor of the revealed sources.
This is a core principle in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) منها (أن لا يخالف نصاً ولا إجماعاً، كإيجاب الصوم على الملك المرفه خاصة في الكفارة) بتعليل الزجر، فإنه مبطل
(And) from them (is that it does not contradict a text nor consensus, like making fasting obligatory on the luxuriant king specifically in expiation) by the cause of deterrence, for it is invalidating.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This short condition states that the 'illah used in qiyas must not contradict a text (nass) or consensus (ijma). The example: if someone argues using a deterrence 'illah that only a rich king must fast in expiation, that would contradict the general text of the Quran or hadith that prescribes expiation without such specificity. Such an 'illah would be invalid because it goes against the established ruling. The Hanafi school strictly prohibits any 'illah that leads to a ruling opposed to an explicit textual proof or agreed upon practice.

Key Terms
نص (nass): A clear and unambiguous textual proof from Quran or Sunnah.
إجماع (ijma): Consensus of the scholars of the community on a legal ruling.
علة ('illah): The effective cause or ratio legis that justifies a ruling in qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if an 'illah (cause) contradicts an explicit text (nass) according to the Hanafi school?
A) The 'illah is preferred if it is stronger.
B) The 'illah is invalid and cannot be used.
C) The text is interpreted metaphorically.
D) Both are applied in different situations.

Key Takeaway
A valid 'illah must never contradict a clear text or consensus; otherwise the qiyas is null.

The First Two Masalik: Ijma' and Nass for 'Illah
Explanation of ijma' and nass as methods to identify the 'illah in qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first two masalik (ijma' and nass) for establishing the 'illah in qiyas and apply the hierarchy of textual indicators to determine the strength of 'illah from a given text.

Core Idea
The 'illah must be proven by consensus or explicit textual particles that denote causality.

This lesson introduces the first two of several methods (masalik) used to establish the effective cause ('illah) in analogical reasoning (qiyas). These methods are consensus (ijma') and explicit textual indication (nass). Understanding their hierarchy is essential for deriving rulings consistently within the Hanafi school. We will explore how ijma' can specify the 'illah and how textual particles like li-ajl and fa' vary in strength.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the need for proof of 'illah
The 'illah is not self-evident; it requires a proof (dalil) called a maslak. The passage starts by noting that the majority hold that 'illah is theoretical and needs proof, except the Mu'tazila who allow intuitive 'illah. Identify that the maslak is the method to prove causality.
2
Understand ijma' as a maslak
The first maslak is ijma' (consensus) on the specification of the ‘illah. The passage gives examples: minority as ‘illah for guardianship of property (consensus), and the combination of causes in inheritance (full brother preferred). Even zanni ijma' (speculative consensus) is binding but can be opposed if a preventer is claimed.
3
Understand nass as a maslak
The second maslak is nass (explicit text) which indicates the ‘illah by linguistic convention. The passage lists a hierarchy of particles from strongest to weakest: li-ajl, kay, idhan, then lam, ba', then in (with kasra and tashdeed), and finally fa' which only indicates sequence.
4
Apply the hierarchy of textual indicators
Given a textual statement containing a particle, determine its strength in indicating causality. For example, li-ajl is the strongest; fa' is the weakest. Practice by ranking particles from the passage.

Source Text

لكن الغلبة للعلة (نظرية ، وعند المعتزلة، وإن جاز البديهية لكنه نادر جداً فلا بد من دليل على العلية (وهو) المسلك، وذلك أنواع : ( المسلك (الأول) الإجماع على تعيين العلة كالصغر في ولاية المال فإن عليته مجمع عليه وامتزاج) السببين في تقديم الأخ عيناً على الأخ لأب في الأرث، فيقاس ولاية (النكاح على الأصلين، فيقال : الصغير الثيب والبكر مولى عليها في النكاح، كما أنها مولى عليها في المال والجامع الصغير، ويقال الأخ العيني مقدم في ولاية النكاح على العلاني، كما إنه مقدم عليه في الإرث بجامع اجتماع السببين (ولا يختلف في الفرع بعد تسليمه أي بعد تسليم وجود العلة فيه لأن تسليم الموجب يوجب تسليم الموجب (وإن كان الإجماع (ظنياً) أي مظنوناً، كالسكوتي والمنقول آحاداً لأن الظن واجب الاعتبار، فيجب اتباعه وفيه ما فيه كذا في الحاشية وجهه أنه لما كان مظنوناً صح مخالفته بالاجتهاد وعدم تسليم الحجية (إلا) بادعاء مانع من الحكم فحينئذ يختلف فيه . However, the majority is for 'illah (theoretical, and according to the Mu'tazila, even if immediate [knowledge] is possible, it is very rare). So there must be a proof for causality (which is) the maslak. And its types: (The first maslak: Ijma' on the specification of the 'illah, like minority in guardianship of property, for its causality is agreed upon. And the mixing of the two causes in preferring the full brother over the consanguine brother in inheritance. So guardianship of marriage is analogized to the two origins. It is said: The minor, whether thayyib or virgin, is under guardianship in marriage, just as she is under guardianship in property, and the common element is minority. And it is said: The full brother is preferred in guardianship of marriage over the consanguine brother, just as he is preferred in inheritance due to the combination of the two causes. And there is no disagreement in the branch after its acceptance, i.e., after accepting the existence of the 'illah in it, because accepting the cause necessitates accepting the effect. And even if ijma' is zanni (speculative), like silent ijma' and that transmitted by ahad, because speculation is considered binding, so it must be followed. And there is some issue in it, as stated in the Hashiyah. Its justification: Because it is speculative, it can be opposed by ijtihad and non-acceptance of its authority, except if a preventer of the ruling is claimed, then it is disputed. (And) the second maslak: Nass (text), which is explicit, and it is that which indicates the 'illah by linguistic convention, as in the Hashiyah. And it has levels in indicating the 'illah in strength and weakness: (Its highest is li-ajl) like his statement: 'The permission to enter was only made for the sake of sight' quoted in the Hashiyah, narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah, and 'min ajl' as in the authentic narrations: 'The permission to enter was only made for the sake of looking.' (And kay) like His saying: 'so that she may be comforted' [Qasas:13]. (And idhan) like his statement in response to Dihyah when he said: 'Shall I make all my prayer for you?' 'Then your concern will be sufficient and your sin will be forgiven' narrated by Ahmad in a long hadith. (And below it) i.e., this type (the lam) like His saying: 'A book We have sent down to you so that you may bring people from darkness to light' [Ibrahim:1]. And it is lower than the first because it is also used for other than 'illah, like consequence, but that does not prevent apparentness as he said. And 'consequence' is a metaphorical meaning that does not prevent apparentness. (And the ba') like His saying: 'So by mercy from Allah you were lenient with them' [Aal-e-Imran:159]. And 'in' with kasra and without tashdeed. And they (lam and ba') are lower because they are commonly used for accompaniment, but that does not prevent apparentness as he said. And mere istishab (presumption of continuity) in the understood meaning is contrary to common usage, so it is not adopted. Like His saying: 'Perhaps you would kill yourself with grief if they do not believe in this message' [Zukhruf:5] with 'in' kasra without tashdeed in the recitation of Nafi', Abu Ja'far, al-Kisa'i, Hamzah, and Khalaf. As for the others, it is with fatha and the lam is implied. And 'in' with kasra and tashdeed after a sentence, as stated by Abd al-Qahir as well, like His saying: 'And I do not claim my soul to be innocent; indeed the soul is prone to evil' [Yusuf:53]. As for 'in' with fatha, it is with an implied lam, i.e., it is for 'illah by an implied lam, not for 'illah by itself. (And below it) i.e., this type (the fa') is weaker in indicating it, rather it only indicates mere succession in

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the methods (masalik) of establishing the 'illah for qiyas. It begins by stating that the majority view holds that 'illah is theoretical and requires a proof, except for the Mu'tazila who allow intuitive 'illah. The proof is the maslak, and the first two types are ijma' and nass. The first maslak is ijma' (consensus) on the 'illah, illustrated by the example of minority as the 'illah for guardianship of property, since scholars agree on that. Then, using analogy, guardianship of marriage is analogized to minority (common element) and also to the combination of causes in inheritance (full brother preferred due to two causes). The text notes that even zanni ijma' (like silent ijma' or ahad reports) is binding in practice, though a scholar may oppose it by ijtihad if a preventer is claimed. The second maslak is nass (explicit text), which indicates the 'illah by linguistic convention. The text then categorizes the levels of strength in text-based 'illah indications: the strongest is the use of 'li-ajl' (for the sake of), with examples from hadith about permission to enter for sight. Next is 'kay' (in order that), as in the Quranic verse about comforting. Next is 'idhan' (then/so), from the hadith with Dihyah. Then comes the lam (the lām of purpose), like in the verse about bringing people from darkness to light; it is slightly weaker because it can also be used for consequence (lām al-āqibah). Then the bā' (preposition) as in the verse about mercy, which is used for accompaniment idiomatically. The text also discusses the use of 'in' (if) with kasra and tashdeed or without, explaining when it implies 'illah. Finally, the fa' (then/so) is the weakest, only indicating sequence, not necessarily causality. The discussion ends mid-sentence here, but the principle is clear: various textual particles indicate 'illah with differing strength, and the Hanafi school employs them in order of priority.

Key Terms
الإجماع الظني (al-ijma' al-zanni): Consensus that is speculative, such as silent consensus or that reported by ahad narrations; it is binding but can be opposed by ijtihad if a preventer is claimed.
النص الصريح (al-nass al-sarih): Explicit textual statement that indicates the 'illah directly through linguistic convention (e.g., particles like 'li-ajl', 'kay', 'idhan', etc.).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is considered the strongest textual indicator of 'illah according to the Hanafi hierarchy?
A) The particle 'lam' (for the purpose of).
B) The particle 'fa' (then/so).
C) The phrase 'li-ajl' (for the sake of).
D) The particle 'ba' (by means of).

Key Takeaway
The first two masalik for establishing the 'illah are consensus on the cause (ijma') and explicit textual particles (nass), with a defined hierarchy of strength from li-ajl (strongest) to fa' (weakest).

Al-Ijma — Consensus

Refuting Objections to the Ijma of the Sahaba on Qiyas
Refuting objections that Sahaba qiyas does not prove ijma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi responses to objections against the use of Sahaba practice as proof for qiyas, and apply the method of reconciling conflicting reports with mutawatir practice.

Core Idea
The Sahaba's mutawatir qiyas practice overrides isolated criticisms; their silence indicates consent, not fear.

The Hanafi school grounds the authority of qiyas partly in the practice and silent consensus of the Companions. Yet opponents raise sophisticated challenges: that silence may be fear, that consent might have been retracted, that Sahaba were uniquely qualified, and that leading Sahaba themselves condemned opinion (ra'y). This passage systematically refutes each objection, showing that the mutawatir character of their practice, their fearlessness, and the availability of reconciliatory interpretations prove the validity of qiyas as a universal proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four objections
The passage lists four key objections against using Sahaba practice to prove qiyas: (1) silence due to fear of the ruling caliphs, (2) possibility of retraction before final consensus, (3) uniqueness of Sahaba qualification, and (4) explicit condemnations of ra'y by leading Sahaba (Abu Bakr, Umar, Ali, Uthman, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Umar).
Each objection targets a different angle: epistemological (fear), procedural (retraction), personal (uniqueness), and textual (condemnation).
2
Extract the Hanafi rebuttal for each objection
For each objection, note the specific rebuttal: (1) Sahaba were fearless in religious matters and often opposed caliphs, (2) circumstantial evidence shows no retraction occurred, (3) qiyas is a universal proof, not a personal prerogative, (4) the condemnations are interpreted contextually to refer only to unfounded ra'y, not valid qiyas.
The rebuttals rely on historical facts, mutawatir reports, and linguistic precision.
3
Analyze the method of reconciling conflicting texts
The opponent cites explicit statements from respected Sahaba. The Hanafi response does not reject these texts but interprets them narrowly: Abu Bakr's statement was about tafsir, Umar's was against those clinging to opinion over sunna, Ali/Uthman's denied religion originating from opinion, Ibn Mas'ud's was for non-mujtahids, and Ibn Umar's distinguished between sunna and proof. This reconciliation preserves both the authority of the Sahaba and the validity of qiyas.
This shows the Hanafi commitment to harmonizing all evidence rather than picking and choosing.
4
Evaluate the overall argument for ijma through practice
The author argues that the mutawatir practice of the Sahaba, combined with their silence in countless incidents, produces certain knowledge ('ilm 'adi) of their consent. The counterarguments are dismissed as based on improbable assumptions (fear, retraction) or misreadings of texts. The strength of the Hanafi position lies in its ability to account for all evidence consistently.
The final stage invites reflection on the epistemological weight of practice over isolated reports.

لهم في واقعة واحدة. ومنها: سلمنا أن الكل راضون به لكن يجوز أن لا يكون رضا البعض قبل رجوع الآخر، فلا يثبت الإجماع، وهذا لأن الكل لم يجتمعوا في محفل واحد ولم يتكلموا معاً. وقد مر جوابه في الأصل الثالث حين خرجوا له فتأمل ومنها : أنا سلمنا أنه وقع اتفاق الصحابة على العمل على مقتضى أقيستهم، لكن لا يلزم . منه عملنا على موجب أقيستنا بل يجوز أن يكونوا مختصين بهذا لكونهم من أفضل الأمة، وكون أذهانهم ثاقبة من أذهاننا وعقولهم متوقدة بنور إلهي، فإصابة الحق برأيهم أكثر وأقوى من إصابتنا فجواز تعبدهم بالقياس لا يوجب جواز تعبدنا به والجواب أنه لا شك في فضل آرائهم على آرائنا وفي كون إصابتهم للحق أكثر، لكن تعبدهم بالقياس لم يكن لاختصاصهم به، بل تلك الوقائع دلت على أن تعبدهم به لكونه حجة لا غير، ونحن وهم رضوان الله عليهم سيان في اتباع الحجج على أن من بعدهم من التابعين أيضاً قاسوا من نكير، فلا وجه الله تعالى عنه حين سئل عن أتي سماء تظلني وأتي أرض تقلني لو قلت في كتاب الله برأيي وهذا ليس من الباب في شيء، فإنه إنما نفى القول بالرأي في تفسير كتاب الله تعالى لا أنه أنكر الرأي والقياس مطلقاً (وعن) أمير المؤمنين (عمر) رضي الله عنه (إياكم وأصحاب الرأي فإنهم أعداء السنن) وأنت لا يذهب عليك أنه لا ذم فيه إلا لأصحاب الرأي والمتبادر منه من هو ملازم للرأي، ولا يلتفت إلى غيره كأصحاب النار، وهذا لا ينفي قياس صاحب السنن» (وعن أميري المؤمنين (علي وعثمان : لو كان الدين بالرأي لكان باطن الخف أولى المسح من ظاهره وأنت لا يذهب عليك أنه إنما ينفي كون الدين ناشئاً عن الرأي، وهو كذلك، لأن وضع حكم ديني ابتداء لا بالرأي أصلاً، ولا يلزم منه نفي استعمال الرأي في مماثل لما ثبت من الدين ليعرف الحكم به (وعن ابن مسعود : إذا قلتم في دينكم بالقياس أحللتم كثيراً مما حرمه الله وحرمتم كثيراً مما أحل (الله) وهذا إنما يتم لو كان الخطاب للكل، وهو خفي بعد بل لعله لقوم ما وصلوا إلى درجة الاجتهاد بالقياس (وعن ابن عمر السنة ما سنه الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم وآله وأصحابه (وسلم، لا تجعلوا الرأي سنة للمسلمين) وهذا لو تم فإنما يدل على أن الرأي ليس سنة، لا أنه ليس حجة (الجواب أنه أي المنقول محمول على تصحيحه فيما لا يصح كالملغي الاعتبار والمصالح المرسلة وتقديمه على ما يقدح فيه من الكتاب والسنة (توفيقاً) بين هذه الروايات وبين ما تواتر عنهم من العمل بالرأي واستدل بما تواتر معناه وإن كانت التفاصيل آحاداً (من ذكره عليه) يصح الكلالة
And among them: We accept that the practice of qiyas is established from some of the Companions, but consensus does not necessarily follow. It would only follow if their silence indicated consent; but it is possible it was due to fear. Al-Nazzam said that only a few of the Companions practiced it, but since they included the Commander of the Faithful Umar, Uthman, and Ali, who were rulers, others feared opposing them, because the custom is to oppose whoever adopts a personal opinion. So there is no consensus at all. The answer: Repeated silence in countless incidents is only customary with consent, especially in matters of the foundation of religion. This silence secretly and publicly by everyone in every incident, and their adherence to the rulings of the rightly guided caliphs arising from analogies, produces customary necessary knowledge of consent and agreement. The assumption of attributing fear to them is a false accusation, for it is part of their noble and mutawatir character that they did not fear anyone in religious matters, especially over a long period. Denying this is perhaps stubbornness. Attributing enmity to the rightly guided caliphs for opposing what they had taken as a school is immense stupidity, for they were more responsive to the truth. Whoever examines the histories and biographies knows with certainty that they often opposed the statements of the caliphs. If they had no fear in opposition in many incidents, what fear would they have in one incident? And among them: We accept that all are satisfied with it, but it is possible that some were satisfied only before others retracted, so consensus is not established. This is because they did not all gather in one assembly and speak together. Its answer has already been given in the third principle: even if they did not speak at once, knowledge was gained through circumstantial evidence that they did not retract. Moreover, if that were not the case, consensus would be invalidated entirely. What is in al-Mahsul—that the Companions were countable in the early period, so they could gather in one assembly and speak together—it is said that if it were complete, it would indicate the invalidity of the occurrence of consensus that is definitively established, because it was not possible for the gatherers in consensus incidents to speak together. However, here also agreement occurred on fighting the refusers of zakat by qiyas suddenly when they went out for it, so reflect. And among them: We accept that agreement occurred among the Companions to act according to their analogies, but it does not necessitate our acting according to our analogies. Rather, it is possible they were singled out for this because they are the best of the Ummah, their minds are more penetrating than ours, and their intellects are enkindled with divine light, so their hitting the truth by their opinion is more and stronger than our hitting it. Thus, the permissibility of their being bound by qiyas does not necessitate our being bound by it. The answer: There is no doubt that their opinions are superior to ours and that they hit the truth more often, but their being bound by qiyas was not due to their being singled out; rather, those incidents indicated that they were bound by it because it is a proof, nothing else. We and they—may Allah be pleased with them—are equal in following proofs. Moreover, those after them from the Tabi'un also analogized without rejection, so there is no basis for singling them out at all. So understand and be firm. And it is objected that the leading Companions condemned it, and the condemned one is not a proof. And it is possible to formulate a refutation of the argument by consensus as well. From the best of mankind after the prophets, the leader of the siddiqin after them, Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, may Allah be exalted pleased with him, when he was asked about 'which sky covers me and which earth carries me if I say in the Book of Allah by my opinion'—this is not from the topic at all, for he only negated saying by opinion in interpreting the Book of Allah, not that he denied opinion and qiyas absolutely. And from the Commander of the Faithful Umar, may Allah be pleased with him: 'Beware of the people of opinion, for they are enemies of the sunan.' It does not escape you that there is no condemnation except of the people of opinion, and the primary meaning is the one who clings to opinion, not others like 'the people of fire'. This does not negate the qiyas of the possessor of the sunna. And from the two commanders of the faithful, Ali and Uthman: 'If religion were by opinion, the inside of the sock would be more deserving of wiping than the outside.' It does not escape you that this only negates that religion arises from opinion, which is true, because establishing a religious ruling from scratch is not by opinion at all, and it does not necessitate negating the use of opinion in what is similar to what is established from religion to know the ruling. And from Ibn Mas'ud: 'If you speak in your religion by qiyas, you will make lawful much of what Allah has forbidden and forbid much of what Allah has permitted.' This is only valid if the address is to everyone, and that is unclear; rather, perhaps it is for a people who have not reached the level of ijtihad by qiyas. And from Ibn Umar: 'The sunna is what the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him and his family and Companions, established; do not make opinion a sunna for the Muslims.' If this is valid, it only indicates that opinion is not a sunna, not that it is not a proof. The answer: That which is transmitted is carried upon its validation in matters that are not valid, such as considerations that are nullified and unrestricted interests, and giving precedence to what contradicts it from the Book and Sunna—in reconciliation between these reports and what is mutawatir from them of practice by opinion. And he argued by what is mutawatir in meaning, even if the details are isolated (from his mentioning of it). It is valid as inheritance of the kalala.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of objections to qiyas. It addresses several sophisticated challenges. First, the objection that the silence of the Companions might be due to fear of the ruling caliphs (Umar, Uthman, Ali) is refuted by pointing out that their mutawatir character was fearless in religion, and history shows they often opposed the caliphs. Second, the objection that consent might have occurred only before retraction is answered by citing the circumstantial evidence that no retraction is known, and if such a condition were accepted, it would invalidate all consensus. Third, the objection that the Sahaba were uniquely qualified for qiyas is refuted by noting that their use was based on qiyas being a proof, not their personal merit, and later generations also used it. Fourth, the objection that leading Sahaba (Abu Bakr, Umar, Ali, Uthman, Ibn Mas'ud, Ibn Umar) condemned qiyas is addressed by interpreting their statements contextually: Abu Bakr's statement was about interpreting the Quran by personal opinion, not about qiyas; Umar's warning against 'people of opinion' refers to those who cling to opinion contrary to sunna, not to valid qiyas; Ali and Uthman's statement negates that religion originates from opinion, not its use in analogical extension; Ibn Mas'ud's warning is for those not qualified for ijtihad; Ibn Umar's statement only says opinion is not sunna, not that it is not a proof. The overall approach is to reconcile these reports with the established mutawatir practice of the Sahaba using qiyas.

Key Terms
إجماع (ijma): Consensus of the Sahaba on the validity of qiyas, established through their consistent practice and silence.
أصحاب الرأي (ashab al-ra'y): Those who cling to personal opinion (ra'y) contrary to the sunna, condemned by Umar. This does not include valid qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the objection that the Sahaba's silence might have been out of fear of the caliphs?
A) He argues that the caliphs never punished those who opposed them.
B) He states that the Sahaba were fearless in religious matters, and history shows they often opposed the caliphs.
C) He says the caliphs themselves used qiyas, so there was no fear.
D) He claims the Sahaba had no reason to fear because the caliphs were just.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defends qiyas as a universal proof by using the mutawatir practice of the Sahaba to override isolated objections and contextualizing apparent condemnations.

Al-Ijma — Consensus

Disputed Sources of Law: Hanafi Critical Analysis
Enumeration of secondary sources debated among the four imams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the seven disputed sources mentioned in the text and state the Hanafi position on its validity as a proof.

Core Idea
Hanafis accept only Qur'an, Sunnah, Ijma', and Qiyas; other sources are either not proofs or reduce to qiyas.

While the four imams agree on the primacy of the Qur'an, Sunnah, Ijma', and Qiyas, they differed on several other proposed sources. This passage from 'Fawatih al-Rahamut' enumerates seven such disputed sources and provides a Hanafi critique of each. Understanding these critiques is essential for applying Hanafi usul correctly and avoiding reliance on sources the school does not recognize as independent proofs.

Guided Path
1
List the four agreed-upon sources
Begin by identifying the sources that all four imams accept: the Book (Qur'an), Sunnah, Ijma' (consensus), and Qiyas (analogy). These are the foundational pillars of Hanafi usul.
These four are mentioned at the start of the passage as the 'usul al-arba'a'.
2
Identify each disputed source
The passage then lists seven sources that are disputed: 1) Laws of previous nations (shara'i' man qablana), 2) Istihsan (juristic preference), 3) Masalih mursala (unrestricted public interest), 4) Qawl al-Sahabi (Companion's opinion), 5) Lack of evidence after search (adam al-dalil ba'd al-fahsh), 6) Taking the minimum said (al-akhdhu bi-aqalla ma qila), and 7) Istiqra' (induction).
The passage uses 'منها' to introduce each.
3
Analyze the Hanafi critique for each
For each disputed source, the text either states it is not a proof (e.g., istihsan is not a controversial proof), attributes it to another school (e.g., masalih mursala to Malik), or offers a specific logical refutation (e.g., adam al-dalil ba'd al-fahsh is not a proof because absence of evidence does not prove non-existence). Pay attention to the reasoning: many critiques argue that the claimed source reduces to qiyas or is merely a preponderance (tarjih) for action, not a proof.
The text often ends with the phrase 'والحق أنه' (the truth is that...) to signal the Hanafi position.
4
Distinguish between proof (dalil) and preponderance (tarjih)
The passage makes a key distinction: some methods like al-akhdhu bi-aqalla ma qila are not proofs (istidlal) but only preponderance for action (tarjih li al-'amal). Similarly, adam al-dalil is not a proof but merely a procedural conclusion. This distinction is crucial for understanding the Hanafi hierarchy of evidence.
This step helps avoid the common mistake of treating tarjih methods as independent sources of law.
5
Summarize the Hanafi position
Conclude that for Hanafis, the only primary sources are the Qur'an, Sunnah, Ijma', and Qiyas. Other proposed sources either lack textual basis, are reducible to these four, or are merely tools for preference when evidence conflicts.
This summary reinforces the core idea.

Completion: The four imams who exerted their efforts to establish the foundations of religion and the basis of Sharia: Abu Hanifa Nu'man ibn Thabit al-Kufi, Malik ibn Anas al-Madani, Muhammad ibn Idris al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Hanbal (may Allah be pleased with them and those who follow them in goodness) agreed upon the four principles: the Book, the Sunnah, Ijma', and Qiyas. They differed on other matters and their authority. Among them: (Laws of previous nations) some of the followers of these imams denied it. (Istihsan) it was already mentioned that it is not a controversial proof. (Masalih mursala) its authority is attributed to Imam Malik. (Qawl al-Sahabi) some Hanafis, Malikis, Hanbalis, and al-Shafi'i in his old opinion went to its authority; it has been presented with its for and against. (Among them: Lack of evidence after search) indicating non-existence, and some Shafi'is chose it, but the truth according to the majority is that it is not a proof, for the absence of evidence does not necessitate the absence of the indicated; except by Sharia, for Sharia rules indicate that what has no specific evidence is permissible, as previously indicated. (Among them: Taking the minimum said) al-Shafi'i took it, e.g., the blood money of a Jew: some said one-third, some half, some the whole, so he took the third. This is corrupt, for why negate the increase? And some claimed it is consensus, but it has been presented; the truth is that it is a preference for action because the least is certain, not a proof. Like taking the principle when things conflict, it works with what aligns with the principle as a preponderance, as we said about the donkey's saliva. (Among them: Istiqra') al-Baydawi from the Shafi'is chose it, but the truth is that it does not indicate Allah's ruling because the Sharia does not provide every ruling of every particular in detail so that one can infer the general ruling from particulars; if it is said that it came as a general, then there is no istiqra', rather the general is the proof, unless it indicates a comprehensive attribute, then the ruling is by that attribute, and istiqra' is only to verify it in the particulars, thus it reduces to qiyas. Consider.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage lists several sources of Islamic law that are disputed among the major schools, particularly between Hanafis and Shafi'is. The four imams unanimously accept the Qur'an, Sunnah, Ijma', and Qiyas as primary sources. The text then mentions: (1) Laws of previous nations (shara'i' man qablana) – some deny their authority. (2) Istihsan – previously stated it is not a controversial proof. (3) Masalih mursala (unrestricted public interest) – attributed to Malik. (4) Qawl al-Sahabi (the opinion of a Companion) – accepted by some Hanafis, Malikis, Hanbalis, and al-Shafi'i in his old position. (5) Lack of evidence after searching (adam al-dalil ba'd al-fahsh) – most scholars say it is not a proof, because absence of evidence does not prove non-existence; only Sharia can indicate permissibility. (6) Taking the minimum said (al-akhdhu bi-aqalla ma qila) – al-Shafi'i used this, e.g., for the blood money of a Jew, but the text rejects it, arguing that the minimum is a preponderance for action, not a proof. (7) Istiqra' (induction) – chosen by al-Baydawi but the text argues it ultimately reduces to qiyas or general evidence. The passage concludes with 'tadabbar' (consider). Each point is briefly criticized from a Hanafi perspective.

Key Terms
المصالح المرسلة (al-masalih al-mursala): Unrestricted public interests not explicitly supported by a specific textual evidence.
الأخذ بأقل ما قيل (al-akhdhu bi-aqalla ma qila): Taking the minimum opinion among different views as the ruling.
الاستقراء (al-istiqra'): Induction – inferring a general ruling from multiple particular instances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in this passage, what is the correct status of 'taking the minimum said' (al-akhdhu bi-aqalla ma qila)?
A) It is a valid proof (hujja) like consensus.
B) It is merely a preponderance for action, not a proof.
C) It is the best method for determining blood money.
D) It is rejected entirely as bid'ah.

Key Takeaway
Hanafis accept only the Qur'an, Sunnah, Ijma', and Qiyas as primary proofs; all other proposed sources are either not proofs or are reducible to these.

Al-Ijma — Consensus

The Definitiveness of General Texts: Hanafi Refutation of al-Jahiz
Hanafi defense of culpability for mistaken mujtahids

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi argument that general Qur'anic verses are definitive in generality ('amm qat'i) and apply it to refute the claim that mistaken mujtahids are exempt from divine threats.

Core Idea
General texts are definitive until proven specific; mistaken mujtahids are included in Qur'anic warnings.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut presents the Hanafi rebuttal of al-Jahiz's view that a mujtahid who errs in fundamentals is not sinful. The Hanafis invoke the consensus of the Salaf and the definitive generality of multiple Qur'anic verses. In this lesson, you will examine how the Hanafi school uses the concept of 'amm qat'i to argue that all disbelievers, including those who err through ijtihad, are addressed by the divine threats. You will also analyse the refutation of al-Jahiz's first two arguments concerning the nature of taklif.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The core dispute is whether a mujtahid who arrives at false belief in fundamentals (e.g., denying Tawhid) is sinful and subject to punishment in the Hereafter. Al-Jahiz denies this; the Hanafis affirm it.
The issue is about culpability for mistaken ijtihad in creedal matters.
2
Locate the Hanafi proofs
The text first cites ijma' (consensus) of the Salaf that all disbelievers are in the Fire, regardless of ijtihad. Then it cites several Qur'anic verses threatening disbelievers with punishment, and argues these verses are general ('amm) and definitive (qat'i) in their generality.
Hanafis consider 'amm as qat'i al-dalala until specification is proven.
3
Examine the refutation of specification
The author responds to the objection that the verses might be specified to exclude mujtahids. The rebuttal: the wording is definite in generality; even if not, the sheer multiplicity of verses cuts off any possibility of specification.
This demonstrates the Hanafi commitment to the definitiveness of general texts when reinforced by tawatur ma'nawi.
4
Analyse al-Jahiz's first argument and Hanafi response
Al-Jahiz argues that taklif (obligation) is directed to the act of ijtihad, not directly to belief (since belief is not directly within one's power). The Hanafi response: the obligation is to correct consideration (nazar sahih) of the definitive proofs. Failure to reach the truth shows deficiency (taqsir) in consideration, not that the obligation was improperly placed.
Hanafis hold that the proofs for God's existence and prophethood are so clear that failure indicates a fault in the mujtahid's reasoning.
5
Examine the second argument and its refutation
Al-Jahiz argues that obliging someone to believe the opposite of what his ijtihad has concluded is impossible (taklif ma la yutaq). The Hanafi response: the impossibility is only due to the mujtahid using corrupted premises (muqaddimat ghayr sahiha). If he had used correct premises, he would have attained certainty. Thus the obligation itself is possible; the impossibility is self-inflicted.
The key distinction: impossibility is not inherent in the command but arises from the subject's own deficient reasoning.

Conclusion on Ijtihad and Taqlid. The entry of disbelievers into Paradise is not conceivable from him, and this contradicts the transmission of most reliable narrators, although the most fitting for us is: Firstly: the consensus of the predecessors on this disagreement among the Companions, Followers, and those who followed them all, that they are people of the Fire absolutely, whether they exerted ijtihad or not. And this is more appropriate than what is in some books that they agreed to kill them without distinction between whether they disbelieved through ijtihad or obstinacy. For this does not invalidate their statement, because they say that the rulings of disbelief apply to them in this abode. And for us, secondly, like His statement: 'So woe to those who disbelieve from the Fire' (Sad:38) and His statement: 'And for them is a great punishment' (al-Baqarah:7, Al Imran:176, al-Nur:23, al-Jathiyah:10) and His statement: 'And he in the Hereafter will be among the losers' (Al Imran:85). If it is said: perhaps the verses are specified for other than the mujtahids among them, he said: and specification for other than the mujtahid, rather its possibility, is repelled by the wording, for it is definite in generality as before. Likewise in the commentary of al-Tahrir and al-Hashiyah, and this is insufficient because the general is definite with the broader sense of definiteness, which cuts off possibility arising from evidence. And what concerns us here is definiteness in the more specific sense, which cuts off possibility absolutely, because this issue is of this kind. So more appropriate is to say: these verses are conveyed with these wordings and with other wordings that convey their meaning, and they have multiplied beyond count, and likewise the hadiths supporting them. And such cuts off from possibility other than the understood meaning in the discourses, as is not hidden. Or it is repelled by consensus on generality, so understand. And al-Jahiz and his followers said: Firstly, there is no obligation in examples of His statement: 'Believe in the Self' except by ijtihad, for faith not by faith itself because belief, how can its obligation be valid? Because it is not in the capacity of the servant. And the mujtahid has done what we said: We do not concede that he is obligated by absolute ijtihad, because he is obligated to correct consideration in the prescribed definite matters. So when his consideration does not lead to the desired, it is known that he is deficient in it and did not consider what he should consider. And the secret is that the verses indicating the existence of the perfect Maker free from deficiencies are clear, such that there is no way to doubt or dispute, and likewise the miracles indicating prophethood. So if he does not reach through his consideration the desired, it is known that he is deficient in consideration and blind to the verses indicating oneness and prophethood due to his deficiency definitely, so it is established. And they said: Secondly, obligation of the opposite of ijtihad, i.e., to affirm it, is an obligation of what is not possible, because affirming the opposite of what he thought or definitely knew with compound ignorance is impossible, and what leads to it is necessary and compelled. So the obligation to affirm the opposite of what he ijtihad is impossible, so he does not sin. We said: That impossibility is due to describing his consideration in incorrect premises and believing its result, and it does not necessitate impossibility in his time, even if it were customary. For if this consideration is absent and replaced by consideration in correct premises, he would obtain definite knowledge contrary to what was obtained by corrupt ijtihad, such that it would not be possible, so he is not obliged by it. This. And they said thirdly: Allah does not oblige except what is easier. Do you not see how He considered the difficulty of removing the foot from the khuff and legislated wiping? And He considered fear of illness and legislated tayammum, and considered the hardship of travel and legislated breaking the fast, and so on. And He saw the need of the poor and legislated salam. And He whose mercy is of this level, and He considered these harms as an excuse, an excuse that is heard, that his consideration does not reach what is real and places in eternal punishment whom He did not place in the punishment of cooling the foot when taking the foot out of the khuff. We said: Obligation to know himself also...
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute presenting the Hanafi rebuttal of al-Jahiz's view that mistaken mujtahids commit no sin. The Hanafis advance two main arguments: First, the consensus (ijma') of the Salaf—Companions, Followers, and their successors—that those who disbelieve, whether by ijtihad or obstinacy, are in the Fire. This consensus is explicit and general. Second, numerous Qur'anic verses threaten disbelievers with punishment without restricting to non-mujtahids (e.g., Surah Sad:38, al-Baqarah:7, Al Imran:176, etc.). Attempts to specify these verses to exclude mujtahids are rejected because the wording is definite in generality (ʿāmm qatʿī). The author notes that the broader sense of definiteness (cutting off possibility arising from evidence) may not suffice here; rather, the verses are so numerous and unequivocal that they cut off all possibility of exception. Then the passage presents and refutes three arguments of al-Jahiz: (1) The obligation to believe is by ijtihad, not directly—refuted by saying the obligation is to correct consideration in definitive matters; failure proves deficiency. (2) Obligation of the opposite is impossible—refuted by noting that impossibility arises only due to corrupted premises; proper consideration would yield certainty. (3) God only obliges what is easy—refuted by analogy: just as He considered hardship in removing the khuff, He would not punish without reason; but He made knowledge of self obligatory as well. The text cuts off during the third response.

Key Terms
إجماع (ijma'): Consensus of the Salaf that disbelievers are in Hell.
تكليف (taklif): Legal obligation; here discussed in relation to belief and ijtihad.
عام قطعي ('amm qat'i): A general text that is definite in its generality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rebuttal, why do the Qur'anic verses against disbelievers apply to mistaken mujtahids?
A) Because the verses are specific to mujtahids.
B) Because the verses are general and their wording is definitive in generality.
C) Because the verses were revealed after the Companions' consensus.
D) Because the verses mention ijtihad explicitly.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that general Qur'anic texts are definitive in their inclusion unless specified, and thus mistaken mujtahids who deny fundamentals are subject to punishment.

Absence of Sin for the Mistaken Mujtahid in Speculative Matters
Proving the absence of sin for the mistaken mujtahid in zanni matters via ijma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that a mujtahid who errs in zanni matters after full effort is not sinful, and identify the proof from the consensus (ijma) of the Sahaba.

Core Idea
No sin on the mujtahid who errs in speculative matters — the Sahaba differed without mutual accusation.

This lesson examines a foundational Hanafi usul rule: the mistaken mujtahid in speculative (zanni) matters is not sinful. The text clarifies the scope of zanni versus qat'i (conclusive) matters and refutes the opposing Mu'tazila view. You will learn to distinguish the two categories and trace the proof from the consensus of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Define 'zanni' as per the text
Read the passage's definition: zanni matters have a possibility of disagreement, even if remote, and are not supported by definitive evidence. Distinguish this from qat'i (conclusive) matters.
Zanni in the 'general sense' includes matters that may be considered conclusive but have a remote possible alternative.
2
State the ruling on the mistaken mujtahid
The passage explicitly states: 'فلا إثم على المخطىء فيها إذا اجتهد كل الجهد' — no sin on the one who errs if he exerted full effort. Understand that this applies only when desire does not lead him.
If desire (hawa) influences the ijtihad, sin is incurred even in zanni matters.
3
Examine the Mu'tazila objection and the Hanafi response
The Mu'tazila (Bishr and Abu Bakr al-Asamm) claimed every ruling has a definitive (qat'i) proof. The Hanafi response: if they mean qat'i in the general sense (including zanni), it does not negate the absence of sin; if they mean qat'i in the specific sense (no valid alternative), it is false because many rulings lack such proof.
The rebuttal is based on the reality of fiqh: not all rulings have a single conclusive evidence.
4
Identify the primary proof: Ijma of the Sahaba
The passage states: 'إذ تواتر اختلافهم بلا تأثيم' — the recurrent disagreements among the Companions without any of them accusing another of sin. This consensus (ijma) proves that error in ijtihad is not sinful.
The proof is qat'i in transmission (tawatur) and establishes the rule definitively.
5
Apply the ruling to the example of Ibn 'Abbas
The text cites Ibn 'Abbas's criticism of Zayd ibn Thabit over inheritance rules. Had Ibn 'Abbas sinned by erring, he would have been sinful, but the lack of mutual accusation shows he was merely mistaken, not sinful.
This demonstrates the practical application of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يسمع عذر من جملة رحمته حتى لا يبقى الإنسان مثل الوحوش، ومن جملة رحمته أن جعل آيات وحدانيته ورسالة رسوله واضحة لا يتطرق فيها شبهة من الشبهات فإذاً لا يمكن أن ينظر الرجل في الآيات من غير مكابرة الهوى على العقل، فلا يؤدي النظر إلى المطلوب الواقعي، وعدم الوصول إليه بقصور منه، فلا العذر في يسمع هذا الواضح، وكيف يسمع وإنه إنما نشأ حماقته استعمال العقل الموهوب له وميله لمكابرة العقل على الهوى، ولا وعدم الحماقة بحال فافهم واستقم كما أمرت فلا عذر لأحد في الجهل بالخالق أصلاً (وأما الظنيات المعلومة ظناً بالمعنى الأعم الذي فيه احتمال الخلاف ولو بعيداً غير ناشيء عن دليل (فلا إثم على المخطىء فيها إذا اجتهد كل الجهد فأدى رأيه إلى الاحتمال البعيد، فأول النص إلى ذلك الاحتمال فإن قلت : قد شمل الظني بتفسيرك القاطع الذي فيه احتمال غير ناشيء عن دليل وخلافه باطل قطعاً حتى ينقض القضاء به، فكيف يصح عدم الإثم؟ قلت : هذا القاطع يحتمل الخلاف في كونه قطعاً، ولما أدى اجتهاده إلى الاحتمال البعيد صار قريباً في ظنه فلم يبق قاطعاً فلا يأثم إلا إذا دعاه الهوى إلى ذلك الاحتمال، وحينئذ نحن نقول بالإثم قطعاً، وأما نقض القضاء فلكونه مقطوعاً عند قاض ناقض مبتلی به فصار عنده باطلاً بيقين فينقض هذا غاية الكلام فافهم ) ولا يعبأ بتأثيم بشر ، و أبي بكر (الأصم) المعتزلين وهما غير إمامي الهدى من الأولياء الكرام بشر الحافي والشيخ أبي بكر الأصم قدس سرهما. فإنهما قالا لا عن دليل، بل زعما منهما أن كل حكم عليه دليل قطعي البتة إن أراد القطعي بالمعنى الأعم، فلا ينفع، فإنه لا يوجب الإثم وإن أراد القطعي بالمعنى الأخص فالدعوى خلاف الضرورة مكابرة قيل : وعليه الظاهرية (والإمامية أما عند الإمامية فظاهر فإنه لا يخلو على ظنهم الكاسد كل عصر عن إمام معصوم قوله ،قطعي كقول الرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم، وقد أبطلنا رأيهم في أصل الإجماع فتذكر وإنما قلنا بعدم التأثيم لدلالة إجماع الصحابة على نفيه) أي نفي التأثيم (إذ تواتر اختلافهم بلا تأثيم) لا حد من المخالفين (وقول ابن عباس : ألا يتقي الله زيد بن ثابت، يجعل ابن الابن ابناً ولا يجعل أبا الأب أباً غير مستلزم للتأثيم فيها كما في التحرير لجواز ادعائه القطعية الثابتة بالدلالة (أو) ادعائه (المبالغة التخطئة) هذا هو في الظاهر هذا كله توضيح هذه العبارة عن ابن عباس، والإجماع على هذا غني عن تجشم الإبانة وقد بلغ حد التواتر حتى صار من ضروريات الدين، واستدل الشيخ عبد الحق في مدارج النبوّة بما مر من حديث صلاة العصر في بني قريظة إلا أنه خبر واحد لا يفيد في المسائل اليقينية إلا أن يدعي الشهرة الموجبة للطمأنينة
An excuse is heard from among His mercy so that man does not remain like wild beasts; and from His mercy is that He made the signs of His oneness and the message of His Messenger clear, not subject to any doubt. So a man cannot look at the signs without forcing his desire over intellect, so consideration does not lead to the real objective, and failure to reach it is due to his shortcoming. So there is no excuse in hearing this clear [evidence] — and how could he hear it when his foolishness arises from using the intellect given to him and his inclination to force intellect over desire? There is no foolishness at all, so understand and be upright as you were commanded. So there is absolutely no excuse for anyone in ignorance of the Creator. (As for speculative matters known by opinion in the general sense, which admits possibility of disagreement even if remote and not arising from evidence, then there is no sin on the mistaken person in them if he exerted his full effort and his opinion led him to the remote possibility, and he turned the text to that possibility. If you say: Your definition has included the conclusive (qat'i) that has possibility not arising from evidence and its opposite is conclusively invalid such that a judgment is overturned, so how is absence of sin correct? I say: This conclusive matter is open to disagreement concerning its conclusiveness, and when his ijtihad led him to the remote possibility, it became close in his opinion and ceased to be conclusive, so he does not sin unless his desire called him to that possibility. In that case we say he sins conclusively. As for overturning the judgment, it is because it is conclusive in the view of the judge who overturns, faced with it, so he considers it definitely invalid, so he overturns it; this is the end of the discussion, understand.) And no attention is paid to the sin-imputing of Bishr and Abu Bakr al-Asamm, the Mu'tazila — they are not imams of guidance among the noble saints, Bishr al-Hafi and Shaykh Abu Bakr al-Asamm, may God sanctify their secrets. For they said it is not based on evidence, rather they claimed that every ruling has a definitive proof absolutely. If they mean qat'i in the general sense, then it is of no use, for it does not necessitate sin. If they mean qat'i in the specific sense, then the claim is contrary to necessity, stubbornness. It was said: And upon it is the Zahiriyya and the Imamiyya — as for the Imamiyya, it is obvious, for according to their corrupt opinion, no age is devoid of an infallible imam whose statement is qat'i like the statement of the Messenger. And we have invalidated their view on the principle of consensus, so remember. And we only said there is no sin-imputation due to the indication of the consensus of the Companions on its negation — that is negation of sin-imputing — because their disagreement has been transmitted recurrently without sin-imputation from any of the opponents. And the statement of Ibn 'Abbas: 'Does Zayd ibn Thabit not fear God? He makes the son's son a son but does not make the father's father a father' is not entailing sin-imputation therein, as in al-Tahrir, because he could claim its conclusiveness established by evidence, or claim exaggeration in declaring error. This is apparent. All this is clarification of this statement of Ibn 'Abbas. And consensus on this is free from the trouble of explanation, and it has reached the level of tawatur until it became from the necessities of religion. Shaykh 'Abd al-Haqq in Madarij al-Nubuwwa used as evidence the hadith of the 'Asr prayer of Banu Qurayza, but it is a single transmission (khabar wahid) that is not useful in certain matters unless one claims the renown (shuhra) that gives reassurance.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a ruling (hukm) on the accountability of a mujtahid who errs in speculative (zanni) matters. The author first establishes that there is no excuse for ignorance of the Creator because His signs are clear. Then he shifts to the rule: In speculative matters where there is a possibility of disagreement (even remote), a mujtahid who exerts full effort and arrives at a conclusion is not sinful, even if he errs. The text clarifies that if a matter is 'conclusive' (qat'i) in the specific sense, then error is sinful, but a mujtahid may consider it non-conclusive due to his ijtihad. The author refutes the Mu'tazila (Bishr and Abu Bakr al-Asamm) who claimed every ruling has a definitive proof, and also rejects the Zahiri and Imami views. The proof lies in the consensus (ijma) of the Companions, as their disagreements were recurrent (tawatur) without any of them accusing the other of sin. The example of Ibn 'Abbas and Zayd ibn Thabit over inheritance rules ('awl) shows that Ibn 'Abbas did not sinfully err; rather he was considered mistaken but not sinful. The hadith of Banu Qurayza (about the 'Asr prayer) is cited but noted as khabar wahid, insufficient for certain matters unless elevated by shuhra. Thus, the Hanafi principle is affirmed: the mujtahid who errs in zanni matters is not sinful.

Key Terms
ظني (zanni): Speculative matter with possibility of disagreement, even if remote, and not arising from definitive evidence.
قطعي (qat'i): Matter that is conclusive, having no valid alternative interpretation; in the specific sense, it is such that its opposite is conclusively invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in this lesson, a mujtahid who errs in a speculative (zanni) matter is not sinful because:
A) Speculative matters are not part of Islamic law.
B) The Sahaba differed without accusing each other of sin, proving error is not sinful.
C) The Quran states that error is forgiven for all mujtahids.
D) The hadith of Banu Qurayza explicitly declares it.

Key Takeaway
The mujtahid who errs in speculative (zanni) matters after exerting full effort incurs no sin, as established by the consensus of the Companions.

Al-Ijma — Consensus
advanced
12 min
The Hanafi Position on Taqlid in Matters of Belief: Certainty vs. Blind Following
Explores the permissibility of taqlid in matters of belief and certainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on taqlid in 'aqliyat, identify the conclusive consensus (ijma' qati') as the primary proof, and evaluate the counterarguments from those who permit or obligate taqlid.

Core Idea
Taqlid cannot produce the certain knowledge required for belief in God; only direct engagement with evidence (nazar) yields certainty.

This passage from a Hanafi usul text tackles a fundamental question: can a person attain true knowledge of God by blindly following the statements of others? The majority of scholars, including the Hanafi school, say no, because belief in God demands unshakable certainty, and taqlid, by its nature, relies on fallible human testimony. Yet the discussion is nuanced: if taqlid happens to produce firm conviction, it may be accepted, but the ideal path is nazar—simple reflection that brings tranquility to the heart. In this lesson, we will unpack the Hanafi proof from conclusive consensus (ijma' qati'), examine the opposing views, and understand the practical implications for one's faith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central issue and the Hanafi position
The issue is whether taqlid is permissible in intellectual matters like the existence of God. The Hanafi/majority position: not permissible. Look for the phrase 'لا يجوز التقليد في العقليات'.
The passage also notes that this does not contradict the earlier consensus on validity of the muqallid's faith.
2
Analyze the Hanafi dalil: conclusive consensus (ijma' qati')
The passage states 'لنا: الاجماع القاطع على وجوب العلم بالله وصفاته'. Understand that this consensus obligates yaqin (certain knowledge), which taqlid cannot provide due to possible error of the informer.
The term 'qati'' indicates irrefutable transmission and meaning.
3
Examine the opposing views
Al-Anbari and some Shafi'is permit taqlid; a third group obligates it while prohibiting nazar. Identify their arguments: taqlid may yield conviction (jazzm), and nazar may lead to doubt.
The passage quotes their arguments and then responds.
4
Explore the Hanafi responses and refinements
The Hanafi response addresses the possibility of conviction after taqlid, the example of contradictory taqlid (eternity vs. creation), and the Companions' practice. Notice the concession that if taqlid produces certainty it may be valid, but nazar is superior.
Also note the story of Abd al-Qadir al-Jilani as evidence that necessary knowledge can occur without formal nazar.
5
Consider the special case of necessary knowledge and the definition of nazar
The passage redefines nazar as 'ما يفيد الطمأنينة' (what gives tranquility), not formal logic. The Bedouin's example illustrates simple reflection that leads to certainty. Understand how this applies to the Companions.
This resolves the objection that nazar was not visibly practiced by early Muslims.
